After I have completed for you, O my sons, this book, which is the last of the science of medicine with its completion, and I have reached the end of it, from its expositions and its explanations, it seemed to me that I should complete it for you with this treatise which is a part of the operation, that is, surgery with the hand.
Because manual operation is neglected in our region and in our time, completely abandoned: until perhaps its science perished, and its trace was cut off: and there remained of it only small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which hands have altered; and error and hesitation befell them, until its intentions were obscured, and its power was weakened: It seemed to me that I should revive it with the arrangement of this Treatise in it according to the way of position, explanation, and abbreviation: and that I should come with the forms of the instruments of the cautery, and of the other instruments of the work: since it is done by the addition of explanation, and by the hastening of that which is necessary.
The reason why a good craftsman of this art with his hand is not found in our time is: because the art of medicine is long, and its author must be exercised beforehand in the science of anatomy, which Galen narrated, until he knows the supports of the limbs, and their forms, and their connections and separations, and has knowledge of the bones, and nerves, and muscles, and their number, and their course, and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their exit.
And because of that Hippocrates said that name is much with name, and little with work, and especially in the art of the hand.
We indeed have already said about that in the introduction of this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said concerning anatomy, is not free from falling into error, by which men are killed: just as I have seen many of those who are formed in this science, and boast of it without knowledge, and without experience.
That is, because I saw a foolish physician incise a scrofulous abscess in a woman's neck, and he cut some arteries in the neck: wherefore the woman's blood flowed until she fell dead in his hands.
And I saw another physician who presumed to extract a stone from a man who was advanced in age, and the stone was large. Therefore he arrived at it, and extracted it with a piece of the bladder. Wherefore the man died on the third day.
And indeed I had already been called to extract it: and I saw from the size of the stone, and the condition of the sick man, that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a certain Duke of our land for medicine: and it happened to a black eunuch who was with him, a fracture in his leg near the heel: and the physician hastened with his ignorance, and bound the fracture over the wound with cushions and splints with a strong binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
Then he completed him according to his desires. Afterwards he dismissed him after a few days, and ordered him not to loosen the bandage: and it remained thus until his leg and his foot became suppurated, and came to destruction.
Therefore I was called to him: and I hastened to loosen the bandage: and calmness followed, and he escaped from his pains.
However, the corruption was already confirmed in the limb: and I could not restrain it.
Therefore the corruption ceased to spread in the limb, until it perished.
And I saw another physician, who perforated a cancerous abscess: and it ulcerated afterwards, until its malignancy was magnified with its master.
Which is, because when a cancer is pure from melancholic humor, then it is not necessary that it be approached with iron at all, unless it is in a member which can tolerate that the whole of it be cut away.
And for that reason, O my sons, it is necessary for you to know that manual operation is divided into two divisions: namely, operation to which safety is associated; and operation with which there is danger in most situations.
And I have already warned in every place of this book where an operation comes, in which there is terror and fear.
Therefore it is necessary for you to avoid it, and to leave it alone: so that fools may not find a way to speak, and to dishonor.
Therefore take measures with care and protection, and treat your patients with ease and firmness, and use the better way leading by God's grace to salvation and laudable success: and leave terrible illnesses of difficult healing: and lift your spirits from that which causes you fear, lest hesitation enter upon you in your faith and in your advantage.
For it is more conducive to your glory, and more sublime in this world, and finally to your rewards.
For Galen already said in some of his admonitions. Do not treat a bad illness: lest you be called bad physicians.
Now however I have divided this Treatise according to three Chapters.
Chapter one.
Concerning cautery with fire, and cautery with a sharp instrument, chaptered and ordered from head to foot, and the forms of instruments, and the tools of cautery: and everything which is necessary in the operation.
Chapter two.
Concerning incision and perforation, phlebotomy and cupping, and wounds: and the extraction of arrows, and similar things: and the whole chaptered, ordered with the forms of instruments.
Chapter three.
Concerning restoration, and dislocation, and the treatment of torsion, and similar things: chaptered, ordered from head to foot, and with the forms of instruments.
But before I recount the operation with it, it is necessary that I speak of the nature of its benefits and harms: and in which complexion it should be used.
Therefore I say that discussion of the nature of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a lengthy and subtle matter, a science and a hidden secret.
Many wise men have also spoken on this, and disagreed: and for me, a little from their discussions suffices, fearing prolixity.
Therefore I say that cautery universally benefits every complexion which is with matter and without matter, except for two complexions, which are a hot complexion without matter and a dry complexion without matter.
However, in a hot and dry complexion with matter they disagreed. For some have said that cautery is beneficial in it. And others said the contrary, namely that cautery is not suitable in illness which is from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat; and it is unsuitable that a hot and dry illness be healed with something hot and dry.
And he who says the contrary said: that through cautery help is given in every hot and dry illness which happens in human bodies, because when you compare a human body and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the human body cold.
And I indeed say, according to his account, because experience has already revealed that to me several times.
However, it ought not to be used except by one who is already proven and practiced in the chapter on cautery, in the final exercise, and has known the diversity of human complexions, and the disposition of illnesses in themselves, and their causes, and their accidents, and their duration.
From the other complexions, however, there is no fear for you; and especially in cold and moist illnesses. For now all physicians have agreed on them and have not disagreed on using cautery in them.
And know, my sons, that the secret of treatment by cautery with fire, and its advantage over cautery with burning medicine, is this: because fire is simple, whose action does not reach except the member that is cauterized; and it does not harm another member joined with it, except with small harm.
The action of burning medicines, however, reaches that which is distant from the members; and perhaps it causes an illness difficult to heal in a member, and perhaps it kills.
Fire, however, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that, unless it overflows.
Now indeed that has become known to us through experiment, because of the length of service and study with the art, and attention to the truth of things; and because of that I apologize for the length of the discussion.
And if it were not that it is not permitted in this my book, I would bring to you a hidden secret in fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies, in its expulsion, by which it expels illnesses with a philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be sharpened by your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time when it is fitting to perform cautery, and they place the best time in spring.
And I say that cautery is suitable at all times: because the harm that happens because of time is submerged in the benefit which is drawn with cautery. And especially if cautery is for pains from necessary, strong, urgent pains, which do not tolerate delay: because it is feared from them that a greater harm may happen than a small harm entering because of time.
And let not this fall into your minds, O sons, which only the common people and foolish physicians fear, namely that the illness which is healed by cautery does not always return: and they posit this consequence, and the matter is not as they think: because cautery is nothing other than a medicine in place of that which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the causes why pains happen.
Nevertheless, cautery surpasses medicine in the speed of its health and the strength of its operation and the vehemence of its dominion: and it is possible that the illness may return at some time according to the complexion of the sick person, and the location of his illness, and its strength: and what is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in it: and what comes to it in its acquisition from foods and similar causes, unless the illness in which cautery is administered happens.
An illness subtle in a limb of few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth because of pain and similar things, it is possible that that pain may not return in it. But this happens in few cases.
But truly the speech of the common people again, that cautery is the last of medicine, is right speech: not to that which they intend in it: because they believe that there is no cure which is brought about with medicine, nor with anything else after the setting of cautery.
But the matter is contrary to that, and the intention that cautery is the last of medicine is not, except that when we administer methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not work: then we administer cautery at the end of the matter, and it works: then it follows that cautery is the last of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and many foolish physicians understand.
The ancients however said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron: and they did not say that except because of the evenness of gold and the excellence of its substance: and they said that the place of cautery does not produce pus. Truly that is not so absolutely: because I have now proved it, and found that it does not do that in some bodies without others.
And cautery with it is good, and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you heat a cautery in the fire made of gold, it is not clear to you when it is heated to the quantity you want, because of the redness of the gold: and when it approaches coldness, and if you add more heating to it, it melts in the fire, and turns into sheets.
Therefore the craftsman falls from it into difficulty: and because of that, cautery with iron is quicker and closer to us, in straightforwardness for the operation.
And I indeed have now arranged this Chapter on Cautery, according to chapters: and I have arranged them from head to foot: so that they may ease him who seeks what he wants from it.
Chapter II.
This cauterization contributes to the dominance of humidity and coldness over the brain: which two are the cause of headache, and the abundance of flow of humors from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and of much sleep, and toothache, and sore throat: and universally of every sickness which happens from coldness, such as paralysis, and headache, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The method of cauterization is that you should order the patient: that he be evacuated first with a cleansing solvent medicine for the head for three nights, or four, according to what the strength of the patient, and his age, and his habit require.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor. Then make him sit between your hands squarely, so that he has now placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the root of your palm on the root of his nose between his eyes. Wherever, therefore, your middle finger reaches, mark that place with ink.
Then heat the olive cautery; this is its form.
Then place it on the place marked with ink, descending, so that your hand is gradually tightened, and you will roll it. Then quickly lift your hand, and look at the place.
If you see now revealed from the bone the quantity of the head of alkiel, or the quantity of zeuherb, then lift your hand. And if not, repeat with your hand the same iron, or with another if it cools, until you see the bones which I told you about.
Then take some salt, and dissolve it in water, and soak the cautery in it, and put it on the place, and leave it for three days.
Then put on it the cautery soaked in butter, and leave it on the place until the fire scab is destroyed. Then treat it with tetrapharmacon ointment until it is healed.
Now they have said that the more a wound remains open, the more pus it emits: wherefore it is better and more helpful.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized as far as the bone; and the cautery should be held until something of the thickness of the bone is burned.
Then shave off what has been burned from the bone, then treat it.
And others say that it is necessary to reach the utmost with the cautery, until it makes a strong impression on the bone, so that the bone falls, which is done like the shape of kirate: or the instrument which women put in infused small writing: and they think that vapors of the head are exhaled from this place, and the wound is left open for a long time.
Then let it be treated until it is consolidated.
But to me these two kinds of cautery do not seem at all suitable except in some men according to the way of fear.
And to leave it, in my opinion, is better with its safety.
For the head is weakened by the solution of its natural continuity: as we have testified in the other members: and especially when the head of the weak person is naturally weak: and indeed the first kind of cautery is safer and better in my opinion, and I use it: therefore operate with it safely.
Chapter III.
When chronic pain occurs in the whole head: and it is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches, and cuppings, and head purges, and oils, and cataplasms. And especially if it has already been cauterized with one cautery, which we have described, and none of those things benefits him: then consider. For if the patient's head is of strong natural constitution, and is not weak, and finds intense cold: then cauterize with another cautery a little below that one. Then cauterize it above every horn of his head with a cautery, until the skin is pierced, and a quantity of bone, as we have described, is uncovered. And cauterize it with one cautery in the occiput of his head, in the place which is called the vessel: and it is the place where a cupping glass is placed: and raise your instrument in this, and do not uncover the bone. For the patient finds a very intense pain because of that, and different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I shall call this cautery in its place: and it is necessary that it be a cautery, with which the horns of the head are cauterized, and its occiput with a finer cautery, with which the middle of the head is cauterized.
Chapter IV.
When pain and throbbing occur in a part of the head, or the pain extends to the eye, and the head is evacuated with purifying medicines, and the remaining treatment which I have described in the divisions of illnesses is administered, and it does not entirely help: then cauterization is performed in it according to two methods: either cauterization with a sharp burning medicine: or with iron.
Now cauterization with a sharp burning medicine is as follows: you take one clove of garlic, and peel it, and cut off its ends on both sides. Then incise the place of pain at the time with a broad phlebotom, until a space is made under the skin, in which the clove is held.
Then insert it under the skin, until it is hidden: then bind over it with a suitable compress, and leave it for fifteen hours, then unbind it, and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days: afterwards apply a linen cloth soaked in butter over it, until the place produces pus: then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish to do it with certain burning medicines, which I have written about in the twelfth treatise, on Burning Medicines.
Cauterization however with iron, is in this way. A fire cautery, whose form is this.
And it is called a nail, because the form of its head is like the form of a nail, in which there is a certain curve, and in the middle of it there is a small projection.
Then place it on the spot, and hold your hand: and you slowly turn the iron quickly: and let the amount that is burned be of the thickness of the skin, as much as half of it. And raise your hand, so that the artery, which is below, is not burned, and bleeding occurs.
Then soak a cloth in salt water, and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days: then apply a cloth with butter: afterwards treat with ointment until it heals.
And if you wish, cauterize this hemicrania with the second cultellari extremity of the Cautery. And beware of cutting the artery in this hemicrania, not the old one properly.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in an old hemicrania.
When you treat a hemicrania with what we have said from the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not help: and you see from the illness that what we have said about the first cautery with medicine, or with fire cautery, is not sufficient, then you must heat the cultellar cautery until it becomes white after you mark on the place of pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or about that: and place your hand at once and press it until you cut the artery, and reach the bone.
Nevertheless it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the mandible, which moves in mastication, lest the side be burned, or the nerve which moves it, whence a spasm may result.
And be watchful and cautious about the flow of blood from the artery which has been cut. For in its incision there is danger, and especially to him who is ignorant of what he does, and is not studious, nor experienced: and he leaves the operation with that in the first place.
And I will come with a remembrance of the regime of the flow of blood happening from the artery according to its manner, and in its place in this book.
But if you see from the illness that this cautery is not sufficient for him, and you see the body of the sick person tolerating cautery in the middle of the head, as we have narrated, treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we mentioned in the chapter on the extraction of the artery with a cautery having two knives. For cauterization is better than this and more beneficial.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in earache.
When pain occurs in the ear from cold, and it is treated with resolvents and the other treatment which we mentioned in the division, and the pain is not removed, then cauterize with fire; and it is that which is called a point, whose form is this.
Then make points with it after its ignition around the whole ear, as it is revolved; or around both, if the pain is in both at the same time. And move the cautery a little away from the root of the ear, after you have marked the places with a hot iron; and let the quantity of the cautery be ten points in each ear, about like that.
Then treat the places until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in torticollis.
Chapter VII.
Torticollis which is treated with cauterization is only from the types which occur from phlegm, according to what we said in the divisions of illnesses.
And avoid cauterization of the type which occurs from dryness and spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this type of torticollis with bleeding, and purgatives, and gargles, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, and a second a little lower than its temple, and a third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected part. Because softening does not occur except in the part which appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with a cautery directly at the end of the upper ear below the horn of the head a little: and another in the temple: whose length should be according to the length of the thumb. And place your hand with the cautery until half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, whose form has been described above; however, it is not a little finer than that, as you see: and it is necessary that there be a knife of a little excess thickness in it.
Then take care of the place, with what preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy becomes chronic, and you treat it with what we have said, and your treatment does not help, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries on each horn of his head, and a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said, and a cautery in the occiput of the head, according to what preceded: and the method of cauterization should be according to what preceded.
Also, cauterization is done with a cautery above the stomach; and this is more of a last resort.
Then let him be treated with what preceded.
Chapter IX.
It is necessary that, in the beginning, large doses of hiera and pills that cleanse the brain be given to the patient in a drink.
Then shave the patient's entire head, and apply to his occiput the mustard plaster described in the treatise on plasters; and apply it several times.
For this is the method of cauterization: and do it according to the same order which I described there.
If therefore he is healed by it, it is good. If not, then make for him three cauterizations in the posterior part of his head, which should descend from the upper part of the head to the lower part of the eye: and place between each cauterization the width of a finger.
Then treat the cauterization with what preceded.
But if you wish an addition, and the patient is able to tolerate it: then make for him a cauterization in the middle. But if you wish an addition: then cauterize him above the two horns.
Then treat it until it heals; and let the cauterization be olive-shaped according to the form which was described before.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in paralysis, and softening.
Chapter X.
It is necessary that you proceed in the cleansing of the head with hyssop, and with that which we have said.
Then shave the head of the patient; afterwards cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery above each horn of the head, and with a cautery behind it, and with three above the vertebrae of the neck.
But if you need in the illness a softening of the body with more of those things, and the patient is tolerant, and the illness is severe: then cauterize him with four cauteries above the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery, until much of his skin is burned, and raise your hand. Then treat him until he is healed: and so cauterize with olive oil.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless his epilepsy is from phlegm.
Therefore it is necessary that you first cleanse his brain with remedies, and with the other treatment which we described in the division: when the patient is strong, or is tolerant in receiving medicines.
But if he is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then a gargle and masticatories cleansing the brain are administered beforehand for many days, with improvement of diet.
Then shave his head, afterwards cauterize it with one cautery in the middle of the head, as preceded in the manner: and with another cautery in his occiput, and over each horn of his head with a cautery. But if the patient is strong and is tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I mentioned in the case of one having paralysis, and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back: and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the method which preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a fine cautery, according to this method.
Concerning the method of cauterization in melancholia.
When the cause of melancholia is corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we mentioned in the case of one having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is an excess inclined to melancholia, and the body of the patient is moist, then give him something to drink which will cleanse his brain, according to what preceded in the division: then shave the head of the patient, and afterwards make a properly made compress of linen, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head: and the patient sitting squarely should be held on every side.
Then present the liniment. And it is more worthy from old sheep butter. And heat it over a fire with a moderate measured heat, which a finger can tolerate when put into it. Then empty it in the middle of his head in a circle, and leave it until it cools.
Do this for the patient once every week, along with his other good regimen, until he is healed.
Or if you wish, cauterize it making very many small points of it, except that you hold your hand with the cautery: rather, it is by smelling.
For this kind of cautery moistens the brain with a quality.
Then place on it a cloth soaked in butter, or chicken fat.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a descending flow to the eye.
Chapter XIII.
When the beginning of water in the eye is declared to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and purge the patient with that which cleanses his head.
And guard him from all dampness, and make him sit in a bath, fasting for several days.
Then order him to shave his head, and cauterize him in the middle of his head. Then cauterize him on the two temples with two cauteries, if the beginning of the descent of water is in both eyes at once; or on one side, if the beginning of water is in one eye: and cut off with the cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin: and let the cauteries be long in the breadth of the temples: and beware of the flow of blood.
But if you see something of it, then cut it off immediately with whatever medicine is possible: and I will come with wisdom, which is in the extraction of arteries, and the cutting off of them, and caution from the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck, under the two bones, with two cauteries ultimately produced.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in old tears.
When the tears of the eyes are old, persistent, and are upon the veins and arteries which are manifest on the exterior of the head, it is verified that this is from cold, phlegmatic superfluities: then cauterize with the same cautery that I narrated in the beginning of the descending water, with a cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries upon the two temples, and two cauteries in the neck under the two bones. And if it needs addition, then cauterize it on every side cautiously of the eye, above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the stench of the nose.
When you treat it with what we said in the division, and the medication does not help: then hasten, and give the patient decoctions for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. Then cauterize it with a nail-shaped cautery, with two cauteries, above the two eyebrows under the hair a little.
And beware of the artery, lest you strike it.
And this is the shape of the nail-shaped cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the softening of the eyelid.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize the eyelid with one cautery, with this lunate cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part: and let them be far from the two temples: and let the length of each cautery be according to the length of the eyebrow: and do not move your hand with the cautery, but according to the amount that burns a third of the eyelid.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the eyelid when the hairs turn inward, pricking the eye.
Cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with a burning medicine.
In cauterization with fire, however, instruct the patient beforehand to remove his hairs, if he is one of those who cleanse them, until they grow long and are equal. But if they prick him at their root, then bind his eyes with a band so that they do not move until they grow.
When therefore they grow and are equal, then place the patient's head in your bosom; then mark above the eyelid of his eyes with a branding iron, a mark which is according to the form of a myrtle leaf; and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
Then place a pledget soaked in egg white or in flaxseed mucilage over the eye.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this.
Then cauterize over the mark which you marked gradually in many times, until the surface of the skin, which is like the form of a myrtle leaf, is entirely burned properly.
And a sign of the health of your work is: that if you see the eyelid of the eye now contract, and the hairs rise up at the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days.
Then place a pledget with butter on it, until the eschar is removed. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some of the hairs return after a while, and the eyelid softens, then repeat the cautery on that place, as you did at first.
But if the hairs are below, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is level so that the hairs do not prick the eye.
It is to instruct the patient to let the hairs grow until they are long and even. Make from paper or cloth the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Then take of known soap and of unslaked lime, one ounce of each or thereabouts; then grind them together well and do it quickly so that it does not get cold. Then spread some of it on the paper you made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place it on one or both eyelids. And place under the eye a piece of cloth soaked in egg white; and let the patient's head be in your bosom.
And place your index finger on the medicine, and press it gently. And you should move it whenever the patient feels the medicine biting, because its bite is found to be like fire.
While therefore the bite persists, then remove the medicine and move it with your finger. When therefore the biting ceases, then remove the medicine, and wash the eye with water.
And then consider, if you see the eyelid raised, as it is raised in contraction with fire, or with incision, it is well: and if not, then repeat on it from the medicine above the place, in which the medicine does not impress, and does not blacken, until your operation is equal, and the eye is contracted.
Then place a pledget with butter on it, until the burnt skin is eradicated. Then treat it with diaphnicion ointment, or another, until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you in the operation, that you keep the utmost care, lest anything of the medicine fall into the eye.
But if the eyelid softens after days, and something fights from the hairs in the eye, then repeat the medicine on that place, which softens from the eyelid itself, as you did in the beginning. Then treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of a myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified in size and smallness. Therefore, according to that, let your consideration be: and the way of rectitude is not hidden from him who studies this art.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a lachrymal fistula.
When you treat a fistula, with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you cauterize it according to this method.
Instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom, and let a helper hold his head between his hands in such a way that it does not move nor be shaken.
Then place a pledget soaked in egg white or in flaxseed mucilage over his eye; then heat the cautery, whose form is this.
Let it be concave, like the form of a vulture's quill from one end, with which the cautery is made: and if you wish it to be penetrating to the other end, and if you wish it to be upward, try thus.
However, this concave shape is better for your work.
Then press the fistula, if it is open, and draw out the pus, and dry it; and if it is not open, then open it, and draw out its pus.
Then place the cautery, which is made very hot, upon it.
And hold your hand with it a little near the cautery from the eye, towards the side of the nose, so that your hand may not err, or the patient be disturbed: and you should place the cautery in the fat of the eye, and destroy it.
Therefore, if you reach with the beginning of your cautery to the bone, it will be well. And if not, then repeat the cautery once, or twice, if you need it: and leave it for three days.
Then apply a pledget with butter, and treat it with a drying ointment, until it is healed.
But if forty days have passed, and it is not healed: then put on it a sharp corrosive medicine, until the bone is exposed: and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which certain early physicians described. Apply to the place of the fistula, and incise it: then place in the incision a thin tube, that is, a slender cannula, of which the form is this. And pour into it the quantity of a drachm of molten lead, and hold your hand with the cannula firmly, and let the patient not move at all, lest the molten lead run, or reach his eye.
And it is necessary that you place over the patient's eye a compress soaked in egg white, or in water.
For the lead burns the place of the fistula, and heals it with a marvelous healing.
If the fistula is healed with what we have said, by cautery and treatment, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to treat the opening of the nose, and to reduce the fistula to the nasal passage, according to what will come to its own proper place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing fissures of the lips.
Often fissures occur on the lip, which are called chaps, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures, with what we said in the division of illnesses, and the treatment does not help, then cauterize with a small knife, like this.
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then quickly place the heated [knife] in the fissures themselves, until the cautery reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerate, until it heals.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a fistula of the mouth.
When an abscess occurs at the root of the gum, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth: then it produces pus, and bursts; afterwards it becomes old, and pus runs, and a fistula forms: then you treat it, and the treatment does not benefit it, then it is necessary that you apply a cautery according to the amount that it takes in the fistula. Then insert the heated iron into the opening of the fistula, and hold your hand until the heated iron reaches its depth and its end; do this once or twice.
Then treat it afterwards with what we have said about treatment until it is cured.
If therefore the pus is cut away, and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place, and to remove the corrupt bone according to what will come into its head.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the gums and softened teeth.
When the gum is softened because of humors, and the teeth are moved, and you treat it with medicines, and it does not help: then place the patient's head in your bosom.
Then a burning cautery, whose shape will come after this, so that you place a cannula on the tooth: and quickly introduce the ignited cautery into it: and hold your hand a little while, until the patient feels the heat of the fire has already reached the root of the tooth.
And lift your hand; then repeat the cautery several times, according to what you wish.
Then let the patient fill his mouth with salt water, and hold it for one hour: and spit it out.
For the moved tooth is strengthened, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt humor is dried.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in toothache.
When the toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold or a worm in the tooth, and treatment with medicines is not helpful: then cauterization is used in two ways: either cauterization with butter or cauterization with fire.
Now cauterization with butter is as follows: take cow's butter and boil it in an iron spoon or in a pan. Then take a tent and wrap it around the end of a probe. Then immerse it in the boiling butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth, and hold it until it cools; then repeat this several times until the effect of the heat reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or a tent in cold butter, and place it on the aching tooth; and place a red-hot iron on top of it until the fire reaches the depth of the tooth's cavity.
Cauterization with fire, however, is done by directing a bronze or iron tube. And let there be something in its body of some thickness, so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery, whatever shape it may be, and place it on that tooth, and hold your hand until the cautery cools.
Do this several times, for the pain will be removed, either on that same day or another.
And it is necessary in the impression of that cautery that the patient fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for one hour; then he should spit it out.
And this is the shape of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever end you wish, as is possible; and this is the shape of the cannula.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in strophuli.
When strophuli are from phlegm and from cold humors, and are not obedient to digestion through medicines, and you wish to digest them quickly: then a concave cautery of fire, whose shape is this.
Having two open ends, so that smoke may escape from the cautery at the other end: and place it ignited upon the abscess once, and a second time if you need it, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
But if the abscess is small, then place the cautery according to the size of the abscess.
Then leave it for three days, and place upon it lint infused in butter until that which the fire burned is removed.
Then treat it with tooth unguent until it is healed in the shape of a tube.
Concerning the method of cauterization in hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
When humors overcome the lung passage, and especially when it is with coldness of complexion, then it is necessary that the sick person be evacuated first with solutive medicines.
Then cauterize it in the uvula near the root of the throat in a depressed place: and beware lest you reach the throat with the cautery, nor the skin, except a little of it.
Then cauterize it with another cautery at the juncture of the neck in the last vertebra thereof, finally: and let the cautery be nail-shaped according to the manner which preceded.
Then treat it with what preceded, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in illnesses of the lung, and cough.
When cough and illness of the lung are from cold humors, and the infirm person does not have fever, nor consumption, and the illness is chronic: then cauterize him with two cauteries above the clavicle in depressed, soft, empty places. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples: and let the cautery be a tube according to the form which preceded.
And if you wish your cauterization to be by puncturing with a cautery, which is called Punctum, whose form preceded in the puncturing of earache: and let the points be from thirty points up to about others.
Then treat him with what preceded, until he is healed.
And now let the cautery have three branches: according to this form. Therefore the cauterization is alleviated with it, when you do with it at one time three cauterizations.
Chapter XXVII.
When the head of the humerus is dislocated by reason of humors, and is not fixed in the hour of its return from its dislocation, until it becomes accustomed to returning, then it is dislocated by a small movement, which happens as we have already testified, then it is necessary that you reduce the dislocation first. Then the patient is laid on his back, or on his healthy side. Then raise the skin which is in the inner armpit upwards with the fingers of your left hand: and if the joint is dislocated inwards.
Then have a cautery with two points. Whose form is this.
Then cauterize the skin until it penetrates it to the other side, and the figure of the cauterization will come with four cauteries.
And sometimes the cauterization is done with a cautery having three points, wherefore the figure of the cauterization then becomes six cauteries: and the points are according to the thinness of a probe.
And this is the form of a cautery having three points.
And sometimes one is added in this way, wherefore there are eight cauteries.
Then put crushed leeks with salt on the cautery. Then make the patient adhere to stillness, so that the limb is not moved for some time, until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is towards the upper parts, and this rarely happens, then cauterize it above the shoulder with a clavicular cautery, or with many cauteries puncturing.
For the joint is strengthened, and the moisture is removed: and the patient is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the stomach.
When cold and much moisture befall the stomach, until they draw it away from its complexion, and catarrhs multiply in it, and the methods of treatment are continuous, and do not help: then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back, and extend his legs and his hands. Then cauterize with three cauteries, a cautery under the breastbone the size of one finger with a nail-shaped cautery, and two cauteries below on the two sides of the single cautery, until the figure of the cauteries becomes triangular. And space them out so that they do not join, when they produce pus.
And let the depth of the cautery be two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And let the figure of the cauteries be according to this form without addition: and if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form: and let the cautery with which this cauterization is done be one according to the figure of a circle according to this form.
And the stomach is cauterized by puncturing it for him who is frightened by this cautery: and it is so that you mark on the stomach a point according to the quantity which you wish with a hot iron: then cauterize it with a pointed cautery: then treat it with the treatment which preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a cold liver.
When pain occurs in the liver from coldness and dampness, or from gross flatulence, until it departs from its natural complexion by superfluous excretion, and the patient is treated with what we have said, and it does not benefit him: then it is necessary that the patient be laid on his back, and you mark with a hot iron three cauteries according to this figure, and this same quantity on the lower liver below the hypochondria, where the elbow of a man reaches: and let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight body: and do not press your hand with the cautery much.
And let the quantity of what is burned be from the thickness of the skin, a quantity of half of it not added: and let the patient stand on his feet.
But if he is not standing, then let him lie down, who now extends his legs and raises his arms.
This is the form of the cautery.
And it is indeed possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife cautery, when you have skill and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed to the end in cauterization, and the thickness of the whole skin is burned, wherefore the whole body is burned, and it reaches the intestines: because the skin there is thin, therefore know that.
Concerning the method of cauterizing abscesses of the liver.
When an exit wound occurs, and you wish to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver, or in its tunic: then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient experiences pain and heaviness without sharpness; and if it is in the tunic of the liver, there is intense, sharp pain; and you see now that its treatment has fatigued the physicians.
Therefore, the patient should lie on his back. Then mark the location of the abscess with a cautery; then heat a cautery that resembles a ray. And this is its form. And cauterize with it one cautery until the whole skin is burned, and it reaches the siphac, until all the pus comes out: then treat it with the treatment of wounds, until it heals.
And it is not fitting that one use this kind of cautery unless his study in the art of medicine is prolonged, and through whose hands these illnesses occur with some experience; for then he may dare to perform such an operation.
But truly, to leave it alone is better in my opinion.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in pleurisy.
The ancients said, cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy in this manner. And it is that you should take of the dried roots of birthwort, one longer root which is found among them, and let it be like the thickness of a finger. Then immerse it in oil and ignite it in the fire. Then cauterize with one cautery in that which is between the continuation of the clavicle with the neck, and with two small cauteries somewhat sloping downwards to the part which is under the chin.
And with two cauteries also in that which is between the fifth and sixth rib, sloping slightly backwards: and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from the back: one in that which is between the two shoulder blades: and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, lower than the cautery which is between the two shoulder blades.
And it is not necessary that you press your hand with the cautery, rather let it be on the surface of the skin, smelling.
And now some ancients have said that there are some men who use a cautery of iron similar to a ray: and they ignite it, and insert it inside in that which is between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess with it, and pus comes out: as we said in the abscess of the Liver.
But in this perforation with a cautery there is fear that either the patient dies immediately, or a fistula occurs in the place, for which there is no healing.
Chapter XXXII. On the method of cauterizing the Spleen.
When you treat the illness of the spleen, with what we said of treatment in the division of illnesses, and your treatment does not help, then cautery in it is according to three methods, which are all correct.
One of which is to make three cauteries, or four, in a line according to the length of the spleen, according to the figure of the cauteries of the Liver, the figure of which preceded.
And let there be between each cautery the quantity of the thickness of a finger, or a little more.
And let the method of cautery be, as we said in the cautery of the liver, equally.
And do not press your hand with the cautery: and let the patient lie supine on his back.
And another method of cauterization is: that you burn with a cautery having two prongs, which we mentioned in the chapter on the dislocation of the humerus: and lift the skin which is in the direction of the spleen, where the left elbow of the patient reaches: and let your lifting of the skin be according to the breadth of the body, so that the cauteries fall according to the length of the body.
Then insert two very hot prongs until you penetrate the skin on the other side with them: then withdraw the cautery: therefore four cauteries are made.
And if you wish to cauterize with another cautery having three prongs.
Then take care of the cautery after you leave it, that it discharge pus for many days: for it is more helpful than all the others which preceded it in the treatment.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in dropsy.
Cauterization does not apply in dropsy, except for ascites specifically.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not help, then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries around the navel, and one cautery above the stomach, and another cautery above the liver, and a cautery above the spleen, and two cauteries on the back between the vertebrae, and one in the region of the chest, and another in the region of the stomach: and let the depth of the cautery be close to the thickness of the skin.
Then leave the cautery open, so that it may discharge pus for a long time.
And do not remove the patient from treatment after cauterization with him: for it is necessary that two intentions are combined for him: and healing will hasten to him with God's help.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized, should be nail-shaped: according to what preceded its form: and that with which the back is cauterized, should be olive-shaped according to what preceded its form.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the feet and legs.
When abscesses form in a dropsical patient and are filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize on the dorsum of the foot in the hollow which is between the little finger and the ring finger: and raise your hand with the cautery, and do not twist it: then raise your hand: and do not repeat it at all.
Then the yellowish water will sweat out, and let the cautery be according to this shape.
Then make two cauteries on each of the two legs, and let the cautery be made with the knife-like end of the cautery: and let the cautery be made according to the length of the leg, one below the knee, and another below that, about the middle of the leg: and on each hip two cauteries: and leave the cautery open without treatment for a long time: so that some water sweats out from it: then treat it with the other things which we have mentioned.
On the method of cauterizing in looseness of the bowels.
Chapter 35.
When there is looseness of the bowels from cold and humors, until it weakens the retentive and digestive powers which are in the stomach and intestines: and it is treated with methods of treatment, and is not healed: and you see the patient tolerating cauterization of exuberant virtue, then cauterize him with a large cautery on his stomach, according to what preceded in the cauterization of the stomach with a circular cautery, and four small cauteries around the navel, with a thin nail-like cautery: and a cautery on the alcatin above the alhosos, large, or two cauteries.
And if you see exuberant humors, and the patient tolerating it, then cauterize him with a cautery on the pubis, and a cautery on each ilium: and perhaps you will add two small cauteries on the stomach near the large one.
For it is a beneficial treatment, whose help does not err.
Concerning the method of cauterizing hemorrhoids.
Chapter XXXVI.
When in the anus there are many old hemorrhoids, or one, and they are from thick, cold humors, or corrupt humors, and they are treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help: then cauterize with three cauteries on the lower vertebra of the back below the almond slightly in a triangle, and with a cautery below the navel about two fingers.
And if you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face becoming inflamed, then cauterize him above the stomach with a large cautery, according to what preceded: and cauterize above his liver, and another above his spleen, with a nail-shaped cautery: and leave the cautery open for some time: then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter XXXVII.
When you cut away althelul, then heat a cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert it, burning, into the excised althelul: and move your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which blood flows: do this once or twice.
If therefore the althelul are many, then cauterize each one with a cautery according to what we have described. Then treat them with what benefits them of ointments, until they are healed.
And if you cauterize it with a large cautery over the alcatin, it is more effective in helping.
Concerning the method of cauterizing a fistula in the anus.
Chapter XXXVIII.
When the patient does not comply with the incision and the procedure which we have described in its place, and is frightened by it, perhaps he may be healed with a cautery.
Therefore, when a fistula occurs in someone, and it becomes old, pus and corrupted humors flow from it. Therefore, it is more fitting that you should probe it with a slender probe. Then ascertain the amount of its depth with a probe.
Then heat a cautery which is similar to a ray. Then insert the heated cautery into the fistula and the amount that the nail entered.
And repeat the cautery on it until all those corrupt bodies are burned, once, or twice, or three times, according to the amount of your necessity.
And guard yourself from burning a nerve, if it is there, or a large vein: or if the fistula goes to the body of the bladder, or to the body of the intestine, then guard yourself from all those places.
And do not do that, except when the fistula is in a fleshy place: and you think that it is not penetrating.
Then take care of the place until it heals.
But if the place is sealed, and the materials are cut away from it, and it remains so for a long time: then know that it is now completely healed.
And if materials are not cut away from it, then know that it is penetrating, or in its depth there is a corrupt bone, or something like that from those: the mention of which will come in its place.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the kidneys.
When pain occurs in the kidneys from cold or from thick wind, and the strength of the sick person is diminished because of it, then it is necessary to cauterize it above the almethen above the kidneys, cauterizing each kidney with a cautery, the mention of which has preceded.
And perhaps we cauterize it a second time in the almeidatu. Therefore three lines will come. Therefore the last will be more helpful.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder from cold and dampness, until the infirm person does not retain urine, then cauterize it in the lower part of the pubis above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and with a cautery from the right of the pubis, and another from the left of it: and let the length of that lateral cauterization be according to the size of the thumb's knuckle; and cauterize it with a cautery in the lower part of the back, or with two cauteries, if you need it: and let it be according to what preceded, a clavile cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the womb.
When cold and dampness occur in the womb, and it is prevented by that from impregnation, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs to her at their arrival, then it is necessary that it be cauterized with three cauteries around the navel, as we said in the cauterization of the bladder, and with a cautery above the pubis in the lower back, or with two cauteries: and let it be a clavile cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing the anus in dislocation.
When mucous humors are poured out to the socket of the hip, and become the cause of its exit from its place: and the sign of this is that the leg is lengthened over the other, when they are compared together: and the place of the dislocation is found in it empty.
Therefore it is necessary that you cauterize the patient above the hip socket with a similar circular cautery: after you mark with a hot iron around the socket, as it is rolled: and that the socket itself fall in the middle of the circle.
But if this cautery is not present to you, then cauterize it with three cauteries, with a large olive-shaped cautery: and let the depth of the cautery be according to the quantity of the thickness of the whole skin.
Then take care of it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in sciatica.
When pain occurs in the hip joint, and its cause is cold and dampness: and the patient is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and it becomes chronic: then it is necessary that you resolve the patient from gross humors with foul-smelling pills, or with artificial pills, and the like. Then cauterize him.
And the method of cauterization in it is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with fire is according to many methods.
One of which is, that you cauterize above the hip joint itself with three triangular cauteries, according to this form. And make them a good, or bad depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the quantity of the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
Sometimes however the cautery is done in the middle above the head of the hip joint itself. Therefore there are four cauteries.
And if you wish to cauterize with a circle, the form of which preceded in the cauterization of the dislocation of the hip, with one: so that it is a circle containing the whole hip.
And burn the thickness of the whole skin.
This is the method of cauterization in sciatica, when the pain is adhering to the hip itself, and is not extended to the thigh and leg.
But if it extends to the thigh and leg, then cauterize it with two cauteries above the thigh, above the place to which the sick person points there, in which there is pain, and with your cautery above the hollow of the leg four fingers towards the outside slightly: and let the cautery be knife-shaped.
And let the depth of the cautery be according to the depth of the thickness of the skin.
But if the sick person indicates that the pain extends almost to the toes, then cauterize it there where he indicates with a pointed cautery, with three, or four, or more, if it needs that.
And if he indicates the pain to be below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize it there with one knife-shaped cautery.
And beware in all your cauterization, lest you go so far with the cautery as to burn a nerve, or a large artery, and draw upon the sick person through that a great harm, or contraction.
And I indeed have already seen one and another, of those who cauterized above the ankle, and went too far in cauterization, and the leg swelled, until the swelling reached the foot, and it was wholly perforated, and the whole foot was corrupted: then there followed a bursting of the belly, and death after that.
If the pain is in two parts at once, cauterize each according to this method.
And certain wise men have said concerning the cauterization of the hip, of which cautery this is the method. Make a similar iron cautery, whose diameter is half a palm: and let it be at its opening according to the thickness of a date stone, or a little less: and within that cautery let there be a third cautery.
And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of a thumb-joint: and let the cauteries be open on two sides: and let their height be like one joint, or two joints: and make for them a handle of iron decently fitted to the cauteries.
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks: then let it be placed upon the hip-joint, the patient lying on his sound side. Therefore cauterize him with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave him for three days. Afterwards apply plasters with butter: and leave the wound open for many days. Then treat him with ointment until he is healed.
Albucasis says, "My son, we have used this type of cautery little, because of the terror of its appearance: and because we have found few who would endure it.
Nevertheless, it is one of the good cauteries for him who endures it, and finds its proper place.
The cautery however with burning medicines is such that you make two samia having a perforated ring, into which descends the hinge of its door of bronze, or iron, the length of the wall: at whose height are two nodes, or things like them.
And let the space which is between both be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, and let them be open below and above: and let that which they contain be joined together, according to this form.
Then place it upon the hip socket, the patient lying on the sound side. And press your hand upon the hip socket very much. Then pour between the two circles warm water, which has been heated a little with fire, and hold it for the amount of an hour: and let the patient endure its biting, and its burning, for he finds the biting like fire, until the biting ceases.
Then lift your hand with the instrument, and wipe away the warm water from the whole hip with fresh water: then leave it for three days.
And place butter upon it, until the blackness of the burn is removed: and leave it for several days, and pus will flow from it. For it is the ultimate in assistance.
Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
But if the pain extends to the hip or leg: make for him a bath in that which contains water, according to what you did to the hip equally.
And this is the method of hot water: and I have already written it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and unslaked lime, one part of each; grind both, and put them in a new pot, the lower part of which is perforated with one small hole, according to the quantity that the ray enters it: and put under the bottom of the pot another glass pot: and pour over the alkali and lime fresh water, which submerges both by a finger's breadth: after which you compress them with your hand with good compression: and leave the pot until the sharp water descends to the lower part of the glass pot.
Then collect all that water, and pour it over more lime; and filter until it is renewed.
For then it will be very strong in sharpness, which is administered in many medical operations, and in the cauterization of other members. Because it produces the operation of fire itself.
And among medicines are those with which ankles are cauterized, also such as tesisie, and cashew nut honey, and lime with soap, so that both are mixed.
And Galen indeed, in narrating, recounted the cure of a certain man from antiquity, by which he was cured of hip pain and sciatica; and he magnified it to be very good. And he said that he did not need another cure, and that he was healed in one time, until he recounted that he perhaps entered a bath carried, and came out of it already healed.
And it is so that it is taken from fresh rue; and if fresh is not found, let dried, recent [rue] be taken; and let it be ground much with a little fat, and let it be placed on the hip, where there is pain, or leg, or in the thigh; and let it be bound, and let it be left for the quantity of three hours, or for the amount that the infirm person feels a soothing of the burning. Then introduce him into a bath.
When therefore his body becomes moist, introduce him into a chair. For the pain recedes, and it is healed.
And know that it is not necessary that anyone use a cautery with these medicines, except after evacuation of the body.
And Dioscorides also said that the dung of goats, when the sciatic vein is cauterized with it, benefits it: and the cautery is done in this way. Let wool be taken, and let it be soaked in old oil, and let it be placed upon the deep place which is between the thumb of the foot and the ankle, and is nearer the ankle.
Then take dry goat dung, and inflame it in the fire until it becomes charcoal.
Then place it upon the wool, and leave it until it is extinguished. Then repeat it. Therefore do not cease doing this until sensation reaches the middle as far as the ankle, and the pain rests.
And this method of cautery is called the Vaccine cautery.
Concerning the cauterization of back pain.
Sometimes pain occurs in the back from many causes, either from a fall, or from a blow, or from superfluous evacuation, and similar things, from the infusion of cold and moist matter; and cautery indeed does not fall except in this one kind which is produced from the infusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuating the patient with foul pills and the like, three lines should be made on the back where the pain is, according to the width of the patient's back, after marking with encaustic in each line five cauteries, or more according to the quantity that comes from the patient's tolerance and strength: and let the cauterization be done with a punctate cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries, with a medial nail cautery, according to this form.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the beginning of gibbosity.
This illness often happens to small infants.
And a sign of its beginning in children is that a constriction occurs in their breathing upon elevation and movement; and you find in the last vertebra of the back and its knots that it has already begun to protrude above the other vertebrae.
Therefore, when you see it and wish to treat it, then cauterize it with a cautery which is circular according to this form.
And let the cautery take in all parts of the vertebra equally; and if you wish, cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery, two or three lines, and let the points be close together.
Then treat the place until it heals with what we have said.
And beware lest you apply the cautery to a hump which is caused by a spasm of the nerve.
Concerning the method of cauterization in gout and the pains of the joints.
When the pains of the joints are from cold humors which are poured out into any limbs of the body, then when pains occur in the feet, it is customary for physicians to name this properly gout.
Therefore, when gout of a cold cause is treated with methods of cure, and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is so that one cauterizes him after evacuation around the joint of the feet, with many cauteries; and let the cautery be olive-shaped.
And if you need to puncture on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. But if the pains ascend to the knees, or the other joints, and this happens many times, then cauterize it above each knee with three cauteries, or four on every side, with this same claviform cautery.
If, however, more are necessary with this cautery, then cauterize it. And do not submerge your hand with the cautery, rather let it be about the thickness of the skin only.
But if the pains ascend to the knees and the back: then administer what we said of the cautery in its chapter.
But if the pains are only in the hands, then puncture around the joints, as two lines are rolled around.
But if pain remains in the fingers, then puncture them, above every knot a puncture, and above the comb of the hand.
But if the pains ascend after days to the elbows or to the shoulders, then cauterize both from every side.
And do not deprive the patient of good regimen, of the reception of medicines. For if good regimen is done, and phlegm is evacuated, it will be healed with God's help with this cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, and the intestine or bladder descends to the testicle, and it is beginning nearby: then it is necessary that you order the patient to omit eating on his day. And that he use lenitives, so that stool descends from his belly.
Then let him sit between your hands on his back; and instruct him to hold his breath until the bladder or intestine emerges or appears.
Then reduce it with your fingers, and mark with a cautery under the rupture above the pubic bone, with a mark whose extremity is similar to half a circle towards the upper parts of the body.
Then heat the cautery, whose form is this, until it becomes white and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or bladder to its cavity. Then let the assistant place his hand on the place, so that the intestine does not come out; and you have now opened between the legs of the patient, and placed a cushion under him, and another servant over his legs, and another over his chest who holds his hands.
Then place the cautery on the mark, your hand with the cautery held straight. And hold it until you reach the bone with it; or repeat with it another time, if you have not reached the bone with it in the first attempt.
And carefully observe the exit of the intestine during your cauterization, lest you burn it, and thereby cause death or great harm to the patient.
And know that when you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not effective.
And it is necessary that the cautery for infants be small, according to their size.
Then treat with other ointments until it is healed.
And let the patient lie on his back for forty days, until the wound is sealed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment for the length of time of his treatment, which softens his bowels, so that the intestine does not come out during straining and evacuation.
Then when he wishes to rise after forty days, then apply a properly made bandage, and let him keep it on for another forty days.
And let him be lessened from labor, and from fullness of food and drink, and from loud voices.
For when he uses this regimen, he is healed with a complete healing.
And I shall describe the treatment of ruptures with incision in its chapter.
But when ruptures occur in the rest of the belly, and are beginning, and you wish that they not increase, then cauterize his rupture with a round cautery according to its size: and let that which is burned be then as one-third of it. Then treat him with what we have said: and it will not increase.
And now some earlier authors have said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this form.
After it is marked with a hot iron, and a line is placed which is across the width on the upper side of the rupture, and another line below. And place your hand in the middle with a nail-shaped cautery.
But the first cautery is easier and better.
Concerning the method of cauterization in pains due to a fall or blow.
Chapter XLVIII.
When pain occurs in some member from a fall or blow, and that pain persists for a long time, and treatment with medicines does not help it: then it is necessary that it be cauterized with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery of every member be according to its size, and its weakness, and its strength, and the location of the torsion, and the pain from it.
If therefore it is healed by the first cauterization, it is well. If not, then repeat the cauterization on it.
Because it is customary for these pains to move from one member to that which is near it. Therefore it is necessary that you pursue them with cautery, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in leprosy.
Lepers, moreover, are sometimes helped by cautery with the greatest benefit. And especially the type of leprosy which arises from the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy: therefore when you wish to cauterize it, consider.
If therefore the leprosy is incipient, and you have treated it with what we have said in the division, and it does not subside nor stand still: and you fear for the patient, that corruption might seize upon his whole constitution: then cauterize him upon the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, noticeably, and a second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two upon the two temples, and one behind upon the nape of the neck.
And lastly move your hand with the cautery a little, until it imprints a small impression in the mouth, and the outer layers are separated from it: so that the exhalation of gross vapors from it may be relieved, and cauterize with the cautery also upon the spleen itself, according to what has preceded.
But if the leprosy is already spread over the patient, and appears manifestly: then it is necessary that you cauterize with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head, and cauterize upon the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries upon the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries upon the vertebrae of the neck, and six upon the vertebrae of the back, and one large one upon the al-hahos (sacrum) near the tailbone and one above it, in the almeidati (coccyx): and two upon the hips: namely upon each hip one, and two upon the knees: one upon each knee, and two upon the shoulders, and two upon the elbows, and two upon the soft parts of his chest: and cauterize him upon every joint, of the joints of his fingers and toes: and cauterize upon every heel of his feet, and the wrists of his hands.
And beware of the nerves which are upon the back of the heel, so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery upon the pubic bone, and another upon the stomach, and another upon the liver.
And know that the more you add cautery, the more it is helpful, and the more beneficial.
And know that the patient does not find pain in cauterization, as healthy people find it: because his body is already numb.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be of size and smallness according to the limbs and joints: according to what preceded in the kinds of instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with the flour of the herb with honey, and the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in numbness.
When a limb is numb, and is treated with medicines, and oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to what the size or smallness of the limb deserves.
And let your cautery penetrate a little into the thickness of the skin. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes it is cauterized for some numbness which happens to the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back near the exit of the nerves which move that limb: and the numbness is removed.
Truly, let no one presume upon this except he who is wise in the anatomy of the limbs, and the exit of the nerves moving the body.
Chapter 51.
When the Albaras becomes chronic and nothing from the arts of medicine benefits it, then cauterize over it with a deep cautery slightly, according to the amount of the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness is removed and its color is changed.
Then treat it with the flour of the herb, and lentils with rose oil, and leaves of arnoglossa, and pigeons blood, and swallows blood: of each let there be one part; let all these be mixed, and spread upon a cloth, and applied to the place, until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Cancer.
When the cancer is beginning, and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it is rolled around.
The wise men, however, have said that it should be cauterized with a very hot cautery in the middle.
But to me that does not seem good; because I fear that it will ulcerate; and I have already seen that happen several times.
And it is better that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we have said; or with many cauteries.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Albucasis.
When someone has a felon, and it is slow in maturing, either because of the superfluity causing it, or because of the age of the patient, when he is decrepit and has little blood, or because of the time, and one wishes to hasten the maturation of the felon, then cauterize around it with many small cauteries, puncturing. Then leave it. For it itself will hasten with maturation.
But if you wish its perforation with a cautery, then a fire cautery, of which this is the form.
And place it in the middle of the felon, until it penetrates the skin: and let the cautery be from that which follows the patient, so that the flow of pus may be relieved.
Then care for it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a corroding sore.
Chapter 54.
A corroding sore is nothing but a corruption in a limb which spreads, and corrodes it: just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore if you see a corroding sore in a place which can tolerate cautery with fire, then have many fire cauteries, small and large nails, according to what is suitable for the place in which the corroding sore is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the whole corruption, and nothing whatsoever of it remains.
Then leave it for three days, and put on the cauterized places ground sulfur with oil, until all the eschars and all the corruption are eradicated.
Then treat it with ointments which generate flesh.
Therefore if you see that after three days flesh grows in a healthy growth, in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not, then repeat the cautery on that which remains of the corrupt places.
And a corrosive is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine. For it arises in the place of the cautery.
However, cautery with fire is of quicker help: and I indeed already said the cauterization of it with a sharp medicine in the division: therefore accept it there when you need it.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Clavus and Warts.
Chapter 55.
Often this sickness happens in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a coarse, filtered thing, which harms the foot.
And indeed cautery in them is according to two methods: cautery with fire, and cautery with hot water.
As for that with fire, you heat a concave cautery similar to a vulture's feather: which is made of iron, according to the size which contains the clavus on every side: and let it be of thin margins.
Then place it, burning, upon the clavus. Then roll the hand with the cautery around the clavus, until the cautery reaches the depth of the clavus: and leave it for three days, until it begins to emit pus. Then plaster it with wild mallows, crushed with salt: and leave the plaster upon it at night. For it itself will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the place with an ointment which generates flesh, until it is healed.
But if they are not inverted, and frequently occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet: then apply a cautery to them with warm water: and it is so that you take a tube of bronze, or iron, or from a vulture's feather: and place it upon the clavus, or althelul.
