Then pour into the tube a small quantity of warm water: hold your hand, and you revolve it with the compression of your hand a little, so that the edge of the tube is pressed into the root of the clavus, and the warm water finds a way to the depth, to the root of the clavus: and let the patient wait a little for the biting of the warm water for one hour.
Then leave it, for the clavus is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you come upon all that are of them in the body.
Then care for them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in Tremor.
When tremor happens to someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or something else: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five, above the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra a cautery, and on his chest a cautery, and above his stomach a cautery.
For tremor rests with olive cautery, and the healing of the cold illness hastens.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter 57.
Sometimes foul things are expelled in the body of Albathir, that is, pustules, which are made from gross corrupt materials.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary that they be cauterized on every head of the pustule with a subtle cautery with myrtle wood, whose end is lit with fire. Or with the root of long birthwort. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they do not increase; and the superfluity making them is broken, and they heal from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Very often there occurs a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut near a wound, which happens externally, or near an incision of an abscess, or a cauterization of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger upon it: and press it strongly, until the blood is stopped under your finger, and nothing escapes from it.
Then put into the fire small olive cauteries, and many large ones: and blow upon them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place in which the artery is broken, and place the cautery upon the vein itself, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
But if [the blood] is expelled when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries which are prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease doing this with one after another, until the blood is stopped.
And beware lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the bleeding is stopped. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or medicines with the property of staunching blood are placed upon it, and it is compressed with a suitable bandage.
But he who administers its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of cauterizing agents, and the like, does not entirely benefit him, except rarely.
But if this should happen to someone, and a physician or medicine is not present, then he should begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein, as we have described, and compressing it strongly, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, pouring very cold water constantly over it, until the blood congeals and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile he should consider what he needs in the way of cautery or medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds and similar things.
We have already said in the first chapter about every illness in which cautery with fire and burning medicine is suitable, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I placed chapters there from head to foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same way: so that the seeker may be relieved of his search.
And before I begin it, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter there is something terrifying that is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, caution is necessary in it more vehemently, because in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood often happens, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision upon an abscess, or opening of an exit, or healing of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision upon a stone, and things like these: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death happens in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that in which there is hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes there fall to you in this art types of men with types of infirmities. For among them is he who is enraged by his illness, and death is light to him: because of the vehemence of what he finds from his infirmity and the length of its malice. And with illness there is fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his wealth, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your greed and your avarice. And do not presume upon anything of it, except after certain knowledge, which verifies with you that to which a laudable outcome has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick, prognosis and indication of that to which health is suitably directed.
For in that is for you help in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding, and may He not take from you direction and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I indeed have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: that the thing which you wish from it, and which you need, may be lightened for you.
Concerning the treatment of water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this infirmity often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife binds the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this sickness in others than in children; and death swiftly followed for all whom I saw suffering from it: therefore it seemed to me best to omit an operation in this case.
And indeed I saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and his head grew larger every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head; and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed, is either gathered between the skin and the bone; or is gathered under the bone upon the dura mater.
And the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with one cut in width; and let the length of the incision be about two finger-breadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the shape of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head opened on every side: and the water is depressed when you compress it with your hand towards the inside, and that is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you make an incision in the middle of the head with three incisions, according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and powders. Then swab it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then untie the binding, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equal tightness. And feed the patient all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is that you consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is greater in the occiput, or in the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear to you: and incise it as much as is possible for you.
And observe that you do not incise an artery, and that a flow of blood happens, and that the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of the humor.
Concerning the incision of the arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs happen to someone in the eyes or in the chest, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment of medicines does not help: then the last treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore it is necessary, when you wish to incise them, that the patient shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the patient bind his neck with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein pulsates. And the places of both are the places which are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with a cautery over each: and incise each with a sharp spatula on both sides, the incision reaching to the bone: and let this be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, introduce the spatula under the artery, and incise it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when a vein is incised, blood flows out with a pulsating flow, which leaps inwards with a frequent leap.
But if the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers. Then mark with a cautery, and cut down to the bone.
And that which it is necessary to let out of blood, is six ounces and perhaps you let out more, or less, of it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then attend to the wound. Then if something of the sifac remains at the bone, then incise it, lest a hot abscess occur: then bind the wound with linen cloths.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic headache, or acute catarrh due to acrimonious humors and heat in the temples, or chronic headache, violent, and similar to these: and it is treated with methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we have experience in these illnesses of extracting the arteries from the temples, or cauterizing them, as we have related.
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the patient should shave the hairs which are in the temples, and should stretch the artery apparent in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and is rarely hidden except in a few men, or by the intensity of cold.
But if it is hidden from you, then let the patient bind his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water, until it appears to you in manifest appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little while ago. Then flay the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then throw a hook into it, and draw it upwards, until you draw it out of the skin, and make it escape from the tunics which are beneath it on every side.
But if the artery is small: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and that a flow of blood does not happen. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not completely ascend.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces, up to three.
But if the artery is large: then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with double thread, strong, of silk: or of alhod cords, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the consolidation of the wound, and a flow of blood happens.
Then cut the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do that in that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife-cautery: until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in the place of the same work.
And it is a better operation, unless the patient has a fever, or is of a heated complexion. Because cautery is among those things which help to end humidities. Therefore it is a better aid.
And it is necessary after the extraction of arteries that the place be filled with old cotton: and that properly made little cushions be placed upon it.
And after the separation it should be treated with dry medicines which cause flesh to grow, and with linen cloths, until it is healed.
But if meanwhile, while you are operating, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hasten to cut it off with a cautery, or to fill the place with zegi: and bind your hand until the blood is cut off. But if none of these things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood clots. And bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness ceases, and bind it as is necessary.
And of that which is lighter and easier in the extraction of an artery, is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to an almond. Nevertheless, they are of much less sharpness than two knives. Because if they are sharp, like two knives, coldness quickly affects both: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: therefore this work is better than every work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with a cautery: and place both very hot, until they arrive at the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that that which is between its extremities may be lengthened. For they do not completely consolidate with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the care of sharp, constant flow of tears from the eyes.
When the tears are constant, and their flow is from the veins which are on the exterior of the skull, and nothing of medicinal cures contributes to them with medicines, and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels a crawling on his forehead, like the crawling of ants, and his eyes are thinned, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion, and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients needed in their cure this operation, and they named it a cure with the knife.
And it is so that you should order the sick person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then incise three equidistant fissures on the forehead according to the length of the forehead: and let the length of the fissure be about two fingers, and one fissure be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the muscles, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are on the two sides: and lengthen your hand from the continuation of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the quantity of three joined fingers: and have for yourself a piece of prepared sponge, or much dried cloth, with which you will dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends, whose following is soon the form. From the incision which is from the side of the temples, up to the second incision: and scrape with it all the skin which is between the two incisions, with the aponeurosis which is above the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and introduce into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp scalpel, smooth on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh connected with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a sufficient quantity of blood has flowed, express the places of the clots of coagulated blood.
Then place in each fissure a pledget of old cotton, and place over them small cushions soaked in wine and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the ligature, and administer much infusion with warm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment and other things with which wounds are treated, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of sharp tears and catarrhs affecting the eyes from the interior of the head.
When sharp, acrid, and constant catarrhs happen to someone, and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their sight already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head, and sneezing follows: then assume from these occurrences that those materials and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the better treatment in them is this. And it is that you order the patient to shave his forehead. Then make in it one fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another somewhat across, and begin with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision reach the bone: and lengthen your hand from the temples sides, which move during mastication, until when the bone is uncovered: and you shall dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with old linen, or with linen cloths. Then bind over it with small cushions; and soak the cushions with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not happen.
And when you loosen that, and you see that the hot abscess is now diminished: then it is necessary that you rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regime which makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of colophony: and prepare from them a plaster, and administer it to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Whatever falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from those which are not added to, nor grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then instill into the ear something of perfumed oil, or sesame oil. Then manage its extraction, and make him sneeze with sneezing powder, and block the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you place around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For very often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then manage its extraction with forceps, that is, with fine tweezers: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with forceps, it is good.
And if not, then manage its extraction with a small, thin, blunt hook of few windings.
If therefore it does not come out by means of that, then make a cannula of bronze, and insert the end of the cannula into the hole of the ear deeply, and fill what is around the cannula with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for the wind except the cannula: then extract it with your strong suction.
For very often it comes out with what we have described. But if it does not come out in this way: then take a little of helkalimbat, or of birdlime, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught: and place it on the end of a probe after you have neatly wrapped a piece of cotton around it: then insert it into the hole of the ear easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then hasten to incise, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is that the phlebotomist first bleeds the patient in the cephalic vein: and draw out blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed place: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever is possible for you of instruments.
Then sew up the incision quickly and take care of it until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell, then manage its extraction.
But if it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this form.
And manage with it the excision of that kind of grain which falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it into many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a thin forceps, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is alleviated.
But in the case of water entering the ear, the patient should use sneezing with snuff first; and he should fill his ear with a little old cooked wine; and he should lie on that ear in which is the water. If therefore it comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them a little with fire, and let the patient insert one of them into the hole of his ear: and let him stand on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore let him not cease doing that with stone and stone, until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out so that you take from a pyre, or from a rush one, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burnt. Then repeat another rush, and do this many times, until all the water comes out: and draw it out with a tube as was previously described for the stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and the like, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which is apparent to the senses: then manage its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all things which are held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate: then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then stop up the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the case of a stone.
And if they do not come out with what we have said, then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in killing worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring out, whereby you pour oils and medicines into the ear, be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, with a small hole, wide above.
Therefore let that which pushes what is in the concavity of the cannula be made neatly of bronze. And if you wish, take a probe and wrap cotton around its end neatly; then pour oil or juice or whatever you wish of these medicines into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the probe with the cotton from above and press it with your hand with moderate compression, until the oil is driven into the concavity of the hearing, and the patient feels it inside; and what is poured into the ear should be warmed slightly with fire; and beware lest that which is distilled into it be very cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of obstruction happening in the ear.
Sometimes certain infants come forth from the wombs of their mothers not perforated.
And it has also happened to some men that there is an obstruction in the passage of the ear from a wound, or from flesh growing there.
And this obstruction sometimes happens in the depth of the opening of the ear, which sight does not reach; and sometimes it happens near the exterior of the opening, and sight reaches it.
Therefore that which happens in the depth of the ear, and which sight does not reach, is in most instances difficult of cure.
And in that which sight reaches, it is necessary that you place the ear of the sick person in the sun, and look into it: then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a thin spatula, of which the form is that which follows, let its extremity in which there is a certain small width, have a certain sharp part: and let the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the obstruction is from flesh grown in the passage of the ear: then hold it with a thin hook, and cut it with strong ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the obstruction is in the depth of the ear: then take a thin, smooth probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then put it into the passage of the ear.
If therefore you feel the obstruction with that which the sick person finds with difficulty of hearing, attempt its opening with ease: and beware lest you injure the nerves of the ear.
Then put into the ear a pledget according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days, until you are sure of the healing of the wound.
Or take a pledget, and dip it in rose water, and powder over it crushed vitriol, and use it.
But if you see the ear already suppurating: then it is necessary that you remove the pledget, and leave it with another pledget, which you will wrap in cerate which is made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides: then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs: then submerge a sponge, or cloths in cold water, and place it over the ear: and use the remaining treatment, which stops the flow of blood, described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of a stye on the eyelid.
Chapter VIII.
Those styes which occur on the eyelids, are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to grasp them with forceps: or with a hook, and to incise them with a spatula from their roots. Then apply crushed vitriol to the places.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a thin lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after incision. For very often they return when something remains of their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Concerning the treatment of the eyelids' hailstone.
Chapter IX.
Very often something happens in the eyelids which resembles a hailstone in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called a hailstone: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then, if it is a hailstone proceeding in it, which appears from the eyelid, and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore find a fissure on it lengthwise. Then scrape it from every side easily, until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
But if its excision is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with an incision a little, then that harms the patient in something.
But if the fissure is large, then join it with a suture: and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it, and makes flesh of it.
And if the hailstone slopes towards the interior of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the hailstone with a hook: except that you need an incision, and press it on every side.
But if the eyelid is lengthened by incision, that does no harm at all.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water: and treat the place with that which makes flesh, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of a stye on the eyelids.
Chapter X.
A stye is fat which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid: and it happens mostly to children: and it weighs down their eyes, and they get catarrh in the mornings, and they cannot look at the sunlight, because tears rush to them, and because of that you see them lying down on their faces always, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows, which an inflammation has already covered: and the prominence of the stye is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the stye between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person puts his head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare a small piece of linen in it, and make from that linen a circle according to the size which contains the stye on every side. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on every side, so that the stye gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then make an incision in the middle of that swelling with a lancet, making cuts in width, and let the incision not be larger than that which is made in bloodletting in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut the whole skin until you reach the stye; for in many conditions the stye proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore draw it out with the cloth which you have infiltrated between your fingers to the outside: and you rotate your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed: and beware lest you insert [the instrument] into the incision so that you do not reach with the lancet to the eye, and injure it.
But if the stye does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you should insert [the instrument] a little into the incision easily, until the stye comes forth. Then draw it out, as we said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the place, and bind it with lint.
And some men grind salt and place it in the hollow of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture may be dissolved.
Then treat it until it is healed.
But if it happens in a place with a hot abscess, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Concerning the methods of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye is softened, whereby it is wrinkled as it ought not to be.
Chapter XI.
When hairs grow on the eyelids in addition to the natural hairs, and grow against the grain: then they harm the eye, and cause various sicknesses, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and albugo, until it becomes the cause of destruction of the eye.
And Ataxmir of the eye is cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, as was mentioned in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or by tubes, as I shall say.
You ought to put the patient's head in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, on which is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let that be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, in which the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a probe.
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with a lancet falling from the larger lachrymal [gland] to the smaller lachrymal [gland].
Then remove the thread, and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton, or cloth: then mark above the eyelid with a cautery, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape is made according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: because it is already diversified in men.
For there is one who needs to have a large quantity cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with a lancet make an incision upon the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger lachrymal [gland] to the smaller lachrymal [gland], and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs, such as is the thickness of a ray.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay it completely. Then unite the two lips with a suture, with a needle and fine woolen thread: and wipe away the blood, and cause that which superabounds of the threads to adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain sticky things, if you wish to do that; and if not, do not care.
Then leave the suture and threads until three or four days: afterwards treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is elevated at the sealing and uniting of the wound: but it will be better afterwards.
The ancients taught this method of treatment, Ataxmir.
However, there is concern for the patient, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is also in Ataxmir: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, separated or joined together, according to this form which follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with evenness of incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger lacrimal duct, and a second thread near the smaller lacrimal duct: and bring together between your fingers the threads with evenness.
Then raise your hand with an even raising: and raise the skin over which the whole has been marked, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and take care of it, until it is healed.
But if an abscess should occur at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with cerate, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that their hairs are turned.
Therefore it is necessary that you use in it what we said in the incision: but suture for both is more skillful. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you turn back the eyelid, and cut the fissure which is within, according to what I have narrated. Then skillfully make two canes, or two thin pieces of wood: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of a spathula: and you have already incised at the extremities of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully apply each over that which is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the two canes from both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For the constricted skin will die, and will blacken, and will putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then incise it with scissors. Then treat it, until it is healed.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two cannulas, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the elevation of hairs pricking in the eye with a needle.
The operation in that is, that you take a fine needle, and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then you bring together its ends, and tie each with a very fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place in Alinxotati another fine thread under it briefly, and knot its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your bosom, and be near the sun so that the operation is illuminated; because it is a subtle, or small, operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out from above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the Alinxotati: and insert that hair into the Alinxotati; if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then draw your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the Alinxotati in the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the Alinxotati is pulled, and the hair does not come out with it: you will pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you placed in it, until the Alinxotati comes out from the upper opening, and you see it with the vision of the eye: then indeed you will pull the Alinxotati and the thread: and pull out both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either associate with it a long hair from the natural hairs, and it will be elevated with it: or leave it, until it lengthens after a time, and then elevate it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore happens from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is that you split that consolidation, and that you separate its lips, and you place in what is between them a pledget of linen, and you bind it, until it heals.
And it is not necessary that you use in its treatment things which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed use in it things which soften, such as fenugreek: and embrocate with water in which has been boiled marshmallow, linseed: and diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil: and wrap with it a pledget, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you strive with all ingenuity that it not consolidate according to the form which it was in at first.
And this indeed operation which we have spoken of, is not except a certain rectification of Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return completely according to what it was.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below, is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental is caused by a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and things similar to them.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle, in which there is double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and you make it penetrate from the left lachrymal to the right lachrymal, until the thread arrives at the two extremities of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and incise it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made from them: until, when they are joined together, their shape becomes this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts; sew it with a woolen thread.
Then treat them with what we have spoken of, with emollient medicines and linen cloths, until they are healed.
And if an abscess occurs from the fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, even according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a linen cloth, according to what we said.
And the sum of the discourse on the treatment of an abscess, when it is above, or below, is that the operation should proceed according to what is prepared in the form of an abscess.
For it itself is of many diversities in form: and a skillful craftsman prepares the means in whichever way is possible for him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or near to it, and the patient himself, according to what is suitable for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and raise it upwards: or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not be with the sharpness of cutting spatulas, but rather be somewhat blunt: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and injures the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you were scraping away that adhesion easily, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water: or distill into it from a sieve made from ziniar dissolved with water.
Then separate the eyelid and eye with a linen cloth: and place on the eye wool infused with egg white: and after the third day use a sieve, and with solidifying agents, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the prominence of the flesh of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that a nail is according to two methods. Or it is sinewy: and it is similar to hard, thin parchment. Or it is not sinewy, similar to congealed white moisture: which when iron touches it, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not fix itself in it.
And both types do not begin except from the greater tear duct, until it gradually goes until it covers the sight, and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on its incision is: that the sick person puts his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift up the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the growth with a hook of little curvature, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hair of a horse, or an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the growth: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the growth with the thread, and extend it upwards: and flay with the hair the side of the growth which follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair up to its end.
Then incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that a constant flow of tears may not result from its incision.
And sometimes the growth is extended with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it off with a smooth spatula, of which this is the form.
And beware lest the spatula touch the cornea, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some crushed salt, or a poultice made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary until it heals.
But if the nail is not fleshy, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is treated many times with medicines, and is healed as I have narrated in the division.
But if you wish to incise it, then open the eye of the patient, and take a thin, smooth spatula of weak sharpness, according to this form. And shave the nail from above carefully with subtlety. But if you see that it is rarefied and broken, and the shaving impresses upon it, then instill from your hour into the eye some sief made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt: and plug the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye contrary to this: for then leave it, and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation on it carefully until it is healed.
But the prominence of the fleshy tear duct, if it causes great harm to the eye, should be suspended with a hook, and a part of it incised: and do not complete the incision, lest the flow of tears be obstructed.
Then instill red tutty or a preparation made from verdigris into the tear duct until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the alguardenic and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes a red, composite flesh grows in the eyes of men, either superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or constricts it over the eyelids, and perhaps inverts the eyelids outwards: and it resembles a wild rose.
And the operation in this case is that the patient should place his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks suitable for it: and hold it with one forceps, or a holder.
Then incise the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And beware of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, with which you incise it, be either with a spatula, which we have described in the incision of the nail; or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to what its form will become after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed, and all the alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or distill into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye externally cotton with egg white: so that it may be secured from hot abscess.
But if some of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then leave the eye, and treat it with that which soothes an abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And similarly do with the added flesh which happens in the eye from this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid treatment of any weak eye, from natural or accidental cause, by operating with iron: and do not introduce your hand into any of these operations, until you see that the member is tolerating that operation.
Concerning the removal of a cataract from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Cataracts are red veins which are woven over the eye, and prevent the vision from its function: and weaken the eye with the passage of days.
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which is the cataract is strong, and there is in it no illness other than the cataract: then remove its cataract.
And it is so that you should instruct the patient to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be a subtle double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks joined in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with subtle scissors those veins with subtlety, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they are resolved: and beware lest you injure the eye with the ends of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and be very firm in your operation: so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye green or red vitriol: so that by its sharpness it corrodes what remains of the sebum.
But if it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for several days, until its pain is soothed: and it is secured from a hot abscess: then repeat on it according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of the [nasal] polypus.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call a polypus a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what has preceded in its description.
And if it is not healed, then there is no other remedy than to incise above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it, until the bone is revealed.
When therefore the bone is revealed, and you see corruption or blackness in it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called a rasp.
Let it be made of iron, and let its head be round like a button, which is pointed by filing, or by a graver's point with a fine point; and place it upon the corrupted part of the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers, and press your hand gently, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying and styptic medicines.
If then the place consolidates, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is stopped, and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not fester, and no accident happens to it: then know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is no remedy but to return the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this method.
And it is so that you uncover the second bone with an iron instrument or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is uncovered, then take an auger of this form, let its iron end be triangular; and let its wooden part be a pyramid tapering to a point, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself; and let it be near the lachrymal duct; and lengthen your hand from the eye a little. Then turn your hand with the auger until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it; by this, that he finds wind coming out of it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with drying styptic medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens, then the matter which was poured out outwardly returns to the nose: therefore it is easier for the patient.
Concerning the reduction of a protrusion of the eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes in its entirety, and no harm or diminution occurs in vision, then you ought to loosen the patient; then you should bleed him; afterwards you should apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye an ointment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above over a plaster with many powders.
And the method of the powders is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth of them should be doubled fourfold: make many of them: then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the eye needs: then bind over them with a hard cloth with a strong binding.
And repeat the cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if the binding is again loosened, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly: then remove the binding after a day and a night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the condition of the tunic, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and the powders, and the binding, and the cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the tunic of the eye.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the tunic of the eye, then it protrudes and comes forth outside the eyelids like a grape seed, and the appearance of the man is disfigured because of it, and it is necessary to incise it: then the operation in it is, according to what I relate: and it is, that you insert a needle into the root of the grape from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle in which there is a double thread from the lachrymal part, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then incise the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the grape around the needle: and tighten it very much.
Then withdraw the needle, and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white: and leave it, until the thread falls with the grape.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the grape is constricted from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds a large prominence.
Then it is perforated with a thin spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white humor flows out, and the eye descends, and immediately enters: then tighten it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter XXII.
This illness which is called Alcomnati is nothing but pus which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take his head with your hands from two sides, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and the light is cleared: and let the patient see things as he saw: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and pierce with it above the corneal membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in a circle, until the pus comes out.
Therefore, when it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water which descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and what is suitable for perforation in a declarative and explanatory statement; then take that same thing according to the health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the infirm one sit between your hands, squarely, opposite the light, near the sun: and bind his sound eye, and bind it tightly: then the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which is the water is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the end of the almond near the crown by the thickness of a ray in the very apparent whiteness of the smaller lachrymal eye: then push the almond with force: and you rotate with it your hand, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel that the almond has now arrived at the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almond to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and that is the crown of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil by the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the almond up to the place where is the water. Then press it downwards one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that upon which he opens his vision immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then remove it a second time, except that the almond comes out.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then draw out the almond with ease: and you roll your hand gradually.
Then dissolve in water something of clear gem salt: and wash with it the eye inside. Then place outside the eye a whole pledget, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white: and bind with it the sound eye.
But we, and those who are of our time, place upon it crushed cumin with egg white.
But if the almond does not obey you in entering the eye because of its hardness, because it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva, and do not put your whole hand in the opening: and it is only that you make a subtle way for the almond to its place.
Then introduce the almond, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a properly made bed be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and let him be forbidden all movements, and coughing: and put his food that which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain in its place until the third day.
Then unbind it in that dark room, and test his sight: and make him see things. Then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that he do this at the hour of treatment, or immediately after the incision: rather it is fitting that he avoid it: because water quickly rises through strong gazing.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then it is necessary to unbind the eye before the seventh day: and rectify it with that which soothes the abscess, until it is soothed: then restore its sight, and place on his face an oral medication by which his sight may be governed under it for a few days, while he remains in that dark room.
Then let him gradually leave the house, and converse in his rooms.
And know that in the cautery the student is not excused from vision sometimes: then indeed let him dare the operation.
And now indeed there has come to us someone from those who are from Alayrach, who said that there was made in Alayrach a perforated tube by which water is sucked.
Truly therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has made that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of kinds from the tube, so that he who is ignorant of them may stand over them.
Let them be made of bronze properly; and let their ends be triangulated with this same thinness, and a sharp end.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the care of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes various kinds of flesh grow in the nose, of which some resembles a scorpion with many legs; and of this is cancerous flesh, stony, dark in color; and of this is flesh that is soft, not dark in color.
Therefore, in that which of these fleshes is soft, not malignant, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the infirm one sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and throw a hook into that flesh; then pull it outwards, and incise what you have grasped of it with a thin, sharp spatula from one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
But if something remains of it whose excision is not possible, then shave it with one of the thin instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. But if the blood overcomes you, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is necessary: or if it is of malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it, until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, and water, or wine, shall be thrown into the nose after the incision. But if the nose is opened, and moisture proceeds from it to the palate, then know that he is now healthy.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it ought, then know that within it is flesh grown in the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then therefore it is necessary that you insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it: and place between each knot the quantity of a finger, or less: and let the sick man contrive to send one end of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made it similar to a cautery: and let him draw his breath until it reaches the cavity, and comes out over his throat.
And indeed boys often do something similar to this in schools: and it is an easy thing for one who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then take and saw the flesh with the knots which are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread: and put into the nose, after wiping away the blood, a pledget which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then, at the end of the matter, put a lead tube in the nose for a few days, until it heals.
But if it needs a drying treatment, use that.
To make a head-bath, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: let it be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its channel likewise.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head-bath should be full, open; which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish of juices and flowing substances.
Chapter 25.
Very often a polyp grows in the extremity of the nose and becomes large and increases with the days, until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that it is necessary that you cut it in the beginning of its appearance and eradicate all of it. Then place on the location either a cautery or a burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore its incision is delayed until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, dark in color, and lacking in sensation: then do not touch it with iron; for it is a cancerous abscess.
For many times I have seen one who incised such an abscess, and great harm resulted to the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that its incision is possible in its entirety, then evacuate the diseased area, and incise it without fear, without dread: and treat the place with that which dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when a discontinuity occurs in one of these cartilages, the operation benefits little, except in certain men.
Therefore, when something like that happens to someone, you must consider if it is a fresh wound with its blood: and then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the discontinuity are already separated, and each part has healed: then it is necessary to scrape away all the part from its apparent skin, until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and bind them, and powder over it dragon's blood and crushed olibanum: and put over the powders a piece of diachylon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is to bring together the discontinuity, either with needles, as we have recounted in the suturing of the belly: or bring together the discontinuity with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of knots from the lips.
Many people often have hard abscesses in their inner lips similar to grains of barley: and some of them are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn back the lip, and incise over each knot, and lift it with a hook, and incise it on every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with powdered vitriol, until the bleeding stops.
Then let it be washed with vinegar, and the place be treated with something styptic until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of proud flesh above the gum.
Often proud flesh grows above the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore it is necessary to suspend it with a hook; or to hold it with forceps, and to incise it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then place pounded alum on the place, or other styptic, drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after treatmentfor it often returnsthen incise it a second time and cauterize it; for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul crusts gather on the surfaces of the teeth, on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums; and sometimes they blacken, and turn yellow and green, until corruption from them reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
Therefore you ought to make the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth where you see crusts and things like sand, until nothing of them remains; and similarly do with the blackness, greenness, yellowness, and others, until they are clean.
If therefore that which is in them is removed from the beginning of the scraping, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another day, a second, and a third, until finally your intention is achieved in what you wish.
And know that a tooth needs scrapers of diverse forms and many shapes, according to what is prepared for your operation; because the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from below is different from the scraper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared for you, which you see.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the extraction of aching teeth.
It is necessary that you treat a tooth from its pain with all skill, and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing which supplies its place when it is eradicated: because it is a noble substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary, when the patient is compelled to eradicate it, that the aching tooth be firmly and certainly grasped by you.
For often pain deceives the patient, and he thinks that it is in the sound tooth and eradicates it: then the pain does not depart, until the diseased tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen this from the practice of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certain to you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side: then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps on it in a good position, and the head of the patient between your knees: which you have now held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it on every side easily, and attempt its movement, as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated, or corroded: then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth: and bind it with the end of a fine rod, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act skillfully with scarification around the gum on every side abundantly.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and a greater evil than its first pain returns upon the patient from it.
And beware lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness concerning its eradication, beyond what we have recounted.
For often they cause great sicknesses to happen to men, which are more difficult to break, and all its roots, or some of them, remain; or that they extract it with some of the jaw bones: as we have often testified to that.
Then let it be rinsed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
But if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it often happens), then grind some zegi and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi does not help you.
The shapes of the fine forceps with which the tooth is moved at first, should be of long extremities, short handle, thick, so that they do not bend when pressure is applied to the tooth with them: they should be like you see thick handles, until when you squeeze with them, they do not yield themselves, nor bend: short extremities, and let them be made of good iron, or of steel decently made, straight extremities: and at their ends let there be teeth, some of which enter into some, and grip with decent, strong constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made like the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When something remains behind at the eradication of a tooth which is broken, then it is necessary that you place a cautery on the place with butter for one or two days, until the place is softened: then introduce to it a small hook, or forceps: the extremities of which are similar to the mouth of a bird which is called a stork. This, however, is the shape of the forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file within: or like aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not yield to you for extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cauterize over the root, and uncover all the flesh, and introduce the instrument which is called a small lever, of which this is the shape.
Let it be short in its extremity, somewhat thick: and let it not be soaked, lest it break.
If therefore the root comes out by means of it, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments, whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first is triangular at the extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of a triangular extremity, slender.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
Also let there be help with this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this shape, of a twisted triangular extremity, in which there is some thickness, so that it is not broken: and let it not be soaked.
And know that the instruments for teeth are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and a skillful artisan, subtle in his art, finds for himself an instrument, according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because there are illnesses to which the ancients did not prescribe an instrument, on account of the diversity of their kinds.
But if a bone is broken from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or putrefies: then investigate it in its place with that which is suitable to it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with a gesti, whose form is this.
Let there be in it some gradual thickness, so that the bone may be tightened, or held with it, and not escape and move, until the bone comes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable to it.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then shave it from its putrefaction, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Concerning the sawing of teeth protruding above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, an ugly form is produced by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely, those who are for sale.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is grown after another tooth, and is not placed, or its sawing or filing is possible, then eradicate it; and if it is connected with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of untempered iron, of a very sharp extremity.
And let your incision, by which you incise it, be in many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not moved.
But if it is prominent, exposed to its filing: then file it with a file of untempered iron, of which this is the form.
Let the whole be of untempered iron, and its handle of very small points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is filed little by little in many days easily, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then smooth it, finally shave it with certain razors.
But if it is a tooth, from which a part is broken off, and injures the tongue: then it is necessary that you file it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made even: and it is smoothed, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold threads.
When a shaking and commotion happens to the front teeth from a blow or a fall: and the sick person cannot bite on what he eats so that it does not fall out: and you have treated them with styptic medicines, and the treatment has not availed: then the method is that they are tied with a gold thread: because silver turns green and rots after a few days: but gold never changes.
And let the thread be moderate in thinness and thickness according to the amount that it occupies between the teeth.
And the method of retention is that you take a thread, and insert the double of it between two healthy teeth: then weave with the two ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether it is one or more, until you arrive with the weave to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the point from which you began: and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom until it is not removed at all: and let your constriction, with which you tighten the thread, be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not escape.
Then cut the superfluous ends of the thread with scissors: and join both, and twist them together: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue: then let it be left so tight as it remains.
But if it is loosened or cut, tighten them with another thread, and it will remain firm with it for the whole time.
And this is the form of the teeth, and the figure of the retention of two healthy teeth, and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after the fall of both, and are retained as we have narrated, and remain: and this is only done by a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman.
And sometimes something is fitted from the bones of animals: and it is made like the shape of a tooth, and is placed in the place, from which the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament which happens under the tongue, is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation in it is, that you open the mouth of the sick person at his head in your lap, and lift up his tongue: then incise that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
But if in it there shall be something of hardness and knotting, and it shall be from the healing of a wound: then throw into it a hook, and split it across, until you incise the ligament, and the knotting is loosed.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you incise an artery there, and a flow of blood happens.
Then let the place of the incision be washed with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a small linen cloth, which the sick person shall hold throughout the night: so that it may not heal together a second time.
But if a flow of blood happens, then place pounded alum on the place. But if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a sluggish cautery, which is suitable for it: then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which is similar to a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function: and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person before the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, to which the sick person feels no sensation: then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it inclines towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then throw into it a hook, and incise it with a thin spatula, and free it from every part. But if blood overcomes you at the time of your operation, then place on it crushed alum until the blood is stopped: then return to your operation, until you extract it with its entirety.
Then let it be washed with vinegar and salt: and treat it with the remaining treatment suitable for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes abscesses occur between the throat glands, that is, glands which resemble the glands which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore the abscess is hard, dark in color, and with little sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is thin, then it ought to be incised.
And the procedure in it is that you consider before its operation if its warm abscess has quieted completely, or is lessened by some diminution; then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and let his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let a servant take [the instrument] between his hands, and press down his tongue with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which is thin like a knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it, then take a hook and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, except that you draw with it something of the tunics.
Then incise it with an instrument, whose shape follows. It is similar to scissors; however, let its ends be curved, so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other sharply; therefore take care that it is made of tempered iron or alfuled.
But if this instrument is not present, then incise it with a spatula, whose shape is this. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you incise one tonsil, incise the other according to this same manner of incision.
Then after the incision let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if a flow of blood happens to him, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate peels have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and things similar to them from among thestyptics, until the flow of blood is stopped: then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses are produced in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore incise them according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
