And I indeed already treated a woman from an abscess which had formed between her throat, which pulled towards blackness, little feeling, which had almost closed her throat: and the woman breathed through a narrow passage, and it had already prevented her eating, and drinking water: and she had already come to death, if she had remained one day, or two days.
And the abscess had ascended so much that it had sent out two branches, until they came out above the two openings of the nose.
Therefore I hastened, and fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion was drawn out from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the opening of the nose.
Then I did the same to that which protruded from the other opening of the nose.
Then I opened its mouth and pressed its tongue; and afterwards I fixed a hook in the abscess; then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it: therefore the woman's throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took some food.
Therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times over a long period: and the abscess kept forming in the place where I incised, until it became too long and with it that thing.
Therefore I devised, and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it stopped growing; then I went away, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
Concerning the incision of the uvular abscess.
Chapter XXXVII.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and becomes an abscess, and is long: then it is called perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the hot abscess has subsided from it, and it is thin: then it is necessary to incise it.
And that which is gathered from it, round, and has no length, and is bloody, or brown in color, or black, and has no feeling: then it is necessary to avoid its incision. For in it there is fear for the patient.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I described, from its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit before the sun: and press his tongue with an instrument, the description of which precedes: then fix a hook in the uvula, and draw it downwards: and incise it with one of the two instruments which I described for the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary not to incise from it, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise more of it, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of the two tonsils: and treat it, until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision: then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear and dread.
And the method of cauterization in it is not, except with a sharp instrument: and it is, that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have mentioned. Then take of the water which I told you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix with it some unslaked lime, and put it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the cavity of this instrument, of which this is the form.
Let the extremity of it in which the medicine is placed have a concavity, like the concavity of a spoon.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself: and let the sick person lie on his side, so that the saliva may run from his mouth from the inside of the medicine, so that nothing from it may descend to his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you hold it over the uvula for the amount of half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the biting of the medicine quieted.
And if you wish to take a pledget, and wrap it around the end of a spoon, and soak the pledget with medicine, and insert the spoon with the pledget into the cannula from above, until the pledget adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you obtain what you wish of the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it: for it itself dries and falls after three days, or four.
But if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization wipe around the uvula with a pledget soaked in butter, and clean with it what is around it of the medicine: and let it be rinsed with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
Therefore this method of treatment is healthier than incision and further removed from fear.
And sometimes the uvula is also cured with something subtler than cautery and incision: for it is cured with fumigation, in this way. Take calamint, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sage, and chamomile, and southernwood, and similar herbs: and combine all of them, or some of them in a pot, and let them be submerged with vinegar, and boil: and let the pot be luted with clay properly: and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument is placed in this way.
Let it be made of silver or bronze: and let the end, in which the pomegranate is, be introduced into the mouth of the sick person, until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is much darkened.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries.
And beware, lest you do this cure at the beginning of the arrival of the abscess. For it often adds to the abscess: and it is not fitting that you do that except at the decline of its hot abscess.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a tube, and place on its end eggshell: so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned: because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Often a bone, or fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore it is necessary that you extract what of them is apparent upon which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument toward the sun, and it is made clear to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the sick person vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps then the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the sick person swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a morsel of dry bread: or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge; and tie it on a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it arrives at the place where the splinters or bone are, and is pulled out, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be somewhat thicker than a radius, and at its end let there be a curve, which he may easily insert into his throat; and let him have his head raised upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest coughing should occur, and push the bone or splinter with it, or let the physician insert it with his hand.
But that the sick man himself insert it is better, because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing; and let him push downwards, or draw his hand upwards with the instrument, all of it according to the amount that is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the extraction of a leech.
Chapter 39.
When the leech is treated with what I have said in the division concerning treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then examine in the throat of the sick man before the sun, or in the sun, after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I have described to you.
Therefore, if your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine forceps decently.
But if it is not possible with them, then take a concave cannula, and insert it into the throat of the sick man as far as the throat: then insert into the concavity of the cannula a piece of iron heated with fire. Do this many times: and let the sick man abstain from water the whole day.
Then let him take a glass full of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it: and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the sick man with bugs, or with asafoetida with the instrument which I described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this many times, because it itself will fall.
And the method of operation in fumigation is that you take a pot in which there are live coals with fire: and the pot is covered with a cover, in the middle of which is a hole: and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then throw in the fumigation, and let the sick man put his mouth to the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has now reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick man endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore it is necessary that it come out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is drawn out from the throat, when it falls upon the sight itself.
It is similar to forceps, as you see, except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is a roughness like a file: when it grasps something, it does not let go of it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many types, according to how their recollection comes, namely one, and one in this book: and they differ in their perforation, and their incision in two ways.
One of which is from the species of the abscess itself, and that which it contains of humors.
And the second method is because of the places in which it happens in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints: and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And among abscesses there are some which it is not necessary to perforate, except after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion: and there are some which it is necessary to perforate while they are raw, and not matured according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its being prolonged until that which is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore it is a more proximate cause of the contraction of that member.
Or there is an abscess near a principal member: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either it, or through it the principal member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it immature: and it is not necessary to perforate a raw abscess, of incomplete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its end that which is not healed.
It is necessary that you know the time of perforation of an abscess which has matured according to its completion: and it is at the sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and pulsation, and acuteness of the head of the abscess: and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of more vehement prominence: and let the perforation go in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in places of muscles, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and lastly in all equal places, in which there is no reflection.
However, in those which are reflected, proceed with the perforation according to that member.
When however the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better that you delay its perforation, until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much dirt and filth, and perhaps its lips and its depth are hardened.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the breadth of the body when necessary: or according to that which the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer in small abscesses a small perforation, and in large abscesses a large perforation: or many incisions in the likeness of the magnitude of the abscess.
And there are some abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be hollowed out: and incised when it has become like a cloth, and has become in the end, that it is already dead: as that which happens in many, like boils and buboes.
And some of them are those which are cut with a three-angled incision. And of them is that which is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like the abscess of the groin.
And some of them are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered: and similar to them from among the incisions.
However, that which does not have a head, such as are mild superficial abscesses, it is necessary that you perforate it with a superficial perforation, or a flat one only.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, that you do not hurry and extract all the pus at that time: rather, extract part of it from it. Then plug the abscess until another day. Then extract some of the pus again.
Do this many times gradually, until it comes out: especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old, decrepit person: for the animal spirit is often dissolved from the body with the exit of pus: and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you clean the wound: and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply linen cloths, or old lint. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures of perforation, then it is necessary that you insert a linen cloth into each fissure, until they meet each other.
And if in the abscess you have cut away a part of the skin, or have hollowed it out around: then it is necessary that you fill it with old lint, or as when cloth is woven, or shaved linen fluff without moisture: and bind it tightly until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with what is necessary of ointment, until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after a cloth has been soaked in them: and apply it to the place of bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regime, and the treatment, and the powders, which I have recounted in many places of this our book, and from the division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you infuse small cushions with warm wine and oil: and place them on the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you apply small cushions soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and all of it cold. Until when it shall be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and clean it, and administer in its treatment that which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This therefore is what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the way of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how there is a way to its treatment chapter by chapter individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur in the skin of the head, and they are of the species of Nodes, which contain tunics, which are sacs for them: as if they themselves were the wattles of a hen.
And their kinds are many.
Of these, some are fatty; and of these, some contain a humidity similar to mud; and of these, some contain a humidity similar to barley gruel and porridge; and similar to them; and of these, some are hard and stony.
And in the incision of all, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as an artery does not oppose their incision.
And the operation in their incision is that you should explore them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains humidity, then incise it with a superficial incision according to its length: according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b. to line g. When therefore the humidity is evacuated, then scrape out the cyst which contains that humidity, and do not leave anything of it at all.
For very often it returns, if anything of it remains.
Then dip a pledget in Egyptian ointment, if it is present: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until the next day. For it will corrode what remains of the cyst.
Then apply a pledget with Egyptian ointment a second and a third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing of the cyst remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node, then incise it in this manner, the incision being cross-shaped: and pull the hooks between the lips of the incision, and scrape it from every side: and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I shall tell you afterwards.
And similarly do in the case of an abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, as we have said.
And the incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it contains little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in certain men, with which there is completely no moisture.
That is: because I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman, and I found an abscess like a solid and hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken; and if anyone were to strike with it by throwing, it would wound him.
In other abscesses, however, occurring in the head, besides those abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and near the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or place their perforation always from their lower part, so that the flow of pus may be relieved downwards.
Then treat them with what is suitable for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of strumae in the neck.
Chapter XLII.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And concerning those which occur in the neck, there are one, and many; and they are generated from one another; and every struma among them is within its own tunic, just as it is in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the types of these strumae are many. And among them are stony ones; and among them are those which contain humors; and among them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one among them which you see to be of good disposition to the touch, whose manifest color is close to that of the skin, and moves in every direction, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the jugular vein, nor to the artery, nor is deep: you must cut it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along line b to line g, and flay it from every side; and extend the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is important to be cautious, lest a vein or nerve be cut.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp: lest it slip in your hand during the incision, or disturb the patient, and you cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery, or it prevents you from operating, then place crushed alum in the wound, or some powders which staunch blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins and wants to fester.
For the blood is stanched. Therefore return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if something remains of the little pustules or other small things; therefore incise them and cleanse them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the pustule: then it is necessary that you not incise that pustule from its root; indeed it is necessary that you tie it with a strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If the whole pustule is incised, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound; and sew it up immediately, after you see that completely no excess remains.
But if you see that excess remains because of the size of the pustule, then it is necessary that you strive to incise its root and extract it: and administer the suture which we have described.
And which of the pustules contains moisture, pierce it with a superficial perforation: so that its place of maturity appears to you: and place the perforation from that which follows the lower part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer after the perforation a pledget with ointment, and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound is now cleansed: then take care of it with flesh-generating ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said this incision in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it: and this is the writing of their speech.
However, in those having synanche, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided: since no help is given by it; because all the veins and the lung are weak.
In those, however, who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or in both tonsils, when there is no illness in the windpipe, it is necessary to use incision of the epiglottis, to escape from the destruction which happens from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should incise the epiglottis between three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across, in that which is between two circles, by an amount such that the incision is in the fat, not in the cartilage: and this indeed is a suitable place for incision, since it is free of flesh, and the blood vessels are far from it.
But if the doctor is timid, then it is necessary that he extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him split the skin, until when he arrives at the windpipe: let him avoid a blood vessel, if he sees any of them.
Then let him cut the fat which we have described: and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the phlegm which comes out of it, with that which is broken, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time in which suffocation is feared is removed, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and sew it alone without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines which cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this discourse which we have narrated is this:  only when they see that one of these abscesses has already obstructed the throat of the patient, and the patient is close to death and wishes his breathing to be cut, do they proceed to cut the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe through the place of the wound with a certain respiration, and be saved from death. And for this reason they instructed that the wound be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and its malignancy is for three days, and about that. Then therefore they instructed that the wound be sewn and treated until it is healed.
And that which I saw with my own eyes is that a certain maidservant took a knife and put it into her throat, and cut with it a part of the windpipe.
And I was called to her treatment, and I found her roaring like the roaring of the dying.
I uncovered the wound therefore, and found the blood which had flowed from the wound to be little. Therefore I was confident that she had not cut a vein nor the gullet; and wind was coming forth from the wound.
Therefore I cleaned and sewed the wound, and treated it until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice: and after days she returned to a better state.
Therefore I say: that in the incision of the epiglottis there is no fear.
Concerning the incision of a throat hernia.
Chapter 44.
This abscess, called a throat hernia, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body: and it is found much in women.
And it is according to two ways: for it is either natural: or it is accidental.
In the natural, certainly there is no pus.
Accidental, however, is according to two ways: of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other way, is similar to the abscess which happens from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not fitting that presumption be made to it with iron, except in that which is small: and you prove it, and investigate it with a probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not suspended with some veins: then cut it, just as nodes are cut: and extract it with what contains it of a cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then search its whole. Then cure the place with what is necessary of treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision upon the kinds of nodes.
There are many kinds of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their kinds in the division: and it is necessary that I relate here the difference between a Node and an Exitus, since it is making doubt.
I say therefore, that when an Exitus is heat, and fever, and vehement pains, until the boiling over of superfluity is settled, and putrefaction is completed: then indeed the fever and heat are quiet.
With a Node however there is not heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it contains them in a kistis sifachin, which is its own sac, and is according to the color of the body: and its beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, and greater and smaller.
And they are according to two kinds: either fat: or they contain humidity. And the colors of humidity are many, according to what I said in the division.
It is necessary therefore, when you arrive at the cure of a Node, that you examine it, and investigate it firstly with the instrument which is called Intromissum, according to what its form will be in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of investigating abscesses, and all Nodes is, that you take this instrument, and insert it into the most humid place, which you find in the abscess: and you roll your fingers with it gradually, until you know that the instrument has now penetrated the skin. Then move your hand according to the measure of the magnitude of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum, and consider that which comes forth in its track.
If therefore a running moisture comes forth, whatever color it may be: then cut it with a superficial incision, according to what I said in other abscesses.
And if moisture does not come forth in the track of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty: therefore cut it above with a cruciform incision, according to this form, as I taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from every part with ease: and guard the cyst if you can over it, so that you extract it whole with the Node.
But if the cyst bursts during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then extract it piecemeal, until nothing of it remains.
For if a little or much of it remains, the Node returns in great measure.
But if it overcomes you, and a little remains of it: then fill the wound at your completion with some sharp corrosive powders: and bind the wound, and place upon it what sits warm on the abscess, and treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
If therefore the Node is large, then sew its two lips, and treat it with that which causes granulation.
But if there is an opposing vein, pulsating or not pulsating: then hasten, and fill the place with pounded alum, and leave it bound for two or three days, until the wound putrefies, and the boiling of the blood ceases: then return to incising what remains of the Node.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the instruments for incision and perforation.
Of these are the shapes of the intromittents, and they are of three kinds.
For of them there is a large one, and of them there is a medium one, and of them there is a small one.
Let them be made of well-tempered iron with square ends, so that they may readily enter the abscess.
And these are the shapes of the exploring instruments, and of the perforating ones: and one of them is named beris, that is, perforating; and they are of three kinds.
For of them there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating by means of them abscesses, and outlets, and fistulas, and buboes from what is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, soft, like large needles with which cushions are sewn, namely of bronze, or of brass, or iron, or silver.
And better than them are those which are made of bronze.
And they are also made from black lead, which are suitable for investigating by means of them fistulas, whose cavities have reflections, so that they are gently reflected with those reflections.
And again they are of three kinds. Because among them are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the size of the fistula and its stricture.
The shape of hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because among them are simple ones, namely those which have one curve: and they are of three kinds as you see, large, medium, and small.
And among them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And among them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and among them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and skinned: and they are of three kinds: for among them is a large, a medium, and a small one.
Their ends with which the cutting is done are sharp, the other ends are not sharp: and they are not placed thus, except that with them help is given in the skinning of nodes for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and that the sick man may be healed by them, and find some relief from the burning which he finds in the skinning of an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and are similar to a spatula with which alcohol is applied: and at the broad end is a concealed spatula point: and in it it runs inwards and outwards when you wish, as you see.
The form of spatulas which are hidden between the fingers in the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The shapes of cupping-glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of bronze or brass, round, somewhat long, as you see: and let them be thin.
And it is necessary that these kinds of cupping-glasses be with you, large and small: so that you may cut off blood with them quickly in necessity, and then when medicine is not present to you.
However, do not use them in the cutting off of blood in every place of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the thighs of the leg, and the hip, and the thighs of the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groin, and similar to them, from fleshy, moist thighs.
And sometimes small instruments are made from them, similar to the husks of nuts, according to their form: with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of the breasts of men which are similar to the breasts of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are swollen upon completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be defiled in sleep) until they are similar to the breasts of women: and they remain badly festering.
Therefore, he who abhors that, ought to cut above the breasts with a lunar incision, according to this form from line b to line g. Then flay all the fat. Afterwards fill the wound with incarnative medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the nipple declines downwards, and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it ought to be incised on its two upper sides with two incisions similar to the shape of a crescent moon, each of which is joined with the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Afterwards flay the skin which is between the two lips, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about suture and powders, which is necessary for the cure, until it is healed.
But if you do not complete the incision of what you ought to incise, because of the patient's restlessness, or because of the flow of blood: then you ought to fill the wound with a pledget soaked in corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it is healed.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess which occurs under the armpit.
Chapter XLVIII.
Those abscesses which occur under the armpit are from the hard species of Strophulae, which contain cysts, and among them are those which contain humours.
Therefore those of them which contain humours ought to be cut with a lunate incision, according to this form, from line b to line g.  When therefore all that is in the abscess of pus is evacuated, then fill it with old linen, and leave it until another day.
Then let the linen be removed, and let it be treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it is healed.
But if the abscess is of the species of the nodes of Strophulae, then incise upon it, as we said in the section on Strophulae equally.
But if the wound extends so that it emits pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we said in what precedes.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which happens because of an artery and a vein.
When an artery is broken and the skin which is above it is consolidated, then many times an abscess happens from it: and similarly it happens to a vein also, so that an abscess and swelling happen in it.
And the signs by which it is known that an abscess and swelling are because of an artery and a vein are: because, when an abscess is because of an artery, it is long, gathered deep in the body: and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking in it.
And that which is because of a vein, is round in the apparent part of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is a mistake, and especially that which is under the armpits, and groins, and neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore it is necessary that its treatment with iron be avoided, and that which of them is also in the extremities, or in the head, it is necessary that it be avoided.
Therefore, upon that which of them is from the swelling of the opening of an artery, make in the skin an incision in length. Then open the incision with hooks, afterwards the artery: and free it from the tunics, until it is uncovered.
Then insert under it a needle, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a spatula, until all the blood which is in it comes out, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment which generates pus, until the ligatures fall off: afterwards treat it with ointments suitable for it, until it is healed.
But if there is an abscess because of a section of the vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much as you are able of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be a double thread in it, until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature: according to what I told you in the ligature of a grape, on two sides with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole abscess at the incision of the needle: press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until when that which is in it comes out: then incise the excess of skin, and leave that which is tied from it.
Then place on it a pledget which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with linen cloths and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning an abscess which happens from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess happens in an artery and vein, similarly an abscess happens in nerves: which happens in them through percussion, or happens from excessive labor, and similar things: and it happens most in those places of the joints, or heel, and in every place in which joints are moved: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in most instances without pain: and when it is squeezed forcefully, the sick person feels in it something like numbness.
And it is not an abscess collected deep in the body, rather it is under the skin, and it itself moves to every part, and does not go forward, or before, nor backward to the posterior.
Therefore, what of these is in the joints, ought not to be invaded with iron. Perhaps, indeed, contraction will happen.
But in that which of them is in the head, or in the forehead, split the skin with a spatula. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps, and cut it off from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and flay it: then remove it, and close the wound with suture: and treat it, until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which happens in the belly.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies, and in the rest of their bodies,  fungi, which are called Fungalia, because of their similarity to mushrooms.
Whose root is thin, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And some of them are small; and some of them become very large.
And I myself have seen a man, and in his belly were two fungi, which were more similar to mushrooms than to other things: there was no difference between them, white, with small roots, and their lips were inverted, and they were split, and moisture always ran from them.
Therefore I cut them, and I found in one about eighteen ounces and in the other about six ounces. And the operation in cutting them is that you should consider then, if the patient is moist, and the color of the fungus is white, moist, with a thin root: then cut it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries in the fire at hand.
For very often much blood is expelled during its section: therefore hasten, if blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
But if you see the patient fearful and timid of incision with iron, then take a properly made lead thread, and bind the fungus with it: the method of which is as we have said, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead constriction. Therefore do not cease to tighten the lead, as often as it presses on the fungus, until it is cut off, and falls off by itself without effort.
But if the fungus is of a thick root, then in its incision there is something of fear and dread; especially that which is in the abdomen.
Nevertheless, it is possible that you incise half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then care for it, until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to incise a fungus which is brown in color, of little sensation, foul in appearance: for it is a cancerous abscess; and I will come to the remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia occurs from many causes. Or from fissures of the sheath which is over the belly; and from it protrudes fat or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood which flows from a vein or from an artery, as has been stated before. Or from wind constricted there.
But if it is because of a fissure of the sheath and the protrusion of fat, then its color is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft, without pain; and it appears in various places. And if it is because of the protrusion of the intestine, then its location, with what we have narrated, is of a more noticeable variety. Therefore, when you press it with your fingers, it is hidden; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it is greatly magnified at the entrance of the bath, and causes great labor.
But if it is because of moisture, it is soft; then it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor constricted, nor is anything added.
But if it is because of blood, then with these signs a swelling appears tending towards blackness.
If however it is because of newly formed flesh, then it is a serious, large, hard swelling, and is fixed to a certain size.
But if it is because of wind, then its touch is soft.
And the procedure in that is that you consider then if the protrusion of the navel is because of blood from an artery or vein, or wind; then you must be forbidden from its treatment. For in that there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter in which I discussed swellings because of an artery and a vein.
But if the protrusion of the navel is because of intestine or hernia, then you must instruct the patient to hold his breath and stand upright and straight. Then mark with a cautery around the whole navel. Afterwards instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then surround with a wide spatula around the navel above the place which you marked with the cautery. Then extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie it around firmly with thread, or with a strong silken cord; and let the knot of the ligature be secure.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligature. If therefore you find it already seized by the ligature, then pull on the ligature, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine into the interior of the abdomen; and if you find a polyp, then seize it with a hook, and incise its superfluity.
But if an artery or vein is opposed to you, then surround it extensively. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and thread them with two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference which you made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then constrict the abscess in four places over the needles: and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place, until the extended flesh putrefies: and falls off by itself; or incise it when it becomes thin and putrefies.
Then treat it with what is necessary of ointments, until it is healed.
But if the prominence of the umbilicus is from raw flesh in it, or from moisture: then it is necessary that you hollow out the abscess, as we said. And extract the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with that which solidifies the place.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of cancer, and how the way to its cure is with medicines: and caution should be exercised with its treatment with iron, so that it is not ulcerated: and we have spoken of cancer generated in the womb, and caution in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible: such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar to them, of the members located for extracting all of it: especially when it is small in the beginning.
When however it is old, and is large, it is not fitting that you approach it.
For I myself have not been able to cure anyone of it: nor have I seen another before me who has arrived at that.
And the operation in it, when it is located as we have said, is that you precede, and you loosen the diseased person from black bile sometimes. Then phlebotomize him if you find him with manifest repletion.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation, in which he is positioned with the operation.
Then throw hooks suitable for it into the cancer. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood run, and do not cut it off quickly, but rather press the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with whatever is possible there of instruments.
But if the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery or vein opposes you, then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of dropsy.
Chapter 54. We have already narrated in the division the types of dropsy, and how the collection of water occurs, and the signs of each type, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron is only a type of ascites alone, and is not approached with iron in the case of tympanites, nor in the case of hyposarca completely, for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this type of dropsy, tympanites, with medicines, and your treatment has not helped, then consider. For if the patient has already suffered weakness, or there is another illness with him besides dropsy, such as if he has a cough, or looseness of the bowels, and things similar to those, then beware lest you treat him with iron, because there is fear of that.
But if you see a patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man, then the method of operation in him is that you raise the standing patient between your hands, and a helper behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a spiny spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp parts, long, the sharp end like a spatula. However, let there be a little shortness in it, so that you do not pass with it in the operation as far as the intestine, and injure it.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen the incision from the navel by the amount of three fingers, downwards in a straight line above the pubis.
But if the generation of dropsy is due to illness of the liver: then let your incision be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not run to that weak place.
Then pierce the whole skin with an instrument. Then insert into that incision, and raise your hand with a spatula between the skin and the fat, as if you are flaying it: and let the amount that is flayed be the amount of a fingernail, or thereabouts.
Then pierce the fat until the spatula reaches the empty place: and that is the place of the water. Then withdraw it, and insert into the opening an instrument, of which this is the form. And it is similar to a tube: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished: which at its lower part has a small opening, and on its sides are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other: and its end is incised, according to the manner of the incision of a reed, as you see: at its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it immediately descends upon the instrument, therefore evacuate half a quantity of water in an hour.
Because if you evacuate more than is necessary, in an hour perhaps the sick man dies through the resolution of his animal spirit: or a syncope happens to him which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of his strength, and according to what the patient's conditions signify to you from the strength of his pulse, and from the goodness of it.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is immediately retained because of the skin which retains the hole which is over the sifac: about which I have narrated, so that you may pierce it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the next day, if you see the patient tolerate it: and also draw out a small quantity of water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until nothing remains of the water except a little.
But if you are worried about the patient, and you leave a large amount of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and much sweating in a bath and sun, and let him suffer from thirst: and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed: and cauterize over the stomach, liver, and spleen, after the extraction of water: according to what I have narrated in what precedes.
Concerning the care of infants who are born with their urinary places not perforated.
Chapter 55.
When one of the infants comes forth from the belly of its mother, and the opening of its penis is not perforated, or the opening is narrow, or not in its proper place.
Therefore it is necessary to help with its perforation immediately when it is born, with a very fine spatula, according to this method.
Then place a thin nail of lead in the opening and bind it: and keep it for three or four days.
When therefore he wishes to urinate, let it be removed, and he will urinate. Then replace it.
And if you do not put lead there, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
But he whose opening is narrow, is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
But those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, are injured. That is because, of those who are born, the opening is at the end of the penis; wherefore he cannot urinate forwards, until he lifts his penis upwards with his hand. Nor is there generation for him, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line: and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation in this case is that the patient is laid on his back. Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a strong extension, and incise the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp instrument, or a sharp spatula: just as you would incise a reed, or as you shave something, so that the middle of it comes to resemble the head of the penis: and so that the opening falls in the middle as is necessary.
And beware in your operation of a flow of blood. For that often happens. Therefore apply to it that which stops blood, and care for the wound, until it is healed.
Concerning Albucasis, what happens in the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 56.
These things often happen to the penis according to Albucasis, and it is a protrusion of foul flesh.
And of it, one is malignant, and another is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a thin hook, and you should cut it away until you cleanse it entirely. Then place upon it a pledget soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterward with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
But if the Albucasis is malignant, of foul color, then it is necessary that you apply a cautery to it after its incision and shaving.
But if the Albucasis is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and part of the Albucasis is between the foreskin and part of it outside: then it is necessary that you remove the Albucasis which is inside first, until when it is consolidated, then treat the outside: because when you treat it at the same time, the foreskin is not secured against being perforated.
And now blackness and corruption also happen in the testicles and foreskin. Therefore, it is necessary that you hollow out all that which is already blackened, and that which wants to be corrupted, or is already corrupted.
Then spread over it afterwards with honey which is already mixed with the flour of pomegranate rinds and crushed, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
But if a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is helpful for two conditions at the same time, namely for the flow of blood, and a wound when it is corrupted.
But if the head of the penis is corroded, and the whole thing is destroyed in an hour: then it is necessary that you insert into the meatus of the penis a small tube of lead, so that the sick person may urinate over it.
In the adhesion, however, of the foreskin with the head of the penis, and this adhesion indeed does not happen except in one whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon it: and sometimes adhesion happens because of a wound, or abscess: therefore it is necessary that you scrape it with a blunt spatula, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
But if it is difficult to distinguish them exquisitely, then it is necessary that you scrape something from the head of the penis, and from the foreskin. That is because when the foreskin is thin, perhaps it is perforated because of its thinness quickly.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then it should be treated with astringent wine, until it is consolidated.
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of infants, and the cure of an error happening to them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is from our operation, our will, and its use is properly in boys, it is necessary that we mark in it a better operation, and a lighter way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because they do not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
That is, because I have found the majority of artisans and phlebotomists use cleansing, circumcising with a razor and with scissors; and they use cautery and binding with thread, and incision with a nail.
And indeed I have already tested all these methods, and I have found none better than cleansing with scissors and binding with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the skin of the foreskin has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper skin is incised, and the lower layer remains; therefore it is forced to a further incision to cut the other, and future pain. And with the cleansing which is done by cautery, that is, a finger-like instrument which suturers use, the incision of the extremity of the virile member is not secured: because perhaps it enters into its opening.
Moreover, in cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron escapes, and corrupts your operation: and indeed a child's skin is short by nature, and many times they are born such that they do not need cleansing: and I have already seen that.
However, cleansing with scissors and ligature with thread revealed a good experiment to me: because scissors are proportional in incision: therefore, because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they incise according to one operation in one time: and a compression of similar figure is made, containing the skin of the penis from every part, with which no error falls at all.
And the proper method of operation is that you cause the boy to be aware, especially if he is among those who understand a little, that you are not doing anything except tying a thread around his penis: and you leave him until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way that is possible for you, from that which his understanding receives. Then make him stand between your hands in a prepared posture, and let him not be sitting: and hide the scissors in your sleeve, or under your foot, lest the boy see them at all, nor any of the instruments.
Then insert your hand to his penis, and inflate the skin, and pull it upwards until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it from the filth that is gathered upon it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a second ligature a little lower down. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with a good hold, and incise between the two ligatures. Then quickly raise the skin upwards, and pull out the head of the rod. Then allow the blood to flow gradually (for this is better) and put powder on top: and over the powder a cloth in which the yolk of a hard-boiled egg has been spread, beaten with good fresh rose oil: and leave it on until the next day: and treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Now the shape of the forceps for cleaning is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and let them meet: lest a nail splinter protrude from them; and let the length of the two sharp parts be equal to the length of the two handles.
The error which occurs in cleaning is that perhaps the whole inner skin, or a part of it, escapes at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend it with your nail before the suppuration of the place, and cut it evenly.
But if you cannot hold it with your nail, then draw it out with a hook, and cut it.
But if three days pass, and what is under the rod remains swollen and suppurated, then leave it until the warm abscess settles, and then flay it easily and incise it as prepared: and beware of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore treat it with that which consolidates the wound from the powders which we have described in the treatise on powders: and if it is incised through the skin beyond measure, and extends to the upper parts, that also will not harm it.
Therefore treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the extraction of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or a growth of flesh, and similar things from those which we have mentioned in the divisions of treatment.
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is now in the neck of the bladder: then it is necessary that the patient be bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the patient hold his breath as much as possible. Therefore, the neck of the bladder is forced with the stone toward the posterior, wherefore the urine is released.
But if it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe for the patient: then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave like the quill of a birds feather in the slenderness of a ray, long in the likeness of one and a half palms: to which there is a slender tube at its head.
And the method of the urines exit through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie wool, or cotton, at its extremity with a good knot: and introduce the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe: and cut with scissors if anything of the wool is superfluous, truly introduce it into the tube forcefully, like an auger.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and egg white: and make the patient sit upon a seat, and moisten the bladder, and its neck with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the neck easily, until it reaches the root of the neck.
Then double it upwards from the part of the navel.
Then push the syringe inside it, until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels it has now reached the empty thing: and this is not done in this order, except because the passage, into which it goes, is one in which there is tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the urine will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and make the urine run through the urinary passage: and repeat the syringe, and do not cease doing that, until the bladder is emptied, and the patient finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood is congealed in it, or pus is constricted in it, and it is necessary to instill waters and medicines into it: then let it be done with an instrument which is called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which impels, should be according to the quantity which fills it without addition, until when something is drawn with it, the humidities are drawn out: and when it is impelled, it is expelled therewith afar: according to what the clepsydra does, by which naphtha is projected, that is, Greek fire, in the battles of Mars.
Therefore when you wish to project humidities into the bladder, insert the end of the clepsydra into the humidity, and draw with it that which impels to the higher parts, for the humidity is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then insert its extremity into the tube, according to what we have related in the syringe. Then impel the humidity with that which impels it; for then the humidity arrives at the bladder immediately, so that the infirm person feels it.
The form of a slender clyster by which the bladder is clysterized.
Let it be made of silver or brass: its head should be higher, similar to a small alembic, and below it a sinus between which the ligature falls.
Then take a ram's bladder and put into it the humidity with which you wish to clysterize the bladder. Then tie it between the two sinuses with a strong ligature with double thread, and warm that humidity a little over the fire.
Then insert the end of the clyster into the rod, afterwards squeeze your hand over the bladder with the humidity with a fitting pressure, until the patient feels that the humidity has already arrived at the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and perforate it all around its circumference. Then insert a strong thread into those holes, as they are rolled up. Then gather the circle, as an *alsofratu* is gathered; after you put in it what you wish of humidities, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder by squeezing with your hand, until the humidity reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
Concerning the extraction of a stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the kinds of stone, and its cure with medicines: and I have spoken of the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of all of it: and I have spoken of the stone in which operation with the hand and incision falls: and it is a stone generated in the bladder properly: and that which is fixed in the urethra: and I am narrating that with manifest exposition and abbreviation.
Therefore I say that a stone generated in the bladder most often happens to boys.
And among its signs is that urine exits from the bladder similar to water in its thinness: and white sand appears in it, and the sick person rubs his penis, and labors with it, and often hangs down. Then he is raised up, and the anus comes out with it in many of them.
And the healing of children is easy, until they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in old men. But in young men it is moderate, in that which is between that: and of him whose stone is larger, the cure is easier: and if small, the care of it is the opposite.
Therefore, when we come to the cure, it is necessary first that we cleanse the patient with a clyster, which draws out all the dung that is in his intestines: for it sometimes prevents the finding of the stone on examination.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up, and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or rise from a high place sometimes.
Then make him sit prepared between your hands, and let his hands be under his hips, so that the whole bladder may slope downwards.
Then examine him, and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel the stone in the space, then hasten immediately with the incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe the index finger with oil of the left hand, if the patient is a boy: or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth: and insert it into his anus, and search for the stone, until it stands under your finger: and gradually turn it towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and push it outwards towards the place, of which you wish to make an incision: and order the assistant to press the bladder with his hand, and order another assistant to draw up the testicles with his right hand: and with his other hand to draw away the skin which is under the testicles in the part from the place where the incision is.
Then insert the cutting spatula, whose shape is this. And cut in that which is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock: and let the incision be upon the stone, and your finger be in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision be wide externally, and narrow internally, according to the quantity that the exit of the stone from it may not be greater.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it comes out without difficulty.
And know that of a stone there are angles and edges, wherefore its exit is made difficult on account of that.
And from that which is smooth, similar to a gland, and round: and its exit is made easy.
Therefore to that which has angles and edges, add to the fissure.
If therefore it does not exit thus, then it is necessary that you operate on it: or bind it with a suitably made bandage, whose end is like the moon, which binds the stone, and does not slip from it: or that you insert under it a slender instrument, of curved end.
But if you cannot operate on it, then enlarge the opening a little. But if something of the blood overcomes you, cut it away with a scalpel.
But if the stone is more than one, then push the largest one first towards the opening of the bladder. Then incise upon it. Afterwards push the small one after that; and similarly do if there are more than two.
But if it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut upon it with a large incision. Because one of two things happens to the patient, either that he dies, or that continuous dribbling of urine occurs to him; because the place does not completely heal.
But manage its expulsion until it comes out; or work on its fracture with forceps until you extract it piecemeal.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with incense, and aloes, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place upon it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that the hot abscess may settle.
Then lay the patient on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you have loosened it, bathe the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the wound, and corrosions, and things like that: as if blood is congealed in the bladder, and urine is prevented from it: and this is known from the exit of blood with urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound, and remove that blood. For if it remains, it provokes corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt: and apply to every species with that which is suitable from the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the hours of the entire treatment to bind the two hips, and bring them together, because of the medicines which are placed upon the place.
But if the stone is small, and is in the passage of the penis, and is fixed in it, and the exit of urine is prevented: then treat it with what I relate before you arrive at the incision. For many times you will be excused from incision by this treatment, for I have already proved it.
And it is so that you take a perforator of iron, whose form is this, a triangular end, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and tie with it the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the perforating iron into the penis easily, until the perforator arrives at the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand in the stone gradually: and try its perforation, until you make it penetrate on the other side.
For the urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the penis. For it is broken, and comes out with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this treatment is not prepared for you, because of something which prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the penis itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards loosen the ligature, and clean the congealed blood which is in the wound.
And the ligature of the thread under the stone is not necessary, except that it not return to the bladder: and the other ligature above, except that when the thread is loosened after the exit of the stone, the skin returns to its place, and covers the wound: and because of that it is necessary for you when you tie the upper thread, that you lift the skin upwards, that it return at your completion, and cover the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a woman's stone.
A stone is rarely produced in women.
But if a stone should happen to one of them, then the cure is difficult, and is forbidden because of many ways.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who will uncover herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or has a husband.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well-skilled in this art, especially manual operation.
And the fourth is the distance from the location of the stone: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and in that there is fear.
But if necessity compels to it, then it is necessary that you take a female physician, well-skilled: and few are found.
If however you deprive her of that, then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a well-taught midwife skilled in women's matters, or a woman who understands some part of this art: therefore make her present, and especially instruct her to do all that you order her concerning the examination of the stone.
First, it is that she should look if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, let her compress it under her finger, and then instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the patient, and to search for the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder to the lower parts with the utmost of her strength, until she arrives with it to the root of the thigh.
Then let her cut upon it at the juncture of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, on whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and let her finger not be removed from the stone, compressing under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then let her insert a surgical instrument into that incision.
Then let her insert a surgical instrument into that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then let her add to the incision according to a proportionate amount so that the stone exits from it.
And know that the types of stones are many. For among these there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Know therefore its types, so that it may be signified to you by this concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add in the place pounded alum, and hold it one hour, until the bleeding is stopped.
Then return to your operation, until the stone comes out: and do so, that you prepare with you of the instruments, which I mentioned in the extraction of the stone in men, so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery which has been cut: then place powder on the place, and bind it with suitable bandages: and do not change it: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. Perhaps therefore the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
Therefore, when the sharpness of the blood has settled down after days, and the place has become putrid, then return to your operation, until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision for watery hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but a collection of moisture in the white fascia, which is under the skin of the scrotum containing the testicle; and it is called fascia.
And sometimes it is in its own tunic, which nature has prepared for it in part of the testicle, until it is putrefied: which is another testicle, and is the skin of the testicle between the white fascia, which we have mentioned; and this does not happen except rarely.
And the generation of this hernia indeed, is from weakness which happens to the testicles: wherefore this matter is poured out to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture is having many colors. For either its color is inclined to yellowness, or it is bloody red, or fecal, black, or it is watery white, and this is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac which we have spoken of, then the abscess is round in length somewhat like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it on every side.
And if the moisture is in its own tunic, then the abscess is round with a part of an egg, and because of this a man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
When therefore you wish knowledge of the color of the moisture, then test the color of the moisture in the abscess with the introduced square, whose form preceded. According to that therefore which comes forth in the track of the introduced, judge concerning that which is between it.
When therefore we come to cure with iron, then it is necessary that you order the sick man, that he be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid on his back on something a little high, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit you on his left, and order the assistant, that he sit on his right, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the flank of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles in the middle lengthwise, even almost to the groin: and let the incision be made according to the straightness of a line equidistant which divides the skin of the testicles, until it arrives at the white fascia enclosing: and flay it and observe, or beware of this, that you cut it: and let your flaying with which you flay it, be from the part which hangs more with the egg: and subtly explore with the flaying according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then perforate the fascia full of water with a wide perforation, and draw out all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the fascia to the upper parts, and do not touch the skin of the testicles enclosing: and incise the fascia however it is possible for you to incise it, either with its totality, or bit by bit, especially its thin side. For if you do not exquisitely search, in its incision there is no security, lest the water return.
But if the egg proceeds outwardly from its skin during the time of your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the fascia, reduce it. Then bring together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with a suture.
Then care for it like the other wounds, until it is healed.
But if you find the egg corrupted by blackness or something else, then it is necessary that you tie off the vessels which are the suspensory, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut away the testicle with the suspensory, and remove the egg; afterwards care for it with what we have said.
If however water has collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut the other part in the same way as you did the other equally.
And if it seems to you that one operation is sufficient, then do it: then wipe away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil: and place outside another wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles, and the scrotum: and place on top folded cloths, and these are cushions: and bind them on top with a bandage which has six [layers] according to this form.
Then two cloths are taken, and sewn, and filled with crimson wool, according to this likeness: and let the ends which press be made of tow, or moist wool: and let them be gathered with a bandage in the lumbar region of the breeches: and one end should reach above the anus to the back, and the second should reach to the part of the groin: and two ends below over the two hips, and two other ends over the two groins, and the whole bandage should be gathered in the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is medically treated with a cautery in place of incision with iron: and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery, and cut with it the skin of the testicles: and it is heated, as we have described: until when the white pus containing water is revealed: then take a cautery, according to this form.
It is similar to a Greek letter.
Then perforate that pus with it, and it is heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the pus with hooks, and flay it with a thin, heated cautery, and incise it as much as possible, until you eradicate all of it.
And beware of the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than the operation with incision.
