If, however, a hot abscess, or blood flow, or something else opposes you during the incision, or during the cauterization: then you must treat what opposes you with the treatment that suits it, as was previously explained.
But if the patient is timid, and does not expect this operation because of its horror: then administer perforation as I relate it: and it is.
When it is confirmed to you by what we have said about the signs, that the branch is watery: then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a broad spatula: and place the perforation according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a pledget over the place, and bind it, and leave it so that the remaining water may run out: then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is opposition to the egress of water at the time when you perforate it: and this is only because a white membrane makes opposition in the orifice of the wound, and prevents the exit of the water: then it is necessary that you introduce a feather into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of dropsical patients is extracted, the form of which has preceded: or add a little to the operation of the wound.
And know that the water returns and gathers after six months, or thereabouts. When therefore it gathers, then let it be perforated in the same way, and the sick person will pass his days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy fistulas.
Know that in the incision upon this fistula, there is fear leading to destruction in many conditions. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is to be cured from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say, therefore, that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed: and this happens from many causes, namely.
Either from an acute or acrid superfluity which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it happens from a knotting and swelling of the arteries, as its mention has already preceded: or from a swelling of the veins.
Therefore, that which is from a swelling of the arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary that you touch it completely.
That which happens from a swelling of the veins, however, does not disperse anything from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section is made upon this type and upon the remaining flesh: and it is so that you cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it with the white tunic; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and ligate the vessels; and incise the spermatic cord after you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
If therefore the testicle is consolidated with those born fleshy parts, then it is necessary that you draw out the testicle, and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between something of the tunic, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion, and incise it with a round incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you incise all of it, and extract the testicle, as we said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine: then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas, which is with a vine.
A vine is a swelling wrapped in a certain wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with softening of the testicles: and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, there is fear, because of that which preceded its recollection.
Truly it is necessary that we speak of the operation in it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels which are near the penis: and let another assistant hold them, and extend them with a strong extension.
Then incise with a broad, sharp spatula with a transverse incision in the direction of the vessels, until the vessels are uncovered.
Then flay from every side, as we told you in the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Afterwards fix in them a needle in which there is a double thread, and tie it in the first places where the veins meet: and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in the middle with a straight incision according to the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from foul, corrupt humours.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which pus is generated, so that the vessels which nourish one testicle may fall away. For there is no harm through it.
But if a vein affects all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed with the vessels: lest the egg be deprived of nourishment, because of the incision, and waste away, or dry up, and there be no benefit with them.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal fistula.
The outcome of this fistula is from a section which happens in the sifach extended over the belly in the groin region from the mirach of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from the sifach or its extension.
And these two types happen from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a shout, or lifting a heavy thing, and similar things.
And the sign, when it is from the extension of the sifach. Is that it happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the deep; therefore, because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes a vehement pain suddenly from its beginning: and the abscess is diverse, appearing under the nearby skin: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the outside of the sifach.
And sometimes the zirbum exits with the intestine: and this is named intestinal Ramex, and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes dung runs into the intestine, and is retained there: and with it is the destruction of the infirm; because it causes a bad pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the cure of the species of this illness with iron there is fear: wherefore it is necessary that the case be avoided in it.
And the method of operation is that you order the infirm to reduce the intestine with his hand to the inside of his cavity, if it obeys return. Then he will be laid supine upon his neck between your hands: and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin to the upper parts, and cut the whole skin of the testicles lengthwise. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them: and let the incision be of such a size that, if possible, the egg may come out of it.
Then flay the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphak is uncovered, hard on every side: then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and absolutely loosen with it the adhesion which is in the posterior part of the egg.
Then move with the right hand to the interior the skin of the testicles: and with this extend the white siphak upwards, with the left hand: and raise the egg with the siphak to the part of the incision: and instruct the assistant to extend the egg upwards: and you loosen the adhesion which is behind, the solution being complete.
And search with your fingers, that there may be nothing there of the intestine involved in the hard white siphak. But if you find something of it, then push it down to the belly.
Then take a needle, in which is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads: and insert it at the last siphak, that is, of the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision: then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some with a figure which is in the manner of a cross: and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles, with a strong ligature, until nothing from the vessels which nourish them can send anything to them: so that a hot abscess may not happen thence. And place again a second ligature outside the first ligature, less than two fingers distant from it. And after these two ligatures leave from the siphak, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity of the size of a finger: and incise all the rest according to the roundness, and remove from it the testicle.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles an incision, from which blood and pus flow: as we have related in what has preceded.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and put it in the wound: and apply the ligature which we have described. And sometimes cauterize the white siphak which we mentioned, after its incision fearing the flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be moistened with warm water: and put on it that which we have said, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Concerning a sucking cupping-glass.
In this cupping-glass I have not seen anyone who dared to operate on it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have narrated in the cupping-glass which is with a reed. That is, to tie the vessels after the incision on it with ease below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat it with that which opens it, until the vessels fall away: and treat the wound, as we have said, until it heals.
Chapter 67.
Concerning the rupture which happens in the groins.
When a rupture happens in the groins, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends, it is a little, and returns at all hours: but if time is prolonged with it, a fissure is added in the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And this happens from the extension of the sifach, which is in the groins, as we have said. That is, because the sifach is extended; then it softens, and the place suppurates, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its account preceded.
And sometimes it is treated with iron, in this way: and it is, that the sick man lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the place of the suppurating and protruding rupture, with a cut across, to the amount of three fingers.
Then constrict the alsifak and what are under the skin, until when the white sifach is revealed, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a radius, and place it on the protruding place of the sifach, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew two prominent places on the end of the radius from the sifach, and connect one of them with the other with a suture; then withdraw the end of the radius.
And do not cut the alsifach completely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I taught you in the treatment of intestinal fistulas.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. Therefore, when you cut the threads, clean it and restore the wound until it heals.
For the sisach is contracted, and no prominence is added to the rupture.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, on account of which the flesh which is between them is softened, and its appearance is made foul.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked in its cure, that he make the sick man lie on his back, and incise all the skin which is softened upon the natural skin.
Then let him bring together the two lips with a suture: and if you wish, first sew the superfluity of the softened skin with three sutures, or four, and strengthen the suture.
Then incise between the sutures.
Then treat it with the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed, and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law: and because of that it would be permitted to me not to mention it in this my book: and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons.
One of which is that it should be in the knowledge of the physician when he is questioned about it: and that he should know the treatment of him to whom it happens.
And the other reason is that we very often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done in two ways. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and incision.
Now in that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is that we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles are softened and relaxed and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and do not appear to the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut over each testicle with one incision until both testicles emerge; then incise them after you have skinned each, and do not leave on them of the sifak anything except the thin sifak which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires coitus.
Then take care of the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the care of a hermaphrodite in two ways.
A hermaphrodite from males is according to two ways. One of them is because there appears in him that which follows the space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a shape like the vulva of a woman, in which there is hair: and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one type, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubis like very small male testicles, projecting outwards: of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three types, two from men, and one type from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them disappears. Then treat them with the treatment of other wounds.
In the second type of men, however, from which urine emerges, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the frenulum, and the projecting flesh in the vulvas of women.
The frenulum is perhaps added to a natural thing, until it is defiled and its appearance becomes foul; and sometimes it is magnified in some women so much that it is spread out like in men, and reaches to coitus.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold the excess of the frenulum with the hand, or with a hook, and incise: and do not go to the extreme in the incision, especially in the depth of the root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds, until it is healed.
The flesh, moreover, which arises is flesh which is born in the orifice of the womb, until it fills it; and perhaps it comes out outwardly according to the likeness of a tail; and on account of this some first name the illness "tailed". Therefore it is necessary that we incise it just as we incise the frenulum, and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of the imperforate vulva of a woman.
Chapter 72.
Alratica is when the vulva of a woman is imperforate, or has a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental [imperforation] is from a preceding illness; and this indeed happens either from added, newly grown flesh, or from a thin membrane, or a thick one; and it occurs either deep within the uterus, or on its sides, or in its upper or lower part; and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps prevents menstruation.
And its recognition is made by the examination of a midwife, if the obstruction is apparent and near.
But if it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is from a thin membrane, and is near the two labia, then hurry and break it.
And it is [advisable] to place small cushions on the two labia.
Then place the index fingers of your two hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then stretch the two labia forcefully until the thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it on the area; and let the woman coitus every day, so that the area does not heal in another way.
But if the membrane is thick, then it is necessary to cut it with a broad spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is from a growth of flesh, then suspend it with hooks and incise it; and have with you medicines that stanch the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense combined with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, keep it in place for several days: and administer a tent of dry linen, then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And sometimes a birth of other flesh occurs in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be incised in this way: provided it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, should never be treated with iron.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and Bothor rubea in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the orifices of the veins, until much blood flows from them constantly.
When therefore hemorrhoids become old, they become athehelil: and I have already described in the division their species, and their signs: and I will here say which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses: then there is no treatment for them with iron.
But those of them which are in the orifice of the womb upon which the senses fall, are those which are cured.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the athehelil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise them from their roots.
Then pulverize upon them at the flow of blood from one of the incisive powders without mastication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar things to them.
Then let him push her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards, place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it upon her back.
If therefore blood is drawn off with it, it is good: and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and breasts, without scarification.
Then take wool and immerse it in the juice of tarthith, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of the branches of alaulat, that is, aglanter, with Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When therefore the abscess is quiet, then treat the place with ointments until it is healed.
Albarth however is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of rough appearance.
It is necessary therefore that you incise what appears of it, according to what I said in thelil equally: and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIIII.
Concerning the perforation of the exit which happens in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is, herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulae, and hemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And indeed I have already described all these illnesses and their kinds, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I describe in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is of the abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with iron.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it redden: if sensation falls upon it, then do not hasten with its perforation: and treat it with that which aids in maturation, until when the acuteness of the symptoms ceases.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat which slopes two seats, and she is reclined, or she is laid upon her back, and she draws her feet to the lower part of her belly: and her hips are spread, and she puts her arms under her knees, and she binds each with a suitable ligature to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with a probe the depth of the woman's womb: so that that which enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb, and harms the weak woman.
But if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions on the covering of the depth of the womb: so that the entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the womb is prevented: and it is necessary that she places the revolving part, which runs in the instrument, from the upper side: and that she holds the instrument, and that the assistant revolves the revolving part, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by hand, and is smooth and soft: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a linen cloth submerged in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some stypticity: and place the linen cloth, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the pubis, clean wool submerged in water, in which mallows have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with an ointment which is suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is clysterized with water and honey: then with water in which the root of lily, or long birthwort has been boiled: then it returns to the ointment.
But if the wound is hidden internally in the womb, then it is necessary that you abstain from its treatment with iron, rather treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter 77.
On the teaching of obstetricians, how live embryos, having emerged according to a non-natural form, are to be treated.
It is necessary for the obstetrician to know first the form of natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is that when you see a woman pressed downwards, and she desires to smell the air, and that which is upon her of the birth is lightened, and the exit of the embryo is hastened: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is head first, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is fitting that her belly be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities with a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this birth, is unnatural, and not praiseworthy.
Because sometimes the fetus exits on its feet, and its body exits before its head, and its feet, and one of its hands, or one of its feet: or its head and its hands exit: or its belly exits: and perhaps it is turned over on its neck, and similar things from unpraiseworthy forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, skilled in all these things, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every form of them, and what its nature is, and it is signified by that, and one should stand upon it.
When the fetus exits head first in its natural way, and it becomes strenuous for the woman, and the birth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her: and vaporize her vulva with a decoction of fenugreek, and with warm oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and let her break the secundine with it, and cut it with her nail, until that which is in it of moisture runs out: and press the woman's belly, until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then instruct her after the enema, that she be compressed; and make her sneeze with a confection, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will exit immediately.
But if two hands of the fetus come out first, then it is necessary that you reduce them with ease gradually. But if it does not obey in entry, then place the woman upon a seat, and raise her feet upward: then shake the seat upon the ground: and let the woman be held, so that she does not fall upon shaking.
If therefore the hands do not come out, and the fetus dies: then incise them, and extract the remainder of the fetus: and tie its hand with a cloth, and extract it evenly: for it will come out.
The exit of the embryo upon its feet. When the fetus comes out upon its feet, then it is necessary that you push them upward: then turn the fetus gradually: afterward straighten it.
Then let him take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they come out as far as the hips, then instruct him that it be compressed, and make her sneeze with sneezing powders: for it will come out, if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have recounted, then return the fetus gradually, until you place it according to its natural form. For it itself will then come out with an easy exit.
But if it is prevented with all that we have recounted, then take of marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound all in a mortar with a good pounding: then anoint with it the vulva of the woman, and the lower part of her belly. Then make her sit in warm water until it reaches her hypochondria.
Therefore, when you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a pessary of myrrh: and apply it. When therefore the pessary has been in place for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and compress her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of the belly with gentle pressure. For the fetus will come out immediately.
The exit of the fetus is over the knees and hands. When it comes out in this manner, then she will succeed in inserting her hands gradually. Then the woman will be laid back on her neck, and her feet will hang downwards: and she will be lying on the bed, and will clasp her hands. Then press gradually on her belly. Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows, until she is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not come out, then take its feet, and move them with a strong movement. Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually, until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him insert his hands and gradually straighten the fetus; and instruct the woman to bear down until the fetus comes out.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. Skill will be needed in the reduction of his hand. But if he cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then make her lie on a bed, and shake her legs with a strong shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
But if labor takes her, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and adjust the fetus according to its natural form; and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to strain herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus is over its neck, with its hands outstretched, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually rotate it and adjust it easily.
When therefore it is adjusted, then place on her vulva that which we have narrated of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and instruct her to lean to her right side. Then instruct her to bear down; and make her sneeze with sneezing powders.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the orifice of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then tear the afterbirth with your nail, until it is evacuated of moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inwards, until you place it as is proper: and instruct the woman to bear down several times: for it itself will exit easily.
The exit of twins, or of many fetuses. Know that twins are often born: and sometimes three, and four are born. Nevertheless that is unusual, and rarely happens.
Five however is a thing unnatural, and they do not live at all: and one afterbirth contains them, however many they are: and they are separated by a septum, distinguishing between them, attached to the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they come out just as one fetus comes out by a natural, easy exit; and they come out by a blameworthy exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and arrive at that which we have spoken of from unnatural positions, then treat them as we have narrated: and let there be a skilled midwife, and let her do what she does with speed and slowness, and let her contrive according to every position with what is prepared for her from skill leading to health.
Concerning what is formed in the womb from embryos, and is aborted.
Sometimes one, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb: and it has already been verified by me, that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
That was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of the midwife. For perhaps a similar thing will come to her: therefore let her oppose it with what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division concerning medication, and it does not come out, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
Therefore if the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which loss is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on her back on the bed, and her head be inclined lower, and her legs raised; and a servant should hold her on both sides, or she should be tied to the bed, so that her body is not pulled out during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils with mallow, fenugreek, and linseed mucilage.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages: then let her introduce it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place in which to fix hooks on the fetus, and suitable places for that.
Consider then if the fetus is prepared to descend headfirst: then fix hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its clavicle, or in places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is on its feet, then in its pubis and middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand: and place its curvature in that which is between the fingers of her left hand: and introduce it with her hand easily, and fix the hook in one of those places which we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty thing.
Then let her fix in opposition to it another hook, or a third, if needed: so that the traction is equal, and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with an equal extension, and let the extension not be according to straightness only, but rather let it be moved in every direction, so that its exit is eased: just as is done with a tooth at its extraction.
And it is necessary that that which is between them be softened for the extension. But if a part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so that she may revolve with them that which is retained.
If therefore a part of the fetus comes out, then let the hooks be moved to another place which is higher: do this until the whole fetus comes out.
If therefore a hand comes out before other things, and it is not possible to reduce it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until, when it comes out entirely, you cut it either at the joint of the sole, or at the elbow; and thus you must do in the other hand, and in the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction happens to it in the exit, or water is collected in its head, then it is necessary that he introduce a spiny spatula between the fingers, and split the head with it, and extract the water; or break it with the instrument which is called Almisdac; the form of which comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly, if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary that you cut with algengematu; or extract it with misdach, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
But if the head comes out, and is constricted at the fork, then let it be split, until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it is not entangled, nor does it obey the exit: then cut the head piecemeal, according to what is possible for you.
But if there is an abscess in the lower part of the belly, or if there is dropsy in it: then it is necessary that you pierce it, until the moisture runs out of it.
If however the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the womb is light.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest: then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you have wrapped around your hand: and split the belly or chest, until that which is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns: then let the midwife insert her left hand. Therefore if the mouth of the womb is open, then let her insert her hand into the depth of the womb, and with it seek the head, and draw it with her fingers to the mouth of the womb: then let her insert into it one hook, or two hooks, and draw it out with them.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, because of a hot abscess which happens to it: then it is not necessary that one should labor over it, rather then it is necessary to administer the pouring of humid, oily things: and administer plasters: and let her sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be straightened: then let that which we have said about a living fetus be administered.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut up piecemeal, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you do not leave anything of the afterbirth inside at all.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said about hot abscesses which happen in the wombs.
But if a flow of blood happens, then let her descend into styptic waters, and administer the remaining treatment.
And I indeed already saw a woman, in whose belly a fetus had died; then she was impregnated over it a second time, afterwards another fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time an abscess happened to her in her navel, and it swelled until it opened: and it began to emit pus.
Therefore I was called to her cure, and I treated her for a long time: and the wound was not consolidated.
Therefore I placed over it some plasters of strong attraction: and a bone came out of the place. Then several days passed for her, and another bone came out. Therefore I was amazed at this, since there is no bone in the belly.
Therefore I considered that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
Therefore I examined the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in better condition: and now she lived thus for a long time, and emitted little pus from the place.
And I did not confirm this extraneous thing here, except because there is knowledge in it, and help for that which the skilled physician administers with the hand in treatment.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of instruments necessary in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the whorl by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the form of a constrictor, by which books are bound, which has equally two whorls at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this whorl ought to be more slender than the constrictor; and it ought to be made either of ebony or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers; and the margin of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the matrix, so that it is opened with them when you revolve the two whorls.
Another form similar to that one, more slender, and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each addition is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore when you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her legs hanging down, and that which is between her legs open. Then insert these two joined additions into the matrix: and you are holding the lower extremity of the instrument between her hips. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount you wish of the opening of the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wants.
The form of another dizziness which the ancients called the form of the impelling thing by which the fetus is impelled.
The form of the Almisdac, by which a large head is crushed: and it is similar to forceps, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it is oblong, like forceps according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, by which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having one point.
The form of a hook having two points.
The form of a spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The form of two spatulas, also broad.
All these instruments are exercised, and the more their kinds are multiplied, and are prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his work, and more highly regarded among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not despise any of these instruments, but let them be prepared for you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the secundines.
When the secundines are retained after childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it comes out by this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid; and put in it herbs which open the mouth of the womb, such as calamus, rue, and dill, and chamomile, and sage, and cassia lignea, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and submerge them with water, and put them over the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the pot's lid, and the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will come out easily.
But if it remains after this treatment, and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to submerge her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vulva, and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
But if it is attached deep within the womb, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and extract it gradually according to straightness, so that the womb does not fall during forceful pulling: rather it is necessary that it be moved easily to the right and left sides: then let the amount of pulling be increased. For it then obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adhesion.
But if the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with medication from a pot with herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then beware lest you tire yourself over it in repeated extraction.
Truly it is necessary that what comes out to the mouth of the womb from it, be tied to the woman's hip. Then give her a clyster with tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it afterwards, and it will dissolve, and it will come out.
However, when it putrefies, an evil odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms the weak woman. Therefore, suitable fumigations ought to be administered to it.
Some of the ancients, however, have already tested a fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated in the retention of afterbirth and menstrual blood.
Let it be made from a glass similar to an alcama, or from a crucible; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed upon the coals; let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed; and repeat with another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus.
Many boys are born, and their anuses are imperforate, which a subtle membrane has already obstructed. Therefore, it is necessary that the midwife perforate that membrane with her finger; and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatula; and let her beware of the muscle, lest she touch it.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil upon it. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a small lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the boy wants to defecate.
And now, obstruction of the anus also happens from the healing of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be cut, then treated with what we said of treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas which occur in the lower part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are a knotting, and a thickening which occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one part; and it is one fistula, or several.
When therefore that knotting becomes old, it is opened, and a watery white moisture or thin pus runs from it.
And sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by that which comes out with the excretion and flatulence, when the patient uses excretion; and perhaps worms come out of them.
And some of these fistulas are penetrating in the space as far as the bladder and as far as the urinary passage. And some of them penetrate as far as the hip joint and as far as the coccyx.
However, what distinguishes a fistula penetrating to the anus from a non-penetrating one is that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe into the fistula made of air or iron, when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe or a horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it completely, nor does anything come out of the opening from an evacuation, nor flatulence, nor a worm as I said, then know that it is non-penetrating: therefore hasten to the cure.
And when a fistula penetrates to the bladder or to the urinary meatus, then its indication is the exit of urine from it, and the prevention of this so that the place is consolidated with medicines.
If however it penetrates to the hip joint or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no tortuosity in it to the bone; and the finding of pain in the circumference of the part, and that pus always exits from it; and that medication is not effective in it, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all: and their cure is labor and vanity for him, of foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
In that which is not penetrating, and not old, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I relate to you, and I have already proven it: and it is so that the infirm person lies between your hands on his back: and raise his legs up above his hips, and his hips are inclined to his belly. Then insert a probe of lead or bronze, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know where the probe arrives.
But if the infirm person feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel in your finger the probe having already penetrated by itself detected, except that which you feel between your finger and the fistula itself, or flesh: then therefore know certainly that it is penetrating: therefore you do not labor in it, because there is no cure of it, as we said.
They have said, however, that in some men the flesh is healed, and of the medication by which help is hoped for it, it is that you heat a slender cautery according to the size of the fistula, as preceded, and insert it burning into the fistula, until it arrives around the anus. Then repeat that twice, or thrice, until you know that now all those added filtered flesh, which are similar to a bird's quill, are burned.
Then care for it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, until those burned fleshes come out: then care for it with ointments generating and consolidating flesh.
If therefore it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it is never healed with another cure.
And if a probe emerges, and does not reach to your finger which is in the anus, and there is between it and the probe a thick covering of flesh or fat, and you see a fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the probe which is in the fistula, until the cutting reaches where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered fleshes, which resemble the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
But if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cut those fleshes, then it is better, and of speedier help, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cauterization with fire gathers good habits, the burning of those added fleshes, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying of humors.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, or with crushed cabbage leaves, until the place discharges pus, and those burnt fleshes come out in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate hard flesh; and there are medicines in which there are stypticity and drying.
For when they are restored, then it is healed; and you do not fear a recurrence.
But if a fistula has already reached to the depth of the anus, and is lengthened by the superfluity of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a probe, and between you and it there is a covering of fat or flesh, and it is near to penetration, then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of trust and hope.
That is, because one of three methods is administered to it: either to cauterize it, as we said, or to cut it away until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert linen cloths and treatment nearby. And do not proceed far with the incision, lest you cut the muscle containing the anus, and there happens to the patient an involuntary discharge of excrement. Then treat it with what we have said.
Perhaps it will heal; or you lengthen that covering and clean it after you cut it almost to the anus, as we said, either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp point. Then cleanse those filtered tissues which are in the incision, as far as you can with exploration.
Then attempt the restoration of the whole wound with a suture, and the upper opening where it is consolidated: and a fistula remains open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier for the patient.
And sometimes a fistula is sewn up in this way: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is at the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus: then take a probe of perforated end, which is like an aleskef needle, that is, of those preparing leathers, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wound from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its depth.
If therefore it penetrates internally near the margin of the anus, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together: and tighten each, and leave it for one or two days: and the more the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well, until those parts of flesh which are between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away: then treat the wound, until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating: then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not for you to do that because of the muscle, so that you do not cut it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
The shape of the spatulite for splitting a fistula, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not cut what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
Concerning the suturing of haemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
There are haemorrhoids according to two methods. For they are either within the anus, similar to bladders of coals, or sloes; or as if they themselves were grape seeds. And of them there are small ones, and large ones: and blood always flows from them, and it is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus, are for the most part few in moisture, from which flows a yellowish water, or a little blood with a constant flow: and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is, that you instruct the patient to strain; and it is pressed out until it leaves the anus, and the athelil appear to you. Therefore, also suspend it with hooks: or hold them with your nail. Then incise them at their roots.
But if the hooks do not hold them, because of their moisture and softness: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a pledget of woolen fabric, and pull them out with your fingers. Then incise them, and sprinkle on them after incision some warm powders, that they may stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if the anus does not obey evacuation, then give the patient an enema in which there is a small amount of biting substance, so that what is in the anus may be washed away with it, and it will obey evacuation quickly, when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then their matter is light, easy: and it is so that you take them with your fingernail, or take them with a hook, and incise them. Then treat them with what we said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wound thread, and insert it into a needle. Then draw the hemorrhoid upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the needle, and let them be laterally: and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong ligature. Then knot the thread, and remove the needle.
Do this with all the hemorrhoids, and leave one of them: nor should you sew it, so that an excess of blood may flow from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth infused in rose oil, or cerate, and place it over the anus: and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls off.
When therefore the hemorrhoids fall off, treat them with ointments and the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of fissures. For they often happen from the dryness of the stool and the constriction of nature.
When therefore they become old, and medicine is not effective in them, then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a spatula, or with your nail, until they become moist, and the upper layer which prevents them from healing is removed from them. Then treat them until they heal as is necessary.
But if they do not heal, then repeat the scraping with greater force than the first time, until they bleed much, and are raw; then treat them until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted haemorrhoids, and dry fissures, and fistulas.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that a haemorrhoid is nothing but a round nodule, the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain from them occurs during walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is around the nail be cut, and held with forceps, or with a hook, and eradicated from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, however, there is a small, rough, filtered, round prominence on the surface of the body. And they often occur on the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you extend the athelil and incise it: or bind it with a silk thread: or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was previously stated with fire, or with a sharp medicine: and now inverted athelil, and non-inverted, are eradicated with the instrument which I shall describe.
An ant, moreover, is also a small, filtered, thick prominence on the surface of the body, extending deeply: and when cold finds it, something similar to the bite of an ant is felt in it, and it occurs throughout the body: and it occurs most on the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a tube from a goose quill, or a strong vulture quill: and place that tube over the ant, until the circle of the tube encompasses parts of it. Then rotate your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the ant: and place it deep in the flesh, and eradicate the ant from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a bronze or iron tube, according to this method.
Let the top of the tube be of a height drawing a rolled-up deafness, so that the grasping and turning of it on the finger is eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the ant according to what was previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are used for clysters in diseases of the anus, and for the loosening of the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast bronze, or of hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments, small and large, according to those in whom they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and of those whose anuses are weak or painful, so also their clysters.
Large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper head in which the bladder is tied, should be wide in this manner: and let it have a distinction so that the bladder may be tied as you see, and its lower extremity which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, very smooth, tapering to a point: and on one of its sides let there be two openings, and on the other side one opening.
And let the width of the opening be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker, and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be made of an animal's bladder: or of sheepskin, which should be in the shape of a small asofratus: whose root is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is rolled up, and let the space which is between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread of raw thread, which is made of ten threads, or thereabouts, and gather up with it the bladder like an asofratus. Therefore, when the bladder is placed in it, let it be tightened at the head of the clyster between two distinctions with a strong constriction. Then clysterize with it.
And this is the form of the circle of bladders here depicted, and of the holes.
Moreover, the quality of clysterizing with it is this: that you fill the bladder, or vesicle, with medicine, which is warmed according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and beware lest it be very hot, or very cold.
Then let the patient be laid on his back, and let him raise his legs upwards; and place under him an inthaan, that is, a hide on which men lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or similar things. Then introduce it into the anus gently. Then let the attendant take the bladder with both hands together, and squeeze it forcefully until he evacuates all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
If the medicine compels him to defecate, then let him hold it back as much as he can. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more effective in helping.
But if you give him a clyster for ailments of looseness of the bowels, and his evacuation is rapid: then repeat the clyster until he is cured.
But if there are haemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very fine, gentle, of the kind with which children are given clysters.
And this is the form of a fine clyster over which the skin is tightly stretched.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division concerning the treatment of wounds which was suitable for the book, and I will here describe their treatment by hand and with medicines according to the supplement.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore the things by which wounds are made are many times such as the throw of a stone: or the incision of a sword, or a knife: or a blow with a lance, or wood, or an arrow: and similar things from many things.
Moreover, wounds which are made according to the places of the body are, such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or breast, or belly, or liver: and similar members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may put that reasoning and rule upon the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds: because the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound happens in the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the throw of a stone, or similar things, and it has now broken only the skin: and it is a large wound, and you fear concerning the bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hasten, and immediately bleed the cephalic vein: and do not delay this: and draw from him blood according to the quantity of his strength: if nothing is prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood which comes forth from the wound is little. And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of repletion, and wine, and coarse foods.
But if something prohibits bloodletting, then let him be reduced in food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess happens to it, a poultice soaked in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are secure from a hot abscess, then place on the wound, if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not changed it, the powder which I shall relate shortly: and if the air has already changed it, then place on it certain ointments, until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is from the incision of a sword, and the like: and its lips do not come together by means of lint, then bring them together with a suture, according to what I shall relate in wounds of the belly concerning their suture.
But if the wound is already detached from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspensory: then incise it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore in it hard flesh in place of skin, and prescribe to the patient that he be nourished with foods in which there is thickness, such as are heads [of grain], and cooked wheat, until it is like pottage: and things similar to these.
But if you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then incise all the putrefied part, and throw it away: then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not cut off by medicines, then inquire about the artery. Therefore if you find it uncut, then cut it with a spatula, or tie it. But if necessity demands when that which we have said is not helpful, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops.
But if these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, light: so that you sprinkle this powder on them; because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of incarnative powder. Take one part frankincense, and two parts dragons blood, and three parts of unslaked lime: let the whole be well ground and sifted: and let the wound be filled with it, and bound tightly until it adheres well to it and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it over with lint, and leave it, and do not unbind it while it remains, so that it may not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes the lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense and dragons blood at hand; and the dragons blood also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to place it on them and bind them, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
But if the air has already altered them in some way, especially in summer, then the application of this powder is not advisable: therefore treat it with what we have said, so that you place some digestive ointments on them: or place barley flour poultices, made with water and honey, on them until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
But if a fracture in a bone occurs with a wound, and it is small, then extract it with a Gesti: and I will now describe the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
But if a wound happens in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to do except to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as ziniar and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the most superior of the members; and let that which heals it be from mild medicines, such as lime washed with fresh water infused several times with oil, or with rose oil, and tutia, when it is also washed with fresh water; and the other mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer a suture; or draw together its edges with powders. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus has already gathered, then perforate it in the lowest place in it.
But if an artery has already been cut in the wound, and a flow of its blood has followed, then separate, or cut it, and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the throat, or the whole of it, have been cut, and the al-haudegi have been saved, then bring together the edges of the skin with a suture over the tube of the throat, and do not touch the throat, but rather level it, and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable bandage, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments which have astringent and drying properties, until it is healed.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then investigate it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract all of them, or as many as you can: and what is not prepared, leave it, until the wound festers, and its discharge is separated and lessened.
But if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle on it the powder which I have described. But if bone is seen in the wound, and a fistula forms, then treat it with that whose mention came in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if air escapes from the wound when the sick person breathes, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep, and is fresh with its blood: then do not put powder on it at first, nor bind it tightly, lest the blood be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the sick person; but put on it an attractive ointment and dissolve it twice a day.
But if you do not have ointment at hand, then put old linen on the opening of the wound, so that it may absorb what comes out of it in the form of moisture; and make the sick person sleep on the wound, so that what collects in it may flow away.
But if three days or more have passed since the wounding, and the sick person has not experienced spasms, nor a bad trembling of the heart, nor shortness of breath, and you see his other conditions to be good: then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it with linen cloths and the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it always opens up again, then know that a fistula has formed. Therefore treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then treat it with what preceded concerning suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and fragments have fallen from it: then explore the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound putrefies, for their extraction is then easier.
The judgment in the treatment of other wounds occurring in the other members is the same as that which we have spoken of.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen, and the protrusion of the intestine, and their treatment.
The rupture which happens to the abdomen is great, moderate, and small.
Therefore, from a great one sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, protrudes.
Therefore their replacement and suture is more forceful and difficult.
And a small rupture is also made difficult in another way. It follows therefore that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hasten in their replacement to their place immediately: because it is inflated, opened, and thickened: wherefore its entry becomes difficult.
Therefore, on account of this, a medial rupture is better: because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in these two kinds.
Know that the suture of the vein is according to four methods.
Two common sutures are suitable for the suture of the abdomen, and for the suture of the other wounds of the body: and two other sutures communicate properly with the remaining wounds. Nevertheless, they are more proper with the suture of the abdomen: and I relate to you each one with explanation and clarification.
Therefore I say, that when a wound is small, and something of the intestine comes out of it, and it becomes difficult to reduce it: then that is because of one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the coldness of the air.
When therefore it is so, then it is necessary that you warm it, so that you submerge a sponge, or a cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone. Or boil in water squills, and cyperus, and spica, and bathe the intestine with it, until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does this: and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or marsh-mallow has been boiled. For by it its entry is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this cure, then incise a little in the disruption, with the instrument with which haemorrhoids are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is prepared, than incision: and constraint to incise is not done after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part of it not sharp, and let the thin end not be with the thinness of a spatula, but rather be a little blunter: and it is that which is similar to a cautery, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own proper place, if possible upon it: because it is better.
But when the disruption is large in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick man lie on his back, and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if there is a disruption in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your intention and your consideration always on this, that the part in which is the wound be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary that you administer this in large wounds and in medium wounds.
But in small wounds, put it according to what is prepared for you.
Then cause a devout assistant to be present between your hands, who holds the whole disruption with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it to him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the method of the common suture of one.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the size of the wound. Then leave from the extremity of the disruption the quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle except that you insert into it thread at two extremities of the skin, with two extremities of the sifac which is under the skin from the insides, until you cause it to penetrate from the other side, and you have already brought together two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the sifac: and four rows are made.
Then tighten with double thread around the needle several times from the two parts together, until the two lips of the wound are joined together with decent joining.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix in them another needle: afterwards draw it back with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with as many needles as you need, until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness: because thin needles very quickly cut the flesh: and thick ones are difficult to enter the skin: on account of that it is necessary that they be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in two extremities of the skin very close together: lest the flesh be quickly cut, and the wound be opened before its consolidation, and they not be elongated with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from consolidation.
Then cut off the ends of the needles, so that they do not injure the patient in his sleep: and place on them small cushions of cloth on every side, which hold the ends of the needles: and leave them, until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And indeed this kind of suture with needles is thus more suitable in small wounds. Because one needle, or two, or about that many suffice in their suture.
The second suture however is common, so that you bring together with the suture four extremities: two extremities of the skin, and two extremities of the fascia at one time, with a needle in which is wound thread equal in fineness and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, bring back the needle from the part from which you began the same, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that the suture is, like the seam of a cloth by which cloths are bound together, and place between each suture the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture men know.
And with this suture I sewed a wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and there came out from the intestines about two palms of the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I reduced it after its intestine had stood outside the wound 24 hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in the space of about fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed: and he lived after that many years, occupied in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that he would not be healed.
And it was by a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor anything of medicines, was found. Therefore I put old linen on the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
But I shall describe two kinds of suture proper to the subject.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and the muscle going according to the length and breadth of the whole body: leave the end sifac on the side in which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside at the other end of mirach.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate in this way at the same end mirach from the outside to the inside: and leave the end sifac, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside: and make it penetrate with your penetration by which you make it penetrate at the sifac end mirach, which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end which is from sifac on the other side, and draw the needle out of the skin which it penetrated.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the end sifac, which is on the other side, with this end mirach: and draw it out of the skin which is from its part, and do that turn by turn, and another: and do that turn after turn, until you sew the whole outside according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew the suture of the skins as they sew skins: so that you sew sifac turn by turn from one part with one end of the skin: and that you leave it turn by turn from another part, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
His speech is told, so that you sew it according to the likeness in which some physicians sew them, so that they join each part to its own naturally corresponding part: and apply the end of sifac to its other end, and the end of mirach to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the needle in the end of the mirach nearest to you from the outside, and make it penetrate to the inside in that alone: and leave the two ends of the sifac. Then turn the needle, and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in the two ends of the sifac, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, also make them penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other ends of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose mention preceded.
This is therefore also the saying of Galen.
And some said. The suture of the belly is straightened with two needles. That is, to insert one thread into both of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from that which is near you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew hides equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly: then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
The judgment, however, of the medication of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you find a fresh wound with its blood, before the air changes it, and you reduce the intestine, and its own [place], and adjust it: then put on incarnative powder.
But if the air has already changed the wound: then put on it some ointments, which are present to you, until they produce pus: and the threads fall out, and the sifac and mirach are consolidated. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
But if medicines are not present to you, then put on it immediately, from the time it begins to emit pus, old lint; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in most instances, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will communicate with the principal members in injury, then it is necessary that you submerge soft wool in oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and put it around the areas which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
But if it feels pain, or pricking, or putrefaction in its intestines (for this often happens) then clysterize it with astringent, black, warm wine: especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and has become an exit penetrating to its concavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing: and that which is from the small intestine is of more difficult healing.
