But the intestine which is called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound which happens to it at all: and that is because of its multitude, which is of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is a hernia, and you obtain it fresh: reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
If however a space has passed, and it has now become green, or black: then it is necessary that you bind it with thread above the place which is blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not happen. For in a hernia are veins and arteries.
Then incise that which is beyond that ligature, and place two ends of thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the attraction of it, and the extraction of it in the case of a hernia, and the emission of pus of the wound, may be alleviated upon you.
Concerning a wound which happens in the intestine.
When a rupture happens in the intestine, and it is small: then it is possible that it is restored in some men: therefore, because I saw one who was wounded in his belly with a blow of a lance, and the wound was from the right of the stomach, and the wound was ancient: and a fistula was made, and excretion and flatulence came out from it.
Therefore I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until it was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the patient, that some evil accident might befall him in his belly: and no evil accident befell him from that. And he remained in better conditions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and entered the bath, and exercised in his diets.
And now some experienced men have said that when a wound happens in the intestine, and it is small: then it is necessary that it be tied according to this method. And it is that you take ants with large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one of those ants which have an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When therefore it gets on top of it, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere, and not become loose.
Then put another ant near the first, and do not cease doing that with ant after ant according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and sew the wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine, until the intestine is nourished, and heals, and no harm at all happens to the patient.
And now it is possible that the intestine may also be tied with a fine thread: which has been wiped clean from the animal's intestine, attached with it after it is put into the needle.
And it is so that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it is much fleshed out. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its end. Then insert that thread into a needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then return it into the rupture.
And this method of suture with ants and intestine is only according to a way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy except thinning, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of fistulas and fistulae.
Know that every wound or abscess, when it grows old and becomes ancient, and becomes an ulcer, and does not consolidate, and always emits pus, and is not excised: then it is universally called a fistula, in whatever member it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula indeed is, according to the truth, a filtered, hard, white knot; with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather; and because of that, some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is at certain times moist, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is split at certain times. And sometimes this moisture is much, and sometimes little; and it is thick, and it is thin.
And I already premised in the division, then when I said wounds, that every ulcer which is not healed, nor does flesh grow in it, is not that except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the paucity of blood in the body: or because of its bad quality: or because between it, and above its lips is hard flesh, preventing the growth of flesh: or because there is much dirt and filth: or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter which is emitted from it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is unsuitable in its cure: or because the corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence, or through a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the healing of abscesses is delayed in it by nature: or because in it is one bone, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that fistula occurs in all the members of the body. For among them are fistulas which reach to the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sifac: or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the composite joints in their composition: such as the hand, and the foot: and among them are those which go to the proximity of a principal member, and similar things.
Therefore, whichever of these fistulas is in this manner, is among the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive a cure, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
But if there is some fistula whose depth does not reach these places which I have mentioned: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at the knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze, or iron, if the fistula extends in a straight line, and explore it with it.
But if there is tortuosity in the fistula, then investigate it with a thin lead probe. Because lead, with the softness of its body, is eased, and bends around the tortuosity.
But if the fistula has many orifices, it is not possible for you to investigate it with a probe. Therefore, inject a clyster into one of its orifices.
For the moisture with which you inject it goes towards the other orifices, and flows out of them.
Then skillfully inquire, in whatever way is possible for you, to know whether there is a bone or nerve there, and whether the concavity of the fistula is distant or near; or whether it is a single fistula with many orifices, and stand over all of it with the greatest strength, with your questioning, by which you question the patient, and by finding pain upon compression with your hand on the place, and by similar signs and causes occurring in an abscess.
Therefore, when you have stood over all of those things with true judgment, then proceed to treatment with confidence: and it is that you consider then, if the fistula is near, appearing, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I have told you: then cut the fistula as has been previously stated in my account, and remove what is within it of pus and corrupted flesh, which grows in its orifice from flesh, or thelul, and similar things: and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you cut it deeply as much as is possible for you. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply pledgets wrapped in sharp medicines: and introduce them up to the concavity of the fistula which you grasped with the iron; do this with it several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the concavity of the fistula from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is confirmed for you: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I have told you: if nothing prevents you from doing so, such as a vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
If therefore bone is detected, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to grow flesh, until it is healed.
But if it is not healed, and emits pus as it did before: then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore investigate it a second time, and carefully perform its scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good: and if not, then know that that corruption is in a distant depth of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
But if a small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps which are suitable for it.
But if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of them all, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If however something of the humors opposes you, and there is no fear or error in incising them, then incise those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If however you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then apply something to the wound that will cause it to putrefy: and leave it until that which is around those bones of flesh and humors has putrefied.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And let the incision which you made in your operation be constricted. Rather, if you fear, place upon it a lint ball submerged in crushed sulfur with oil: or a lint ball submerged in Egyptian ointment, or some green ointment.
For when those humors putrefy, and the bones are separated from them, their removal and extraction will be easier.
But if it is one large bone, such as a bone of the leg, or a hip bone, and similar to them, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the ultimate method, until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
But if that which is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it heals.
And I tell you, a case which happened to a man in his leg: so that you may place it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a man, young in age, about thirty years old, who suffered pain in his leg, from a cause which moved upon it within the body, that is, from the interior, until the materials reached the leg, and it became an abscess with a large abscess: and there was no external cause for it.
Therefore, time passed with him through the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many materials gathered with it. And his treatment was delayed, until the leg became fistulated, and many openings were made in it: all of which emitted pus and the body's humors.
Therefore, many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one skilled in the art of surgery, until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the materials which flowed from those openings with a great flow: indeed, the whole body of the man was now wasted, and his color was yellow.
Therefore I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found them to connect with each other from all parts of the leg.
I therefore prepared and cut above one of those openings, until I exposed a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted; which was now eroded, and blackened, and putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore I sawed what was exposed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone but that corruption, which I cut away and sawed off. And I had now eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried, and I uncovered the bone a second time above the first uncovering: and I found continuous corruption with the bone: and I sawed off what appeared to me also of that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I uncovered it again, and I did not cease cutting the bone piece by piece, and trying its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut away about one palm's breadth of bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it consolidated quickly, and healed: and this iteration in its operation, and section of it, was not necessary except because of the disposition of the weakness of the patient, and the smallness of his tolerance, and my fear for him of death: because syncopes happened to him at all hours from the superfluity of evacuation.
Therefore he healed with a complete healing: and firm flesh was born in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he was occupied in his affairs: and it caused him no hindrance at all in walking.
But if a prominent bone in the body is already broken: then it is necessary that you saw it, according to this method: and it is that you take a ligature, and tighten it at the end of the prominent bone: and instruct someone to pull it upwards: and place another ligature of wool thicker than the first ligature. Then tie it over the flesh which is under the bone: and extend its extremity, so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you uncover the flesh over the place which you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place a piece of wood, or a board, decently under the bone below: because when you do that, it is not prevented from cutting the corrupt flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be somewhat above the corrupt place, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone: and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and forces another sawing in its place.
But if the bone is corrupt, and is not prominent, but rather a part of it is continuous with a part: and the corruption is in the middle of it, or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all parts completely. Then place wood underneath.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing is continued from the other part: and let the sawing be somewhat longer than the corruption, according to what we have said.
But if the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupt joint itself, and saw the bone where it is continuous on the other side.
However, if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it except abrasion.
But if the corruption is in the metacarpus or in the metatarsus, then the matter is very difficult. Truly it is necessary that you investigate how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever instrument is rectified for you: when it does not oppose you, vein or nerve.
And know that cutting instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their thinness, and their size, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument suitable to that operation.
And know that these operations signify to you the kind of instrument you need, when you have long study. And the knowledge of the methods of this art, a testimony of the methods of illnesses: for then you will find by yourself that which is suitable to it from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I am confirming to you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will put as examples upon which you will cut, and the test by which you will try upon others.
The form of another saw.
The form of a joint rasp. Let the head of this rasp be according to the form of the head of a star-headed nail; and let its points be according to the form of the points of an elischetfegi.
And it is not suitable except that the heads of joints are rubbed with it when they are corrupted, or a bone is ample and large.
The form of a larger rasp.
The form of a wide rasp.
The form of a rasp in which there is a concavity.
The form of another, fine rasp.
The form of another rasp with a curved end.
The form of a small, thin, fine rasp.
This is similar to a saw, and the end is like a saw; it is shown in many places for the sawing of bones.
The form of a rasp which is suitable for rasping that which has been perforated from bones.
Its end is triangular, of sharp points.
It is made of Indian wood. And similarly it is necessary that all those things which we have spoken of be made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy, either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal cures, and the cure does not help, and you see the corruption cutting into the limb, which nothing repels, then it is necessary that you incise that limb up to where the corruption has reached, so that the sick person may escape death through it, or the harm which is greater than the loss of the limb.
And a sign that this has happened is that the limb blackens until it seems that fire has burned it, or it putrefies after the blackness, until that putrefaction is incised up to what follows that limb, and it creeps into the whole body.
Therefore hasten with its incision, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the striking of some poisonous things, such as a sea scorpion, and viper, and whelk, and the like.
If therefore the corruption or blow is in the extremity of a finger, then incise the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, that it may proceed to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then incise it from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it invades it, then incise the arm at the elbow in its joint.
But if the corruption passes through, and you see it around the elbow: then in that is the death of the infirm. And to administer beyond that in treatment, is a matter of skill.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then incise it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold in the instep, then incise the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then incise the leg at the knee joint.
But if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except giving up, and leaving the infirm to death.
And the method of incising a limb, or sawing it, is to tighten a ligature below what you wish to incise; and tighten another ligature above the place. And let one assistant extend the lower ligature; and let another assistant extend the upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures with a broad spatula, until all the flesh is uncovered. Then let it be incised, or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and that the saw not touch the healthy place; and there may happen to the sick person an added injury, and a hot abscess.
But if there happens an internal flow of blood while you operate, then cauterize the place quickly; or place upon it some powders that staunch blood. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded member with a bandage suitable to it, and take care of it until it is healed.
And I tell you that which happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I relate to you.
Which is, because there happened in his foot a blackness, with a burning which is similar to the burning of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began to affect the whole foot.
Therefore the man hastened of himself, after he saw the corruption falling upon the member with its vehemence, which he found from its pain and burning: and he cut it off at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore he came to me, and I tried to recover that excess with what I put on the hand from medicines after cleansing his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to proceed into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
Therefore he called me, to cut off his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from myself concerning the repulsion of that excess: and I also feared for him concerning the cutting off of his hand, death. Because the man's strength was beyond the case.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and it came to me concerning him, that he had hastened, and had cut off his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this account, except that it may be a help concerning that which falls under the kind of this sickness: and that it may be a signification by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses, and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus: then it breaks or is perforated, and that which is in it of pus goes out, and a vacant place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it is like a cloth which has been thinned: and it does not arrive at the last in its corruption, nor does it impress upon bone, nor upon nerve, nor upon ligament: it deserves to be named a Sinus: and it is not named a Fistula: nevertheless if its space is prolonged until the corruption impresses upon any of these members, then it is named a Fistula, or zuken.
And its cure is, that you incise that whole skin as it is rolled up: and especially when it has been thinned, and made like a cloth: and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres, and that last corruption does not follow from it: and if there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster: and it is, that you consider the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus which is emitted from it is of a foul odor: then clysterize it with the Egyptian medicine, which is of vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, they are collected in a vessel: and they are boiled over a fire, until the medicine reddens: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take of it what you need: and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the Sinus with it: and bind its orifice, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
Then draw it out with expression: do this for so many days, until the Sinus is cleansed, and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is, that you take the ash of vine wood, or ash from oak wood: and pour water over it. Then strain it, and clysterize with it, until you are confident that the Sinus is now cleansed.
But if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the ash water, then clysterize him with water and honey, to which something of crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or clysterize him with mixed honey and wine, because it is of the property of honey to cleanse and wash: and wine causes adhesion to the Sinus, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the Sinus has now been removed: then clysterize him with that which causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is, that you take of diafinicon plaster: and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or clysterize him with certain other medicines and ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
But if the opening of the Sinus is narrow, so that the clyster cannula does not enter: then enlarge it slightly with iron: or place in it a linen tent soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in basilicon ointment, until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the opening is also wide, then bring together its edges with a suture: and leave of it an amount into which the clyster may enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus flows is elevated to the upper parts: then cut it in a lower place which is in it, so that the pus flows from it downwards: because pus, when constricted in the depth of the Sinus, prevents flesh from growing in it.
But if it is not possible for you to cut the Sinus around its lower part as you wish: then try to prepare the member with a preparation by which pus flows from it easily, according to what is prepared for you. Try that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all: and take consolidating medicines which are placed on the wound, suitable to what you wish. And spread them on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a quantity that the whole Sinus is communicated with. Then cut with scissors in a straight line with the opening of the Sinus a hole, a little larger than the opening of the Sinus. Then spread some mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole: and place it over it at a distance from the opening of the Sinus, so that the exit of pus from it may be relieved: and do not remove the large cloth, except after a few days at one time.
But the one which is over the opening of the Sinus, is the one which you remove every hour, so that what collects in it from pus may come out: and so that you may know whether that which flows from it is much or little virus, or digested, or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the Sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it, or whether there is no pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or whether there is no abscess in it.
When therefore your medication is extended in this way, and you see the matter which comes out over the opening of the Sinus, then know that the Sinus is now adhering, or approaching adhesion: then therefore dissolve it in the morning, or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change to another cloth, and renew the ointment.
But if a subtle virus comes out of the Sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
But if undigested pus comes out of the Sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adhesion of the Sinus is delayed, and its matter is prolonged: then add to the drying of the medicines, truly let the physicians do, so that the medicines are moist in their substance, and dry in their power: such as diafinicon ointment, when an excess of addition of zegi is added to it.
And of the most last with which it is cured, is that you take diafinicon ointment, and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle over it old wine equal in its substance.
Then cook it with it, and use it.
And now it does similarly to this action, when you do not have diafinicon ointment present, honey, when you boil it down until it thickens, and you use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then spread over it a cloth, and bind it over the sinus: and spread with honey which you have boiled down until it has thickened: and powder over it the spices, and put over it a cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes there is administered in it long birthwort, and roots of the celestial lily, and there is lily: and herb flour, and centaury. Use them simple, or compounded, grind them, and sift them: and powder over the honey which I have related, and use it.
But if the matter of the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment: then know that in its depth there is corruption: or it already makes an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment of fistulas, according to what has been premised.
Concerning the treatment of a whitlow and a worn nail, and the addition of a finger incision, and section.
A whitlow is an abscess which is born under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it is born in the other fingers.
When therefore its matter is prolonged, and its treatment is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess: or it is corrupted, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it corrupts the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odour: and the extremity of the finger becomes broad, and its colour becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it, with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary that you incise with an iron instrument all the excess which remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound afterwards. For cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the empty additions of the nail now compress the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it, and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin probe under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards: and incise that flesh easily, and put on what remains of the flesh from burning, corrosive medicines, until the whole of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption already affects the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone, and extract it: for it is never healed while the corrupted bone remains in it.
But if you see corrosion and corruption advancing in the finger, then incise it at one of the joints, according to what has been previously mentioned. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if attrition or percussion should happen to the nail, and intense pain occurs, then it is necessary to first bleed the patient. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula with a transverse incision from above downwards.
And take care that the incision does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause very intense pain to the patient by that, and it will be the cause of the growth of excess flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with that which soothes pain.
The added digit which is born in some men's hands, perhaps is wholly fleshy; and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail on it; and some of them are born at the root of a joint, or its origin is in some knots of a finger. Therefore, that which is born in some knots of a finger, is not moved; and that which is born near the joint of a finger, perhaps is moved.
Therefore, that which is fleshy among them, its excision is easy. That is, to incise it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of that whose origin is at the root of a joint, is difficult. Therefore abstain from its incision.
However, in that which is born in a finger at the last joint, then it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a circular incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
Moreover, the consolidation which happens to fingers mutually, very often happens either from a person's birth, that is, he is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them linen cloths, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate them; or place between them a thin lead plate until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly if consolidation happens to some fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the member.
Concerning the incision of vices.
Varicose veins are twisted, thick veins, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many members of the body; and their occurrence is most frequent in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and work, and carry burdens.
Therefore it is necessary that you administer a shaking of the body from black bile, sometimes with a strong shaking.
Then you should bleed the patient in the basilic vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cut, and the black blood is drawn out. And the other method is that the vein is raised, and the whole thing is drawn out.
The cutting of it, however, is according to this method. First bathe the leg with much warm water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the leg of the sick person from the upper part of his hip to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw out the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from the upper part the phlebotomist ought to do it first. Then cut the vein downwards until a quantity of blood comes out which you see to be sufficient, and which the patient's strength can bear.
Afterwards bind it: and instruct him to abstain from foods which produce black bile: and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are full, and it harms the patient.
The elevation of it is in this manner. Shave the patient's leg if there is much hair on it. Then put him in a bath; or rub his leg with warm water until it reddens and the veins swell; or let him take vigorous exercise, if a bath is not available, until the limb is warm.
Then cut the skin in line with the vein lengthwise; either at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein on all sides until it appears to the senses; and at its appearance, you will see it red tending to black. When it is thus freed from the skin, you see it white as if it were a cord.
Then insert a spatula under it until it is raised and emerges from the skin; suspend it with a blunt hook gently.
Then make another incision close to that incision, three fingers apart. Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with spatulas as you did before, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need it. Then raise it, and incise it with the last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision which is above it.
And do this until you pull it out from the incision which is higher, or higher than all the incisions, until when it comes out entirely: then incise it.
But if it does not obey you to pull it out and lift it: then insert a needle with strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert a probe under it: and turn your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware that it is not incised. For if it is incised, it becomes very difficult for you to lift it, and harm from it enters upon the sick person.
Therefore, when you have pulled it out entirely: then place on the place of the wounds wool submerged in wine and rose oil, or oil: and treat it until it is healed.
But if the thing is constricted, having a tortuosity to which there is a winding to the parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you split it along all the part of its tortuosity, and the places of its winding.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you lift it entirely: and beware in your cutting upon it lest you cut a vein, or wound it: for it will become difficult for you to lift, or pull it out. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The form of the incision by which the thing is healed.
The form of a blind hook.
It does not have a curve like the other hooks, nor is it of a sharp extremity, so that it does not wound a vein: and it is of a thick, gentle curve: because if it were thin, it would cut a vein by its thinness: indeed let it have superfluous thickness, as we have said.
On the extraction of a vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Algezira, and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands, of little softness, that is, in which grass or trees do not grow.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation indeed is from putrefaction which happens under the skin, just as snakes and ascarid worms happen within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein is that a vehement inflammation happens in the leg. Then the place is blistered. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that blister, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
When therefore its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary that you wrap around it a piece of lead, whose weight is from one keil drachm up to two drachms, and tie it: and let the lead hang from the leg.
As often therefore as something comes out of it to the exterior, wrap that in lead, and tie it. But if it is prolonged much, then cut a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it radically before the whole comes out. Because if you cut, it contracts, and enters into the flesh: and it causes an abscess, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be treated gently, and extracted little by little, until it comes out to the last of it, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein indeed comes out in some men that which is in its length five palms and ten. And now it has come to me, that it came out of a man twenty palms.
But if it is cut into during your treatment: then insert a probe into its hole, and pierce it with a long hole, with the body, until all that is in it of matter is evacuated: and treat the putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears in the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself: and many openings happen to it, and a branch comes out of every opening.
Therefore treat it, as we said in the division, and with what has been said before.
Concerning the incision upon worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in a certain land among us bovine illness, because, for the reason that it very often happens to cattle, and it is a small single worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it goes throughout the whole body, ascending and descending; and its movement from member to member is shown to the sense, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through in the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and serpents, that is, roundworms and pinworms, occur in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it goes in the body and rises to the head and arrives at a place, then perhaps it opens there and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, then this is not done except at its movement and its appearance to the sense.
Therefore it is necessary that you bind what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it penetrates deeply into the flesh and you do not find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the place until it dies.
And what is more feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
But if you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind below it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the infirm person use cleansing of his body with medicines dissolving putrid, bad humors.
And let him beware of foods producing putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the illness Nakir.
This illness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain which happens in certain members: then it is changed from member to member.
And I myself have already seen it, according to what I relate to you. I was called to a woman ill in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained one hour, I saw one swelling proceeding with speed, just as a worm proceeds, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it can be: so that it is like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain departed from that place, and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it arrived in the other arm.
Then she told me that it revolved in her whole body, according to what I saw.
Therefore I was amazed at the speed of its change from member to member. And indeed I had not before seen this sickness with my own eyes in this manner. However, I saw many who found pain to be transferred from member to member: and I did not see it with my eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is, except that it is because the woman was of those who are of the villages of dry bodies with exposed veins: hence therefore that windy quality appears to the sense as changed.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and of moist bodies, with hidden veins.
Therefore when you wish its cure, and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to you by sight, as we said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that constricted wind escapes: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels winds, and removes them with fetid pills, and pills of serapinum, and the like.
Concerning the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they fall in the body.
Their diversity is this: for among them are those which have large and small points: and concave, and blunt. And among them are those which have three points. And among them are those which have barbs. And among them are those which have barbs.
Those, however, which are according to the members in which they fall, are according to two methods. For either the members are of the principal concave members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar things. Therefore when it falls into any of these members, and the signs of death, which I shall relate to you afterwards, appear to you, then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in most instances. And when those bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it, and penetrates the bone, and exits the membrane which is above the brain, are: that there occur from it violent thirst, and dimness of vision, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white moisture similar to pap comes out from the place of the wound. And something similar to the wateriness of flesh comes out of it.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs, however, of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple; and it feels as if it were fixed in something hard, not in something empty. And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the motion of the pulse: and black blood runs from the wound. And coldness of the extremities follows, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death is approaching without doubt.
And a sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels which follow the neck become inflamed, and the color of the sick man changes. And he breathes a large, deep breath; and he seeks the intake of cold air.
But if the arrow falls on the membrane which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is great breathlessness with vehement pain, with sighing: and all the members of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from food comes out of the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the belly, and is fixed in it: something of the faeces comes out of the wound, or the gut, or the intestine which has already been broken. Therefore there is no hope in his cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
But if an arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger on the patient: then know that it is mortal.
The remaining members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and clavicle, and the vertebrae of the back, and the breastbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members are saved according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery, or nerve; and the arrow is not venomous.
And I tell you what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, that you may signify by that concerning your cure.
That is, because an arrow fell on a certain man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I obtained from it only a small part of its end, with which it adhered to its shaft: and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no barbs.
Therefore the Jew was healed, and no evil accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabian arrow, and it is one which has barbs. Therefore I cut above it between two guide-lines: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore I worked delicately with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his abdomen: and we thought that he would die from it.
Therefore, after thirty days had passed him, and nothing in his condition had changed, I cut above the arrow; and I manipulated it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no ill effect happened to him.
And I saw a man into whose back an arrow had fallen, and the wound had closed over it. Therefore, after seven years had passed, the arrow emerged at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman into whose abdomen an arrow had fallen, and the wound had closed over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and none of her condition changed, and the woman found no harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, into whose face an arrow had fallen: and the wound closed, and it remained, causing him not much pain: and many similar things to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's chieftains, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already deviated slightly to the right side: and the whole arrow was hidden.
Therefore I was called to his treatment after the arrow's impact for three days. Therefore I found the arrow wound very tight.
Therefore I searched for it with a slender probe, and did not feel it: and he himself found a puncture and pain under his right ear. Therefore I hoped that that puncture was from the tip of the arrow.
Therefore I plastered the place with a plaster which had attractive and digestive power, trusting in myself that the place would suppurate, and the sign of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could cut upon it: and there did not happen in the place an event signifying that the arrow had reached the place.
Therefore I persevered with the plaster upon him for many days: and no event happened, and the wound meanwhile healed: and he remained ill from its extraction for a space of days, until on a certain day he felt the arrow within his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it was opened: and when I probed it, I felt the slender point of the arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound by means of that sharp medicine, until the point of the arrow appeared to us visibly: and there passed for me with him a space of time of about four months.
Then after the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not yield to extraction: then I did not cease to refine and devise concerning it with ingenious methods, until I began on a certain day with decently made forceps, according to what their form will be at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound consolidated, and the patient was healed with complete health: nor did anything harm him at all.
And I relate to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows: so that you may place that as a rule and explanation concerning that which I have not said.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not grasped with narration: nor does the book comprehend that, and a prudent artisan compares a little with much: and with that which is present, with that which is absent: and finds a new operation, and a new instrument in the descent of foreign things of this art when they happen.
Therefore I say that arrows do not come out from the members in which they are fixed except in two ways. Or by pulling from the place from which it entered. Or by the opposite of the other part.
And that which goes out from where it entered, is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is pulled, and it goes out.
But if it does not obey exit at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its extraction is lessened, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and it does not obey you for exit, then leave it also for some days: and repeat it with pulling and movement every day, until it goes out.
But if it does not obey you for exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone itself on every side with a thin drill, until the arrow is widened. Then pull it, and extract it.
But if the arrow is now hidden in the place of the body, and is hidden and concealed from the sense: then search for it with a probe. But if you feel it, then pull it out with certain instruments which are suitable for its extraction.
But if you cannot operate on it, because of the tightness of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep inside: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then cut over it, until the wound is widened, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
But if it has barbs by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them on every side, with every skill which you can, and skills, if it cannot be done by pulling the flesh in the fracture of the barbs, and twisting them, until it comes out.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, in whichever place it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps to all parts, until you make it come out: and the fine points with the finest skill, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you, and its very removal.
But if you cannot operate on it within an hour, then leave it for a few days, until those things which are around it putrefy. Then try again: for the swelling will be lessened.  But if the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said of treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And administer skill in every way that is possible for you to remove the arrow: and let it be with ease and calmness, or slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that you do, as you pull out the arrow, that the patient be positioned in accordance with the way the arrow was positioned at the time of the injury. Because it is more helpful. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from among the positions.
But an arrow which emerges from the opposite side, or something of it already protrudes outwards: or the tip of the arrow makes a swelling by feeling from the upper part of the nearby skin, and you see it protruding: therefore cut over it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grasp.
Then pull it out: for it will then be easy to remove.
But if it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow is embedded in the bone and enlarges itself: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for a few days: then repeat it, until it comes out.
But if the wood of the arrow is in it, then push it in with that.
But if the wood has fallen off, and you wish to administer a push: then introduce a concave instrument to it, so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow: then push it with that.
But if the arrow is concave, then push it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is lightened by it.
But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you hollow out all the flesh in which is the poison, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with what is suitable for it.
But if the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is near to that which you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew up the wound, if you see it, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not let it go.
And many kinds are made of them, large and small and medium-sized, according to the size and smallness of the arrow, and the size of the wound, and its narrowness.
The shape of the concave pusher.
And the shape of the blunt pusher.
This is of a blunt end like a radius, so that its entrance into the concave arrow is eased, and it is pushed with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be phlebotomised, whose custom runs in the body, are thirty.
Of which sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae; and two arteries, which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two lachrymal ducts of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the tip of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins, however, which are bled in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the outer side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Basilic. And there is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic, and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilic, and it is situated on the inner side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, and it is situated above the bend of the elbow: and it is that in which bloodletting is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with manifest pressure.
And the Salvatella: and it is a vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is beneath the curve of the thumb from the outer side. And the second is the Saphena: and its place is near the heel from the inner side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is near the heel from the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Indeed, phlebotomy of the two veins which are behind the ears, is a help for old catarrhs, and hemicrania, and sahafati, and old bad head ulcers.
And the manner of phlebotomizing it is according to what I relate. And it is that the head of the sick person is shaved: and his occiput in the place of the two veins is rubbed with a rough cloth with a good rubbing. Then let the sick person bind his neck with a band, until the two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark with a cautery.
Then take a lancet phlebotome, and it is that which is called Incidens. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the phlebotome arrives at the mouth. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and incise the vein with the skin with an incisive incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or about that: and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with pledgets, and leave it until it heals.
And already the incision of these and their cauterization preceded in the beginning of the book.
Truly, the two arteries which are in the two times of phlebotomy are helpful for an old hemicrania: and a bad toothache, and a persistent ophthalmia: and the flow of sharp effused superfluities to the eyes.
And the manner of phlebotomizing them is according to what I relate to you. Let the infirm person strongly constrict his neck until two veins appear manifestly to the sense: and let their pulse be demonstrated under your finger. Then mark them with cautery.
Then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the cutting phlebotome underneath, and lift the vein upwards. And incise it, as you did in the other two veins: and make the blood flow according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then loosen the constriction of the infirm, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards place a compress and pledgets on it, and bind it tightly above with a strong constriction: and leave it until it is healed.
And now the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction have preceded in the beginning of the book.
The benefit, however, of phlebotomy of the vein of the forehead is, after phlebotomy of the Cephalic, for old facial illnesses: and for hemicrania, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The infirm person binds his neck with a fillet until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called a Fossorium.
Place the prominent point which is on the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike it with a comb, or with something else in its manner: and let the blood run according to the quantity which you wish. Then untie the perforation of the infirm person, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad phlebotome. However, it is not necessary that the phlebotome be of a sharp end, like the other phlebotomes, but rather of a broad end. And phlebotomize it according to the transverse direction: because the bone is near: and perhaps in it the phlebotome will be broken when it is thin.
Moreover, the help of the two veins which are in the two lachrymal ducts of the two eyes, is for the eye from scab and redness: and for sebum in the eye, and for sicknesses of the face.
And the manner of their phlebotomy is that the infirm person should bind his neck with a fillet. Then phlebotomize them; and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across, to a little length, with a small, somewhat broad phlebotome, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotome is of a thin point, perhaps it will break.
Then let blood flow as much as is necessary for you. And place a pledget on them, and bind them for one night, and unbind them.
Moreover, phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever, and violent headache, and diseases of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness which happens in the face: especially when it is longstanding.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the infirm person should bind his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin, long phlebotomy instrument; and fix it in the middle of his nose, between that which divides the nose according to its length. Because the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the phlebotomy instrument a little while; and you should let the blood run according to your need. Then bind it one night. For it itself is restored quickly.
Truly, the phlebotomy of the two Guidegi is a help for the constriction of the breath, and the beginning of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad ulcers, and corroding ones, that is, herpetic esthiomenes.
And the manner of their phlebotomy is that the sick person should be bound under both in his neck with a ligature: and the surgeon should stand above the head of the sick person, and the sick person should be sitting on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist opens the vein to the length of a fairly large phlebotomy. Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to what you see is necessary for it. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and bind the two veins with a moderate binding: so that the sick person is not strangled. And leave it until morning. For the wound heals.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins, Albucasis. Moreover, Albucasis's phlebotomy is a help: because it contributes to canker in the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad ulcers: and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those which are in the nose, and in its circumference.
And the manner of their phlebotomy is that you make the infirm one sit before you: and bind his neck with a fillet. Then roll back his lip, and look at the two veins: of which one is from the right of the lip, and the second from its left: and the indication of each is by the blackness of both. And this is true: because in their circumference are small, black veins. Therefore incise each with an incisive incision.
But if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is: then attend to the incision of the larger of them, and the more manifest of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed custom runs most with it: and incise the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the vein under the tongue after cephalic phlebotomy. Indeed, the help of phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that cephalic phlebotomy is for the inflammations which are in the throat, and the disease of the uvula, and the diseases of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is that the sick person sits between your hands before the sun: and lift his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side of it a vein, and on the other side of it a vein: and the color of each inclines towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize both: and take care lest you injure your hand in phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps there will happen a flow of blood from those arteries.
Now the three veins which are phlebotomized in the elbow are those in whose phlebotomy custom prevails in all men.
And phlebotomy of them is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome: or an olive-shaped one, sloping to a point: or by cutting with a knife-like phlebotome, and this is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And of them there are broad kinds, and fine ones according to the size of the veins and their narrowness. The form of the myrtle phlebotome is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of less breadth, and more finely pointed.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of fine veins, which contain fine, clear blood.
Moreover, the basilica, which is one of these three veins, is an aid to phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and the belly: and it befits the phlebotomist, when he phlebotomizes it, that he be cautious, and be on his guard against it, for under it is an artery. Therefore if he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will result. Therefore it is necessary that he not phlebotomize it with a pointed phlebotome; rather, let it be cut with an Alnesil.
But if the basilica does not appear in manifest appearance, then it is necessary that you abandon it, and change to another, or seek some branch of it: or phlebotomize in its place the vein of the arm. For it is manifest, and cut it with an Alnesil phlebotome, as we have said.
But if you wish the phlebotomy of the same, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm that you touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the place with a cautery, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with an Alnesil phlebotome, as we have said. And beware lest the incision fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, when you tighten the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with a cautery, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery: therefore avoid it.
But if you see the blood at the phlebotomy leap up like the urine of a boy, and it is thin blood, red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then therefore hasten, and place your finger on it for a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore the blood is cut off (for it is often cut off) then bind the arm, and release it: and let the patient beware of movement of it: and let him be upon his guard.
And let him not move it for some days, until it is healed.
But if the blood is not cut off, and overcomes you: and medicine is not present to you in your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities are constricted, and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a pistachio nut, and split it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the constriction of the vein decently, with a bandage and powders until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that whose mention preceded from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in most instances, because of the smallness of the wound, and the possibility of bandaging from the arm.
Now the benefit of phlebotomy of the dark vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body: therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist that he be wary of its phlebotomy, for beneath it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, and finds a nerve, numbness occurs in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve is often apparent to the sense. But if it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and is not shown: then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy, by which you phlebotomize, a section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
But if the vein is between two nerves, then slit the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the Cephalic vein. Moreover, phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is helpful, because it draws blood from the head, and contributes to diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by puncturing with an olive-shaped phlebotome, or with a myrtle-shaped phlebotome: because it is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
Nevertheless it is fitting for you in phlebotomy, that you avoid with the phlebotome only the head of the humerus, and seek a softer place: and it does not harm it, if it is not found with the first puncture, that it is repeated upon it with phlebotomy several times.
Nevertheless perhaps it becomes inflamed in some men when it is not phlebotomized at the first puncture, but that inflammation does not harm it in anything.
Concerning the usefulness of phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. Moreover, the quality of phlebotomy, and its consequences arising from it, in whose rectification it behooves you to think.
First therefore of that it is necessary that you should know, that phlebotomy is not administered, except in the preservation of health, and the continuance thereof, and precaution against the coming of illnesses.
And that phlebotomy be done in one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Median, and the Basilic. And that phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in Spring and Summer: and in Autumn and Winter after a meal, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Phlebotomy however which is administered in illnesses, does not have a determined hour: but when convenience and necessity prompt to it, by night or by day, and at every hour, and at every time.
And it is not fitting that boys be bled until fourteen years come upon them. Nor let a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth month until the seventh. Nor let old men be bled, who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore when someone wishes to be bled for whatever reason: it is necessary that he cleanse his intestines before phlebotomy with a mild clyster, if much retained excrement is in them: lest the veins during phlebotomy attract putrid superfluities from the intestines, harmful to the principal members.
Nor let a patient suffering from nausea, that is, sickening satiety, be bled; nor one suffering from a hangover, nor one recovering from drunkenness, until that is removed from them. Nor one ill with dropsy.
And let phlebotomy be avoided again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and everything that weakens the bodily or animal strength.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks and medicines: if that is possible for him.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him exercise in some way, so that the blood may be thinned.
And place his phlebotomy in the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said: and try that his chest be evacuated that day from all evil accidents, animal and bodily, such as distress, anger, and fear; and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, pain, excessive intercourse, and the like.
And let him have present in his seat things with the use of which the custom of men runs, from aromatics and perfumes and dancing and things like that, as much as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one being bled; afterwards let him draw out his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tie the ligature with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is unequal, or excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then also it prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the one being bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and are apparent to the sense.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the patient with a little old oil.
Then let him place the index finger of his left hand on the vein itself below the place where he wishes to bleed, a little: so that the vein does not flee, and the incision is not missed. Because among the veins there is one which you find like a fleeing cord during phlebotomy: and among them there is one which is full of wind; and when you place the phlebotomy tool on it, it contracts under the tool, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and does not open the vein; and if he opens it, it is constricted: therefore it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm, and be careful in all these things.
Then let him remove the phlebotomy tool. If therefore the vein is opened at once, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time a little below that place, or above it quickly, if the place is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, or the patient is frightened, then leave him for a day or two days; and do not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw a hot abscess. Nor should he enter a bath.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
But if the opening is small, or the blood flows thinly, and you fear that the quantity of blood you desire will not flow out, then repeat the phlebotomy in the same opening, according to its straightness, and add a little to the operation.
And do this quickly, before the place becomes abscessed, for an abscessed place is in many men a small operation.
If therefore you see it already abscessed, then do not repeat it at all upon it, for nothing excuses you there. And place upon it something of the sediment of oil; for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than the other oils.
And similarly administer the sediment of oil in all your phlebotomy, which phlebotomizes veins against the prohibition of blood flow: and theriac alfarach and sagzenea do this, when one of them is placed upon the place.
For the blood is thinned and dissolved when it is thick.
But if a large abscess should occur at the place of phlebotomy, for this often happens, especially to him who is not phlebotomized except at that time, or the opening of the vein is small, then hasten and place upon the place a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated a little: and bind it for one hour: for it is dissolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the exit of blood from the vein itself, with the completion of its [flow], or from another vein.
But if something of blackness or greenness remains in the place after some days, it does not harm: and if you wish, place upon it something of aloe or dissolved myrrh: or something of the juice of mint, that is, calamint or something similar to it.
And very often indeed abscesses and swellings occur near the phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
Therefore place your hand upon it; then if you find softness upon your compression upon it, then that swelling is bad. Beware therefore, that you place upon it something of that which we have said. For perhaps blood of an artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man from the blood according to the quantity of his strength. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it reddens; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it out until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once, that the vein's phlebotomy be widened, and the phlebotomy be wide; and in him who is weak, the opposite of this.
And it is necessary that he extract it in installments, and that the opening be narrow; and better that which is administered in the phlebotomy of the vein is that there be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from a flow of blood, and from the incision of a nerve; and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And for whom it is accustomed to arrive at syncope during phlebotomy, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy a little bread infused in pomegranate water: or secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from his blood in three or four installments.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take before phlebotomy bread infused in grape syrup, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in aromatic perfumed syrup.
But if syncope happens during phlebotomy, and its cause is the exit of much blood, then it is necessary that he take in drink meat water and subtle perfumed syrup; and let him use aromatics with algalia, and let his chest be anointed with it; and let him use the other things which we said in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
But he who wishes to evacuate his arm, and to let his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he let blood gradually according to the amount of strength in continuous days.
But he who wishes to evacuate his arm, and to let his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy.
But he who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite of the part to which it inclines: it is necessary that he be bled on the second or third day.
But in whom there is much blood already heated, and it is sharpened, and causes fever to happen: it is necessary that blood go out from him at one time, and that a large quantity be drawn from him: and let the opening be widened until syncope happens: after you shall have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy: and that you place your hand upon his pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not happen instead of syncope. For often that happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary, when you wish to open the arm and let blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of blood becomes difficult, that it be pressed upon forcefully and bent and extended quickly. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it until he is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of a phlebotomy instrument that which is clotted from the blood in the opening of the vein, and place upon it something of salt dissolved in water. And place upon it something of theriaca Andromachi, or sagapenum, and apply gentle compression until the blood flows out.
But if the vein is already festering, then leave it and do not cover it until the abscess subsides.
But if necessity requires bleeding a second time, and there is no excuse, then either phlebotomize in the same place, or in another vein in the other arm.
Moreover, the rope of the arm is phlebotomized in place of the Median and Basilic veins when they are not found and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the patient should insert his hand into warm water until it readily reddens and the vein clearly appears.
