Then it should be tied above it with a ligature of moderate tightness. Then phlebotomize transversely little by little, neither laterally nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that you phlebotomize above the joint of the hand a little.
But if the flow of blood is prevented, then put the hand back into warm water, and let the blood run into the water, until you obtain your requirement.
But if it is in the days of summer, then you are excused from putting the hand back into warm water. And indeed, the running of blood into warm water is mostly done in wintertime.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve beneath it.
However, phlebotomy of the Salvatella vein of the right hand benefits liver diseases.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that you bind the  *mahasun* of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you have put it into warm water, until the vein swells and is very clearly seen. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you incise it completely, it will not harm it in any way.
And take care not to overextend your hand with the phlebotome. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers, denuded of flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will coagulate in the opening of the vein, and will be prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have drawn the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
The help, however, of phlebotomy from the left hand, is: because it is beneficial for diseases of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
The help of phlebotomy in the saphenous veins, however, is for illnesses which are in the lower part of the body, such as diseases of the womb, and retention of menses, and infirmities of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the manner of this phlebotomy is that the infirm person should put his foot into warm water: and make friction upon it, until the veins swell; and bind above the joint of the foot with a strap. And indeed the location of the vein is near the heel, appearing towards the thumb; and many branches ramify from it in the surface of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or near the heel in its confluence; because it is better and safer.
But if you phlebotomize it in the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy, by which you phlebotomize, transversely, as if you wished to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be skillful.
But if the exit of blood is prevented, then bathe his foot again in warm water: and allow the blood to flow into it, until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein at the first attempt, then let him repeat with the phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you avoid the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The place of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its phlebotomy is for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the manner of its phlebotomy is that the patient should enter the bath and hurry; and let him bind his leg from the side of the hip to above the heel with four fingers' breadth of a long, thin band; for it does not appear except because of that.
When therefore it appears, then phlebotomize it according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to a transverse incision; and it is better, or that you incise it with an incision, or separate it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most men. But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the sense at all: then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the little toe; and beware of the nerves, and let the quantity of blood which you wish flow.
Then loosen the constriction, and place a piece of leather over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from brass, and from glass.
And cupping is done in two ways. With scarification and extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places on the body for cuppings which are administered with scarification and bloodletting. One of which is the cupping of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and the cuppings of Alhagdahim, and they are two plates of the neck on both sides together: and the cuppings of the chin, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades: and the cuppings of Alachosos above the end of the spine: and the cuppings of the two hips, and they are the middle of the two arms: and the cuppings of the two thighs: and the cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, strength does not fall from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor is it necessary that cupping be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any illnesses which occur from repletion, until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts cupping because of illness or habit, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
That is because there are men in whom blood multiplies until it needs extraction by cupping, and they find heaviness and headache in their head.
And some of them find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And some of them find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the place of their cupping. And some of them have increased laughter. And some of them find the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue is abscessed, and they spit blood. And some of them have increased sleep. And some of them see in their sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to them.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order that venesection be performed, after two or three hours of the day have passed.
The benefit of venesection of the nape of the neck is: because it helps with heaviness in the head, and what is poured out into the eyes. However, it is necessary that this be after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted that one who is of cold complexion, or in whom there is catarrh, use it. For it brings great harm to him.
And on account of that, it is not necessary that the elderly use it, and those in whose head there are cold illnesses.
But he who is assiduous over it, forgetfulness is generated upon him.
And on account of that it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, that he put down his hand with the cupping glass a little to the lower parts, fearing from the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or alcahel is in place of the median vein phlebotomy and the basilic vein phlebotomy, and on account of that it benefits asthma, and constriction of breath, and fissure of the instrument of breath, and cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the alcahel cupping glass be applied a little, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping, however, alhagdahim, benefits pains happening in the head, and ophthalmia, and hemicrania, and perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of the basilic vein phlebotomy.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, lest he press his hand with scarification: lest he cut an artery, and a flow of blood happen.
Cupping, moreover, under the chin, benefits canker in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar to them from sicknesses which are in the mouth: and it stands in place of the  phlebotomy alieherich, which are in the two lips.
Cupping of the shoulder blades, however, benefits alchafacham, which happens from repletion and heat.
Cupping, moreover, of the inner parts of the two thighs, provides aid to the phlebotomy of the three veins, the Basilic, and the Median, and the Cephalic: because it itself draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins draw from other thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper, lest he proceed much in scarification: because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
Cupping, moreover, which is cupped over the alchausos, benefits hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that the cupping glass be large, and that it be of metal: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps thin cupping glasses break: and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping of the legs, moreover, diminishes noticeably: because they draw blood from the whole body, and benefit old pains in the kidneys, and the womb, and the bladder: they provoke menstruation, and benefit boils, and ademenul: and they stand in place of the phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to occur in many men.
And the benefit of cupping of the alhorcobeni is close to the benefit of cupping of the two legs.
The quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass first empty, and suck with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat that and continue, until you see the place now redden and swell, and the redness of blood appear: then therefore scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For he who is of men of tender flesh, of rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, not more: lest the place be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification, and deepen it a little: and temper the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle movement.
But if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. In the first instance indeed a way is opened for the thinning of the blood, and its watery part: and in the second indeed for the thorough searching out of the extraction of thick blood.
If however the blood is very foul, then let a scarification be made a third time: that it may achieve the ultimate.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little, one scarification suffices us. But if we wish the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification: and the equal limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which it is necessary to administer from oils at the application of cupping glasses, and what the cupper should avoid.
Indeed, in that one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places to be cupped be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving oils: if it is in the time of summer, such as is oil of alkeiri, or oil of violets, or oil of sweet almonds, or oil of gourd seed.
And if it is in the time of winter, then daffodil oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbatine oil: and similar things.
But if the excess is thick, cold: then let the oil be marjoram oil, or balm oil, or white oil, or dill oil: and similar things.
But if the one to be cupped has large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he be forbidden oil, and it is necessary that he wash the places of the cupping glasses after cupping with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solanum, or with gourd water, or with purslane water: and similar things.
But he whose blood is of much humidity, let him wash the cupping glasses with vinegar, or with myrtle water and sumac: and similar things.
But he whose excesses are thick, let him wash his cupping glasses with old wine, or with water of samsucus, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile: and similar things.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in the bath, or immediately after the bath; indeed, it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the cupper or phlebotomist ought to be guided before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary that you consider first: then if the cupped or phlebotomized person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then it is necessary that he receive cooling things, such as pomegranate; and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and succotash, and julep: and similar things.
And put among his foods chickens, and the flesh of young sheep, and foods seasoned with verjuice: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take moderately fragrant wine, which is between old and new: and let him be instructed to use a small amount of food. And put among his food chickens, and canebir; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and young pigeons he should eat.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of cupping and phlebotomy be more than food: and it is necessary that in some men in the hours there be given in drink theriac alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, nutmeg, or electuary before cupping: and before phlebotomy, or after: that the principal members may be strengthened, and the blood thinned. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. Cuppings, however, which are without scarification, are cuppings which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, navel, and the regions of the kidneys, and the hip joint: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And nothing is sought by them, except either the drawing of blood from a member to a member: just as we place a cupping glass upon the breasts in the illness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to resolve cold, hidden flatulence in a member: just as we place a cupping glass upon the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes pain on account of this, because it resolves that flatulence.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when there happens in them an obstruction, or a stone. For by the virtue of its attraction perhaps it opens the obstruction, or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed upon the liver and spleen with rubbing flatulence in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only an incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of pleurisy: and that is so that the cupping glass is filled, and it is large, with warm water alone, or with water in which herbs which are suitable have been decocted.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the size of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be in its side, in the place which is about its middle, a small hole, according to the size that a needle enters it: let it be made of bronze together, or citrine, of thick edges, smooth, even, firm: so that it does not injure the limb in its placement upon it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of bronze, or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than that which we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For cupping glasses for children and thin people are different from cupping glasses for men and those with thick bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire upon a limb is to light a small linen wick with fire decently, or a small wax candle: and place it upon the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the burning fire is around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then let it be placed upon the limb: and a finger should be placed over the hole which I mentioned, while we hold the cupping glass: when we need to, let us remove the finger, and the vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately be released.
Then relight the wick in its manner, and repeat it, if you need it.
In the cupping glass however which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor hole: and it is not administered, unless it is filled with water, and placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it may hold much water, is better.
Concerning the application of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many places except in those members where the application of cupping glasses is not possible, because of their smallness, such as the lip and the gums, and similar things. Or in a member denuded of flesh, such as a finger and nose, and similar things.
And the manner of using them is: that you should obtain leeches which are in fresh, clean waters, free from putrefaction.
Then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then let the body be evacuated first with phlebotomy and with cupping glasses. Then wipe the member of the sick person until it becomes red. Afterwards, let them be placed upon it.
Therefore, when they are full and fall off, and suction of the place with a cupping glass is possible, then it is most effective in helping; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and let it be rubbed, and squeezed out.
But if the flow of blood is prolonged after the fall of the leeches, and it is by oozing: then dip a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing is stopped.
But if the blood increases, then powder over it crushed alum, or galls, and similar things from styptics, until the blood is stopped.
Or let there be placed on the place halves of shelled beans: and let them remain, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And it is necessary, if a repetition of leeches is necessary, when it is possible, to change them.
But if leeches are prevented from clinging, then let the place be wiped with fresh blood. Or prick the places with a needle until something of the blood comes out. Then let them be applied, for when they sense something of the blood, they will adhere immediately.
Therefore, when you wish them to fall off, sprinkle upon them something of powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations happening in bones.
Know, O sons, that foolish physicians and the common people boast of this chapter, and those who have never inspected the books of the ancients, nor read a letter from them.
And because of this reason, this method is deprived from the sciences in our land. And I never encountered anything good in it, and I did not obtain from it what I obtained except because of the long reading of the books of the ancients, and study concerning their understanding, until I extracted its science from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and what my experiment surpassed, after I have shortened it for you, and freed it from the burden of length, and shortened it with a fine abbreviation, and showed it with a fine declination: and I have formed in it many forms of the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse and chapters, to whose understanding the craftsman is compelled first to stand upon their truth; namely that you and those who are ready may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall happens to someone, then it is necessary that you hasten first to bleed him and loosen him, or both together; if nothing forbids, such as weakness of strength, or he to whom something of these things has happened is a child or a decrepit old man, or it is a time of vehement heat or of very vehement cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables and the flesh of birds and kids; and forbid wine and thick meats, and being filled with foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the infirm return to his first regimen with which his custom has run.
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the infirm be nourished with nourishing foods, with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and things similar to them. For with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and the decrepit, it is not possible that they are joined together and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs; and those which are in the end of softness are consolidated and joined together, like the bones of small children.
Nevertheless, nature attaches to the broken bone from every part something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you give the infirm nourishment of foods in which there are heaviness, and viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all the other limbs are all different from one another: and I have come with a recollection of every kind of fracture explained in its Chapter, distinct from another. And the kinds of bone fracture are also diversified in themselves.
Because its fracture is made curved, besides what splinters happen in it. And sometimes its fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and its fractures are splinters, that is, splinters, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is sharpened with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And each kind has its own method, or instrument, in its restoration, according to what its remembrance will come, distinct in its places.
And from what a bone fracture is known, is its crookedness, and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor sound on your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the infirm find great pain: then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that there is crookedness, or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression completely, indeed place on it of the medicines, of which remembrance comes, after that moment which suits the place: then let a subtle bandage be tightened.
And know that the place when it is bent, and split in two, besides what happens in it of injuries, nevertheless every part is removed from its pair: then it is necessary for you, that you begin from your hour to its rectification, equaling it before a hot abscess happens to it.
But if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days, until the abscess rests. Then equal it according to whichever way is possible from gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration, and its equaling, is easier than of a bone, in which injuries happen.
Then bind it, according to what recollection of it will come to you.
But if there are injuries in the bone, then extension of this broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the member upon an even place, according to its natural form, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try, so that you make those additions return with all skill, and in whichever way you can into their places: and beware according to your strength, lest pain or injury happen to the weak one through your operation: and try according to your strength to apply one of the two bones to its fellow, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the measure of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many foolish men do. For very often they cause by that operation of theirs a hot abscess, or a contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations very often.
Then, after equalization and proper operation and the binding of that member, and rest and tranquility, give advice to the patient about it. And make him beware lest he move it at the hour of his waking, and his sleep, in its turning and agitation, and in his evacuation, and all his movements, with his utmost strength. And that he beware lest the position of the member be a position with which pain follows. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the position of the member, then it is necessary that you change it to another disposition from that with which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him, that its position be equal and straight, so that torsion does not occur in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the binding of the broken member is as I relate.
Know that broken members are diversified in their smallness and their size and their form.
Therefore, those of them which are small, such as the arm and finger and the forearm and similar things, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. But those of them which are thick, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be in them a broad, firm wrapping.
Because a broad ligature connects a large member and binds it on every side with an equal binding, so that no spot enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation, by placing a suitable liniment on it evenly, on soft lint over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the ligature over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the member deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the ligature.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the place of fracture, and tighten less than was your tightening with which you tightened the fractured place. Then extend yourself with the wrapping from the place of fracture a little, and soften the binding gradually, until you take from the healthy place a large amount.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the fractured place with wrappings. Afterwards proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightening of the wrapping, and the softening of it, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft tow, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards evenly place over these bandages properly made splints immediately, if there is neither swelling nor a warm abscess in the place.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it, then place on it that which settles that abscess and removes the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be from the middle of a cane, smoothed, skillfully shaped; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the size of each splint be according to this form and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other splints; and the length of the splint should be according to what is permitted to the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind over the splints with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it neatly with threads, as we said about the binding: and it is so that your binding over the place of the fracture is tighter; and the further it extends from the fracture, the looser the binding.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of foolish men placing their threads from twisted linen cords, it is a great error; for the binding falls with them outside the proper measure.
And thin threads again are not suitable; because you will not achieve with them through the binding what you wish.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between splint and splint.
But if the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after binding in healthy places: then place under them some soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is injured by it.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I will deal with it by recalling it in the chapter according to its individuality.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with splints from the first day. That is, because when a limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints upon it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your assurance from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients used to treat, which are placed upon fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children: because it is from that which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is, that you take from the dust of the mill, and it is the marrow of flour which hangs on the walls of the mill at the motion of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and make its consistency neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
The method of another plaster, restoring fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take almess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath: and the juice of all, of each ten drachms, of myrrh and aloes of both kinds, of each five drachms, and of alethil twenty drachms. Let all be ground and sifted, and mixed with alethil water; or with egg white, if the complexion of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
The method of another plaster, which also benefits fracture and torsion. Take mugath, and mess, and white mallow, of each ten drachms, of myrrh and aloes of both kinds, of each five drachms, of acacia six drachms, of Armenian bole twenty drachms. Let all be ground very finely and sifted, and made up with water, or egg white: and let it be applied.
The method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and it soothes the pain occurring in them, and a fissure of the bone, and a fracture. Let greasy wool be taken and submerged in vinegar and boiled oil: and let it be placed on the place.
And in this plaster indeed there is no power of restoration, but it is good in soothing a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
The method of a plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig, and leaves of wild poppy: and grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
The method of an abbreviated plaster, administered in the restoration of a bone fracture: and when you wish the resolution of the remainder of an abscess. Take of marshmallow root, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let everything be ground, and prepared with wine, if the member is not heated.
But if it is heated, then prepare it with the water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster in resolution, than the first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a member. Take of marshmallow root, and linseed, and balaustium, and melilot, and bugloss, and violet flower, and chamomile, of each one part; let everything be ground, and prepared with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All that according to the heat of the member, and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a broken member in its restoration except cerate made from wax and oil: nothing else: and he related that it should be medium between thickness and thinness.
To Galen however it seemed that things should be placed on a broken member in its restoration, in which there is drying of heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar things to them from those which we have related.
Method of a plaster which helps with injury and pain. Take mugath, and chickpea, and cut human hair, or bird feather, and marshmallow, and salt, equal parts: let them be ground, and sifted, and prepared: and let a plaster be made from it.
The amount of time for which the bandage ought to remain, then be loosened, is that you should consider then, if no pain happens to the patient, nor itching, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not loosen it for many days. But if vehement itching or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is calmed, and the pain is settled.
But if vehement itching or disturbing pain, or swelling happens to him in the place: then hasten, and loosen it at once, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moistened: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching is calmed, and the pain is settled. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place over it cut wool, immersed in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it on for one night, until you are secure from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb is calmed, and its pain is removed.
Then return it to a thin bandage, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, and thin it with it, until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and bandage, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not unbind it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, the whole of it, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of flesh approaches over it: add then also to the bandage more than your first entire bandage: and add also to the thickening of the weak food, according to what the previous recollection of it was.
But if you see the place of the fracture drying up, and becoming thinner than it ought: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore bathe it with warm water at every time when you unbind it, and let this be for three days in all, and ease the bandage a little.
By this operation therefore nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
But that which foolish restorers of a limb do in another way, when it is not restored at first as it ought, and is restored according to crookedness, is an error of their operation, and great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have nowhere seen any trace of them at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the treatment of an accident in the head.
The kinds of fracture are diverse, and its forms many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which is made by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone completely down to the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of this this kind of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword cuts some of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end: and this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a contusion or attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the membrane which is under the bone: or it is in the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this kind of fracture is called Capillary.
And there is a fracture which is caused by a fall or blow from a stone and similar things, which cause a plate of bone to enter inwards, and a concavity is made in the place: just as happens to copper boilers, when a blow strikes them, and part of them enters inwards.
And this happens most in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And for all these kinds of fractures separate treatments are made, and I will come with the recollection of all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures by examining them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The hair-line fracture however is known by this, that it is detected on the bone, and wiped clean, and smeared over with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the patient: then if you see from the accidents that it signifies manifestly danger, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things among the accidents in many conditions, without doubt it is not healed: and if you see accidents not alarming you, and you hope for recovery: then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded man comes to you immediately after being wounded: and it is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day according to every condition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone of the membrane is corrupted, and those accidents which we have recounted occur.
But if the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with small pieces, and attraction: then it is necessary that you cut away the comminuted and abraded part, as I tell you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded man: and uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and that which is light upon the patient.
But if a flow of blood or a hot abscess happens to you during the uncovering of the bone, then apply to it what is necessary. And it is necessary to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the abscess settles, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to incise the bone with a sharp-edged incisor: and this is its form.
Then use another incisor, a little wider than the first: and this is its form.
Then use yet another incisor, wider still than the second.
And the second method is to have at hand a number of different incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some shorter than others: and let them be sharp at their ends, and made of good iron or steel: and use care in striking the incisor, so that it does not shake the head and injure it.
But if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to perforate its circumference before you use the incisors, with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are so called because they do not pass through the limit of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it: because the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take a large number of these drills, each of which suits the quantity of the thickness of the bone, until you have for every skull a drill: the quantity of whose sharp end in length and shortness is according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The quality of perforating around the fractured bone is: that you place the drill on the bone, and revolve it with your fingers, until you know that the bone is now penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place what is between each hole a quantity of the thickness of a finger, or about that.
Then incise with incisors what is between each hole of bone: and make it thinner than you can on that from the end of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with something else from certain instruments which you prepared for it: such as forceps, and fine scissors.
And it is necessary that you beware with all caution, lest the drill or incisor touch anything of the dura mater.
When therefore you have gone around the bone by perforating, and it has been separated from the dura mater, if it was continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you shave, and smooth the end of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. However, it is necessary that it be thinner and more delicate than other incisors.
But if something remains of small bones and splinters: then take them away easily, with that which is with you from the instruments. Then treat the wound afterwards with lint and ointments which we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen mentioned, and praised it with great praise: and this is his account. It is necessary for you first, to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more evident: until when you have uncovered that part, and have placed under it the end of this lenticular incisor, of which this is the form.
And the lenticular part, gently does not cut anything; and its sharp part on its two sides going lengthwise, as you see: so that the lenticular part is resting against the soft tissues, its sharp face striking against the bone.
Then strike the incisor from one side with a small hammer, until you cut through the whole bone easily, as if it were being rolled: and be assured from the membrane, that no accident happens in it which you fear at all.
And if the physician were not the most foolish of men, and more ignorant of it, nor were sleeping, then if something remained attached to the bone from the membrane in some places, he would cut it from it with the end of its lenticular incisor, and free it from it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and fine splinters remain in it: then it is necessary that you shave that roughness, and remove those splinters with fine scrapers from among which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it is possible for you to use among them in every place the most suitable and straightest of them, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its shape. And it is necessary that you use in your scraping, with which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those scrapers. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you use in your scraping, with which you first scrape the bone, the wider of those scrapers. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease doing that continuously, until you come to using the finest and most delicate of them all.
In each of the remaining small, hair-like fissures and fine fractures, it is necessary that you administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter which is not hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and has considered what we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the exposed membrane remains when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do this as easily as you can so that the intestines are not burdened.
Then apply a wide bandage over it; and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds only the wound; and leave it with this dressing for one day or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then untie it, and afterwards apply medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things: and it is to make a powder from these medicines, which is to be sprinkled on the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor let there be filth of ointment, nor dirt.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean: nor let there be filth of ointment, nor dirt. And do not allow pus to gather in it at all: because when pus gathers on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and great harm happens to the patient afterwards: and already it happens in the sheath of the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider therefore then, if the accidents which we have mentioned happen to the patient, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not happen except from the medicine which is put upon it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and apply a cloth with them. Then place it on the sheath; afterwards treat it with the kinds of treatment which are suitable, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that there are not broken of the nose except two of its upper parts together, or one of them; because of this, that they are two bones: because its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and there does not happen to it except abrasion and crookedness and flatness.
If one of the two parts of it is broken, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the opening of the nose, and you should straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural form: and let this be done skillfully, with ease. And beware lest there should happen through your operation upon the sick man pain.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that it be straightened with the end of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in two parts, then do the like of that: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the rest of a warm abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a pledget of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the pledget a thickness of such quantity, which fills the hole of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that pledgets should be infused in butter, and changed every day. That indeed does not seem to me, nay it is necessary, that the pledget be infused in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the pledget until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of a pledget, after a soft cloth is wrapped around it: and it happens that it holds the fracture of the nose more strongly, and so that the sick person is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, make pledgets.
But if a hot abscess should happen to the nose again while you are operating, plaster the nose with cerate, or with cotton infused in rose oil, or a little diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not happen, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour, and frankincense powder made with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
But if the bones of the nose are fractured by a small fracture, or are comminuted, then it is necessary that you make an incision over them, and extract them with instruments which are suitable for them. Then sew the incision, and treat it with that which causes granulation and consolidation from suitable plasters or ointments.
But if a wound happens between the nostrils, then it is necessary that you treat it with linen cloths; and administer a lead cannula until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then see if its fracture is only external, and is not broken in two, and is bent inwards; then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if its fracture is on the right side, you should insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the sick person; similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and push with it the hump of the fracture inwards with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone decently, and with it equalize it.
But if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from two parts, according to straightness, until its equalization is possible.
But if a shaking or disruption has already happened in the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with a thread of gold, or silver, or bristle. Then place cerate over the broken jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth a large, decently made splint; or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above, according to how its bandage is prepared and its application is suitable, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the patient to use tranquility and quiet; and give him as food soft drinks.
But if you estimate that something of the form is already altered in some way, then hasten and unbind it on the third day. Then rectify what has been altered of it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the cerate; and place soft wool over the plaster.
Therefore, while that plaster remains attached to it, and the arrangement of the bone is not altered, leave it, lest you unbind it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For often this fracture is bound in three weeks.
But if in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have often said in its sedation, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the splinter: then be skillful in this, so that you remove those splinters with what is suitable to you in their removal from the instruments.
But if the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a fleam according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those splinters, and nothing of them remains, sew then the opening of the wound, if it is wide; and if not, place upon it of the plasters which are suitable for it, and they will cause it to grow flesh, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle, when it is broken.
Indeed, the clavicle is mostly broken on the anterior side from the side of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two, besides which splinters occur in it: and it is easier to restore it.
Or so that splinters occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or so that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation in it when the fracture is without a wound, is that you cause two assistants to be present, so that one of them holds the aid which follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers, until its form is made, according to what is necessary: and let there be neither prominence nor concavity in it.
But if you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the armpit of the patient a sphere of cloth or wool: and let its size be according to the quantity of your necessity: and extend and elevate the clavicle, and compress the sphere with your hand, until you equalize the fracture, according to what is necessary.
But if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards, because it has reached the depth, then it is necessary that the patient be laid supine on his neck: and you place under his shoulder a pillow moderate in size, and let an assistant press his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the clavicle which is deep is elevated upwards: and then rectify the fracture, and equalize it with your fingers.
If however you feel something displaced from the clavicle, and it moves, then it is necessary that you make an incision upon it, and extract that displaced thing easily.
But if it is retained in the bone, then make an incision upon it with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the clavicle an instrument which protects the trachea: and this instrument is of wood or of iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need of it, from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length however, also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you cut when you extracted the splinter of bone, is wide, and you are secure from a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths, and powders according to the size of the wound's fissure.
But if a hot abscess should occur, then dip a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it on top.
A constriction however of the bone when it is without a wound, and without a cut, is that you place on the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place soft lint over it. Then place a ball under its tickling, if you need that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be of one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a small splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the alhimemati which falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it is rolled, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy little finger, and under its injured little finger: and bring it over every part, and as you see that the bandage holds the fracture with a decent bandage: and that is, which is not hidden from you, and the whole, so that the splint is not removed from above the broken bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you look at the patient every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, and you see the splint being removed: then straighten it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back, and place under his little finger at his sleep a small cushion at night, so that with it his aid may be raised from his side. Therefore the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not untie the bandage, unless an accident happens in the place from itching, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its face [i.e., the surface of the wound] to it. And add to the constriction, and leave it until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the clavicle is tightened and strengthened mostly within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some men in a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad part; and only its margins are fractured from it.
Therefore when a place is fractured from it, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not known except by touch: then according to what the figure of the fracture is, attempt its preparation. And reduce it to its natural form in every way possible to your skill.
Then place over the area mill dust with egg white, and soft lint, and place a small pillow of doubled cloth over it. Then place over it a broad splint of thin board according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little larger.
But if under the splint there is a concavity in any place of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with soft lint until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the splint, until it is not removed from its place.
And examine the bandage every day. Whenever, therefore, the bandage is loosened, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it is removed from its place; and let the patient lie on his sound side.
And indeed the shoulder blade is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then unbind the bandage: and you shall be in safety. For it is of bones whose consolidation is not feared, nor their separation.
But if a fragment of bone protrudes and punctures beneath the skin: then incise over it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the settling of a hot abscess, if anything should result from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are very often fractured and crushed by accidents.
When its middle, which inclines downwards, is fractured, and intense pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing occur: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity in the broken bone is apparent and not hidden from perception, its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back; and place a cushion between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and bring together the ribs on both sides or flanks with your hands; and carefully make it even, in every way that is possible and fitting, until the shape of the bone is restored as it should be.
Then place a plaster and lint over it, and place on top a splint of thin willow or alder wood, or similar light woods, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and go with the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a suitable constriction.
Then examine the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if necessity requires its loosening because of itching, or ulceration that occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and correct what has occurred according to the method of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see that it is needed, and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts which follow the back, and at their extremities from the front. And attrition does not happen to them except because they are cartilaginous. And its recognition is not hidden from the sense upon examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with the fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equalized, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then a violent pain and a stabbing pain happens to the patient, like the stabbing pain that happens to someone who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty of breathing, coughing, and vomiting much blood happen to him: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many things for it: and among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods that produce inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflamed and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we avoid causing a rapid hot abscess if it does not already exist. But if it already exists, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a cupping glass be placed on the area, then let it be strongly sucked, and this is more reasonable. However, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the area, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the area be covered with wool which has already been submerged in hot oil: and let small cushions be placed between the ribs, until it is full: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it around the circumference.
Then let the patient be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
But if a severe condition constricts the patient, which he cannot tolerate, and the bone pierces the covering with a lacerating puncture, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary that we incise over the area, and uncover the fractured rib. Then let us place upon it the instrument which preserves the sifac, the mention of which preceded: and let us incise the bone easily, and let us cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture: and let us treat it with plasters, until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess should occur meanwhile, then hasten, and infuse lint in rose oil, and place it on the area: and let the patient be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile also let him be laid on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs to the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly also to the vertebrae of the back. Therefore when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he will not be healed, and in most instances it is mortal.
But if he moves them, and feels constriction with the fingers and the pricking: then know that the bone of the neck is safe, and that the sick man will be healed with treatment.
But if something similar to that happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and that which happened in the hands happens in them: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion go out without will; and when he lies on his belly, excretion goes out without will; and when he lies on his back, and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in his healing.
If however nothing of that happens to him, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of that which happens from that is, that you strive for the sedation of the warm abscess: thus, that you place on the rubbed vertebra rose oil alone; or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that on it several times a day, until when the warm abscess is sedated: then place on the place one of the strengthening, drying plasters, and bind it on tightly with a bandage: and instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility. Nor let him sleep on the side where he finds pain, until it is healed.
But if it happens at the rubbing of the bone, saturated, or something which is already separated from it: then it is necessary that you cut above the skin, and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some incarnative, of ointments, until it heals.
But if the last bone of the alhausos is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and set the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and by a second setting.
Then place it on and remove it: and treat the wound with the treatment which preceded, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the hip.
Chapter XI.
Rarely are the bones of the hip broken. But if they are broken, then their fracture is nothing but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise, and are deflected inwards: and there happens to the sick man pain in the place, and a puncture: and the leg which is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is, that you extend your hand over it, until you stand over the fracture in whatever way is its form.
But if the fracture is only at its extremities, then set that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from setting, until it is assimilated to its natural form.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or now deviates inwards: then make the sick man lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. Therefore, when you have set it, place a plaster over it; then place over it a splint of wood, or of leather; and bind it tightly, over which you do not fear a change of the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those with that which fills it, until the binding receives it according to equality: and instruct the sick man to sleep on his back, or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess happens to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess is seated; and place over it that which seats it, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
But if it happens in a splintered bone, or is crushed, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the Ulna.
The ulna is that which is between the elbow and the head of the scapula.
Therefore, if it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two ends.
Then let it be suspended from a high place: and let the patient sit on a seat. Then let him throw his fractured arm over the wood, until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the wood is attached.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that the assistant may extend it downwards. Then let the physician equalize the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns, as it ought.
And another method is, that the patient be laid supine upon his neck: and let his hand be suspended from his neck with a ligature. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with his hands; and that the other hold that which is below it, and that each of them extend to his own part.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a ligature, and above it with another ligature: and let each of the two assistants extend to his two parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligature be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then set the fracture easily without effort, until when the fracture is set as it ought, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave off the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess is quiet: then bind it: and the manner of binding it is, to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let him place the arm on a support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm to the support, so that the arm remains in the place of the stars: if nothing prevents you from it, nor is anything changed in you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something will be changed in you from the broken bone, then use splints: and it is, that you place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your bandage be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is lengthened, the less tight should the bandage be.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, at the hour in which you set the limb, then do so: and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave off the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then examine the bandage on all three days, lest there occur in the place itching, or swelling, or the food be prevented from reaching the limb, because of the excessive tightness of the bandage. Therefore rectify all that, according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage except after three days, or more.
And let the sleep of the patient be on his back, and his hands upon his stomach; and place under the injured part a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day, lest the shape of the broken limb be loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore rectify all that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have set forth, so that the food be light at first, until the mouth begins to close; then it is necessary that you increase his food.
For it is the custom of a broken arm and leg that it be bound for forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be unbound, and a bath be administered, and it be treated with ointments which rectify it.
But if the fracture be foul, and comminuted, then do not unbind the bandage and splints until fifty days, and even up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearms.
Of which one is small: and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm is broken, or the smaller; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the small upper forearm is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the small upper forearm, then it is suitable for the physician, in its restoration, to put a small extension easily, until he sets it.
But if it is the large forearm that is broken: then it is necessary that he put a much stronger tightening.
And it is necessary that he put the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extension stretched out on a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lowest joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest; and let the patient sit squarely, and let a cushion be in front of him, or directly in line with him, at a height such that the patient does not incur labor.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage. And let another assistant extend from above similarly.
Then let the physician set the bone until he restores it to the best possible shape.
But if there are splinters in the bone fracture, then try to restore each fragment to its place by your skill.
If, however, a separated splinter appears and penetrates the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it, then incise over it and remove it in the manner we described previously.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already devoted a separate chapter to that, so take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your reduction, then smear a cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, of medium consistency, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from ground powder with egg white. Then apply splints, and let the splint placed directly over the fracture be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones at once.
Then apply your bandage over the fracture site, firmly and tightly, somewhat more tightly above or below the fracture; or apply a bandage slightly looser, as was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, and not hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of linen, of medium thickness, as we have described.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. But if something occurs that requires correction, such as itching affecting the limb, then you must bathe the limb with warm water until the itching subsides, and leave the limb unbound until it rests; then reapply the bandage.
But if the bandage is now softened, and the bone is now set, and similar things: then correct it all with your skill, and examine it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness of the bandage, then you must soften it a little, and leave it off for a few days, until nourishment flows to it; then bind it.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary to unbind it except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended from his neck. And his neck should be straight, and it should be kept still by its own strength. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture rarely happens to the back of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers. And it does not happen to it, except by much rubbing.
When therefore a fracture or rubbing happens to the palm, then it is necessary that the sick person sit squarely; and in front of him should be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician align them until when they are joined with a good joining.
Then it is necessary that he apply a plaster and lint, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the place; and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is towards the lower parts around the sole of the foot, then make something similar to a sphere out of cloth; and instruct the patient to bind it over the broken sole of his foot; then let it be bound with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the sole; and bind it, as it is necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary that you place one splint on top, and another splint below on the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage, just as the hand is rolled; and extend it between the fingers with a ligature.
But if a fracture should happen to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, let it be aligned as is necessary. Then let it be bound with the sole; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it, so that the fracture may be raised and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, or little, and ring fingers, then let it be set, and bound with the healthy finger which follows it; or let them all be bound together continuously; and this is better. Or place a small splint on it, as we said in the case of the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition of a warm abscess, and oppose it with what is necessary, when something happens from it, according to what is repeated in its account.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and this is evident to the senses; because it is turned towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligature is tightened above the fracture, and another ligature below the fracture. And let the patient be laid on his face.
